1. THE SECTOR
A. SECTORAL HISTORY AND CONDITIONS
Country Background
I. I   After several decades of war and disunity, Viet Nam was unified under socialism in
1975. In 1989, Government launched a successful program of adjustment and reform
("Doi Moi"), with the aim of achieving macroeconomic structural reform, liberalization
and stabilization   Structural reforms included the decollectivization of agriculture,
liberalization of prices and foreign trade, and contraction of state enterprises and subsidies.
Under this program, real annual GDP growth has averaged 7 percent, inflation rates and
the fiscal deficit have been substantially reduced, and exports have grown by more than 30
percent per annum to become a leading sector in the economy. Prospects for the coming
decade are bright.
1.2   Viet Nam also continues to benefit from many positive legacies of the decades of
socialist rule. The distribution of consumption is relatively equal by comparison with
other countries, and an impressive network of basic social services has been put in place
over the past three decades. As a result, 88 percent of all adults are literate, including 84
percent of adult women, while 78 percent of children aged 6-10 years are enrolled in
primary school. Life expectancy is currently estimated at around 67 years and infant
mortality at around 40 per 1,000 live births. More than 94 percent of children survive
their first five years of life.
1.3   However, Viet Nam still remains one of the poorest countries in the world, with a
per capita income estimated at under US$200. About one quarter of the population is too
poor to afford an adequate daily basic food intake, and more than half of all young
children are stunted. Between 40 and 50 percent of the population are below a poverty
line that includes expenses for both food and non-food basic needs.  Though the
adjustment and reform program provided much more broadly-based benefits than in other
transition economies because of the dominant role of family-based agriculture, and though
its social costs have been relatively mild, the poorest groups are still disadvantaged and
likely to lag behind without continuing special attention. A combination of broad-based
economic growth with continued access for the poor to basic social services will be
essential for long-term national economic development and poverty reduction.
1.4   Reduction of population growth will make a vital contribution to both economic
growth and poverty alleviation. Viet Nam's population now stands at around 73 million, a
35 percent increase over the 54 million enumerated in the census of 1979 shortly after
reunification. Pressure on the country's agricultural and forest land is acute. Despite
recent falls in fertility, the population is still growing at over 2 percent per annum, due to a



- 2 -
combination of extremely low mortality with the large cohorts in the young reproductive
ages resulting from past high fertility.  If this rate of growth were maintained, the
population would double within the next 33 years. Dependency ratios are also still high,
resulting in heavy costs in education and child health care relative to productive capacity.
Fertility urgently needs to be reduced to replacement level and maintained there if Viet
Nam's productive potential is to be maximized.
1.5    Viet Nam's development strategy is directed at five specific objectives: (1) to
maintain macroeconomic stability; (2) to make a steady transition to an open market
economy; (3) to improve basic infrastructure; (4) to alleviate poverty and reduce regional
inequalities; (5) to preserve the nation's natural resource base so that economic
achievements can be sustained over the long term. Development of the population and
health sectors is closely linked to the objectives of poverty alleviation, reduction of
regional inequalities and natural resource sustainability.
Sector History and Status
1.6    Health Status. Viet Nam has achieved an extraordinarily low level of mortality
for its income and stage of development. Nevertheless, its main health problems remain
those common to most developing countries.  Infectious and parasitic diseases,
particularly TB, malaria and acute respiratory infections (ARIs), are still a substantial
threat to the population at large.  Among children, the communicable diseases of
childhood have declined rapidly in incidence and severity following the success of the
Expanded Program of Immunization (EPI), but ARIs and diarrheal diseases are still major
killers of children and constitute their most important health problems. Nearly 60 percent
of infant deaths are due to these two groups of diseases.  Severe and acute child
malnutrition is now very uncommon, but chronic undernutrition manifested as stunting is
still the fate of the majority. Among infants, prematurity, low birthweight and problems in
delivery are also major causes of death and ill-health. For women, pregnancy and delivery
are still important health risks, with estimates of the maternal mortality ratio (MMR) in
1990 ranging from 110 to over 200 per 100,000 live births. Other major health problems
for women include a very high prevalence of anaemia, at two thirds of all pregnant
women, and widespread reproductive tract infections (RTIs). According to the 1993
World Development Report (WDR) "Investing in Health", the vast bulk of all these
conditions can be successfully prevented or treated in a highly cost-effective manner
within a primary health care (PHC) system, backed by appropriate first-level referral
facilities and selected additional disease control interventions.
1.7   Primary Health Services. A life expectancy of 67 years could not have been
achieved in a country as poor and as war-affected as Viet Nam without extremely effective
basic health services. Viet Nam was in fact one of the pioneers of primary health care (see
Annex I for a detailed description of the system), and has achieved a vast, dense and staff-
intensive network of health facilities far exceeding those of its much richer neighbors. The



- 3 -
ratio of the lowest-level health facility (the commune health centre, or CHC) per million
population is around 170 in Viet Nam, compared with 32 in Indonesia, 63 in China and
141 in Thailand; each CHC is staffed with about four health workers including an assistant
doctor, a secondary midwife, a nurse and a pharmacist. Average traveling time to obtain a
modern contraceptive method (mostly supplied through public facilities) is reported to be
only 20 minutes. Only three percent of rural households live in communes without a
CHC, and a Government-financed program of CHC construction plans to fill these gaps
within the next few years. Similarly, Viet Nam's hospital bed ratio of one per 389 persons
compares favorably with one per 465 in China, 665 in Thailand and 1,743 in Indonesia.
1.8    Viet Nam is also performing relatively well in terms of many basic primary health
care programs, given the lack of adequate financing which continues to be a major
constraint to full coverage and effectiveness.  According to the 1994 Intercensal
Demographic Survey (ICDS), seventy five percent of children have received at least one
immunization. Over 40 percent of pregnant women are receiving full tetanus toxoid
vaccination. The Control of Diarrheal Diseases (CDD) program, which focuses on use of
oral rehydration therapy, is being expanded, it now covers over 80 percent of all
communes and more than half of CHC staff are fully trained. Most mothers are reported
to maintain or increase normal levels of fluids for children with diarrhea and to continue
breastfeeding them, although use of packaged oral rehydration salts or home solutions
itself is still low. The ARI control program is now estimated to cover 40 percent of all
children across all provinces, though less than a quarter of children yet receive the correct
antibiotic treatment. According to the 1994 ICDS, 55 percent of mothers receive some
ante-natal care from a medical or paramedical professional, and nearly 60 percent deliver
in health facilities. Forty four percent of married women are using a modern family
planning method.
1.9   Most of this primary health care was and is delivered at the CHCs and District
Health Centres (DHCs), which in many ways form a partnership through which DHCs
supplement and support CHCs through supervision, referral and mobile teams which tour
CHCs to deliver services beyond the capability of CHC staff For example, the 1994
ICDS reported that 37 percent of all mothers delivered at CHCs, and another 9 percent at
DHCs, together making up more than 80 percent of all institutional deliveries. Likewise,
45 percent of women using modern contraception obtained it at CHCs in 1988 and 44
percent in 1994, according respectively to the 1988 Viet Nam Demographic and Health
Survey and the 1994 ICDS.
I.10  However, the disappearance in recent years of the agricultural cooperative system
which largely financed and supported CHC staff (see para. 1.13), combined with pressures
on public expenditure since the late 1980s, has resulted in a marked deterioration of the
public health service at all levels. This is manifest in dilapidated buildings, acute shortages
of furniture, equipment and supplies, and low morale and productivity of health workers
due to declining real salaries and poor working conditions. In some facilities, health



- 4 -
workers have not only been sleeping on the premises during their duty but staying there
on a longer-term basis. While there are still no shortages of health staff on paper, except
for secondary midwives, many health workers are now said to be working only a few
hours per day in health facilities, in order to supplement their salaries through other
income-earning activities. At the same time, the cost of using public health care has risen
through the introduction in 1989 of official user fees for all facilities except CHCs, and
private health practitioners and pharmacies have been licensed.
1.11   As a result of all these factors, utilization rates for public health facilities have
fallen dramatically for clinical and in-patient services. Numbers of annual out-patient
consultations halved from 2.1 per capita in 1987 to 0.9 in 1993, and numbers of annual
inpatient admissions fell from 105 per 1,000 population to 68 in 1990. Many CHCs and
DHCs are said to be catering to less than 10 outpatients seeking minor curative care per
day, and performing only a few deliveries per month. According to recent data from the
1992-93 Viet Nam Living Standards Survey (VLSS), one third of the population currently
go to any health practitioner when sick, with the other two-thirds resorting to self-
medication. Among the poorest 20 percent of the population, 70 percent self-medicate.
The degree of substitution between public and private providers over time is not known,
but of those who do see a health practitioner, more than half currently see a private
provider; poorer patients are more likely to see a paramedic and richer patients a doctor.
Among public facilities, the poorest are more likely to visit CHCs and richer groups a
hospital. Thus the poor gravitate towards the lowest levels of service in both private and
public sectors for curative care, no doubt because they entail the least immediate direct
expense.
1.12   The overall picture is thus of a public health care sector which has reached a
crossroads. Basic primary health care interventions of a preventive or palliative nature are
being delivered, but coverage is still well below satisfactory levels and the quality of
services is uncertain. If facilities and staffing continue to deteriorate, and resources are
not forthcoming for upgrading and maintaining a well-supplied and well-functioning
system, it is likely that even these basic PHC services will cease to perform adequately.
Because of resource constraints and the ready availability of pharmacies, the public system
is already little used by any section of the population for basic curative care.
1.13   Financing of Health Services. Data on health expenditure for Viet Nam are
weak and need further review  and analysis.  However, on the basis of available
information, it appears that state financing for the public health care system is split
between central and local government budgets. The centre picks up the costs for central
and branch hospitals, preventive health care (including the national malaria, TB and ARI
control programs, EPI and the national MCH program), medical training and medical
research, amounting all together to about one quarter of the state budget for health. The
other three-quarters of the budget is spent by local (provincial and district) governments,
which finance the costs of their respective hospitals with district budgets also picking up



some costs for CHCs. The salaries of CHC staff and maintenance of CHC facilities were in
the past supported almost entirely outside the state health budget by the commune
agricultural cooperatives, through contributions in cash, kind or labor. However the
collapse of the cooperative system has reduced commune-level financing to derisory
levels. A decision was therefore made in 1994 to phase in financing of CHC staff salaries
by the regular state health budget over the next three years, with two-thirds of communes
covered so far.
1.14  In 1993, overall state budget expenditures on health amounted to 1,442 billion
dong (about US$144 million), or some 20,000 dong (US$2) per capita; this was about half
the budget for education. About 90 percent of this amount was spent on curative care in
hospitals and only about 2 percent on CHCs. According to the 1992-3 VLSS, another
7,500 billion (US$750 million) dong were spent directly on health care by households, or
some 104,000 dongs (US$10) per capita Almost all of this private expenditure went for
purchase of drugs. Thus total per capita annual health spending in Viet Nam would be
around US$12, most of it used for medicines.
1.15  By comparison, the 1993 WDR estimates that a minimal package of essential
clinical health services would cost about US$8 per capita in a low-income country, all
calculated to be highly cost-effective and all to be delivered at district and lower levels of
the health system.  A further basic package of "public health" programs (including
immunization and family planning), which benefit the entire community and are also highly
cost-effective, would cost another US$4. The two packages together would make up
US$12 per capita, or roughly what Viet Nam already spends. It would seem therefore,
that total health expenditure in Viet Nam could be adequate for basic health needs,
provided the allocation of spending were appropriate. Since health budgets and data on
private expenditure in Viet Nam are insufficiently detailed to separate out spending by the
-R basic needs categories, it is impossible to judge just how appropriate current
patterns of expenditure actually are. However, it seems extremely likely that too little is
being spent at the lower levels of public and private health systems and on basic cost-
effective clinical and "public health" services.
1.16  Development of Family Planning Policies and Programs.  Concern over
population growth has a long history in Viet Nam. Rapid population growth in the Red
River Delta, an area of recurring past food shortages, induced the government in northern
Viet Nam to promulgate an official population policy as early as 1963. The goals of this
policy were set as a reduction of the current population growth rate of 3.5 percent to 2
percent and achievement of an average family size of 2 to 3 children with 5-6 years spacing
between births. To support the policy, use of condoms and the MJD was promoted and
abortion services began to be made available at health facilities. No equivalent policies
were established in southern Viet Nam, where the pronatalist attitudes of the French
colonial regime and the Catholic church had long been dominant, but family planning
services were available in urban areas. However the subsequent decade of war severely



limited resources for family planning throughout the country, though USAID did begin
funding for population and FP programs in the South in the early 1970s.
1.17  After reunification, Government took a succession of steps to standardize and
increase the availability and uptake of family planning throughout Viet Nam, spurred on by
alarm over the rapid growth of population revealed by the 1979 census. The Family Law
of 1977 attempted to promote the protection of women's rights, and the Ministry of
Health (MOH) worked to improve availability of FP and abortion services through public
sector facilities with help from the Institute for the Protection of Mothers and Newborn
(IPMN). A cadre of volunteer outreach workers, called "motivators", was also established
at the hamlet level by various mass organizations, such as the Women's Union, the
Peasants' Union and the Farmers' Union; their task was to promote the adoption of family
planning among hamlet families.
1.18  In 1984, the National Committee for Population and Family Planning (NCPFP)
was set up to implement the national population policy and to direct the national family
planning program. A large part of NCPFP's staff was drawn from MOH, and a major
principle of its mandate was that family planning services would continue to be delivered
entirely through the health system. The NCPFP was to contract out the actual delivery of
FP and abortion services to MOH, but would coordinate all population and family
planning activities in the country and would itself implement a nationwide program to
promote family planning through social mobilization efforts. To this end, a network of
subsidiary committees was established at provincial (PCPFPs), district (DCPFPs) and
commune (CPFPBs) level, all with participation of the corresponding level of People's
Committees, mass organizations and health authorities.
1.19  In 1988, Viet Nam officially introduced a national "one-child or two-child" policy,
formalizing a system of childbearing guidelines, free provision of contraceptives and
abortion, and economic incentives and disincentives. Following the licensing of a private
health sector, FP and abortion services also became openly available through the private
sector, though public facilities are still by far the most important providers. In 1993, the
NCPFP set up its own nationwide cadre of hamlet-level outreach workers called
"collaborators", who receive a small allowance plus performance-based incentives for the
promotion of family planning, including distribution of condoms and oral contraceptives.
Annex 2 provides a detailed description of the entire current system of family planning
activities.
1.20  Donor Activities. Several donors have been active in the population and family
planning fields in Viet Nam. USAID provided some support in the south during the early
1970s, but this ceased in 1975 and has not been resumed to date. The major donor since
reunification has been UNFPA, who are now nearing the end of their fourth year country
assistance cycle and have been contributing about US$4-5 million per year to the FP
program. They have provided assistance for a full range of population and family planning



- 7 -
activities, comprising demographic and FP data collection and research (including the
1989 census, the 1988 Viet Nam Demographic and Health Survey, and the 1994
Intercensal Demographic Survey), FP/MCH services, contraceptive supplies and
equipment, IEC (including population education), training and MIS. The fifth country
cycle is currently under preparation. Other donors in the field include UNICEF, GTZ,
Australia and various NGOs, who have mainly supported small projects in selected
provinces.
1.21  Family Planning Status.  These family planning efforts were remarkably
successful in their early stages. The 1988 Viet Nam Demographic and Health Survey
(VNDHS) reported a national contraceptive prevalence rate (CPR) of 53 percent of
currently married women of reproductive age, with 95 percent of married women knowing
of at least one modem family planning method. This total CPR was made up of a CPR for
modem methods of 38 percent and a CPR for "non-modern", "traditional" or "natural"
methods (mainly withdrawal and rhythm) of 15 percent. The total fertility rate (TFR) was
estimated from the same survey and from the 1989 census at just under 4 births per
woman for the previous year, down from over 6 in the late 1950s and around 5 in 1979.
Moreover, norms of fertility control and small families appeared to be taking hold;
according to the VNDHS, the average preferred number of children for young married
women (those married less than 5 years) was 2.6 and only 35 percent of all women wanted
any more children.
1.22  This success was achieved using a remarkably limited and inexpensive approach to
FP service delivery. Of current acceptors in the 1988 VNDHS, 62 percent were using the
IUD, amounting to one-third of all currently married women in Viet Nam. Another 29
percent of contraceptors were using "traditional" or "natural" methods, mainly consisting
of withdrawal and rhythm. The third most important method was sterilization (mostly
mini-laps, rarely post-partum) at 6 percent of contraceptors. Only two percent were using
condoms and less than one percent the pill. Thus the vast majority of FP users were
relying on methods that either entailed no cost at all or required only very infrequent, or
once-off, minor surgical procedures with minimal supplies and re-usable equipment.
Although traditional methods usually have high user failure rates, experience even in
developed countries such as the USA shows that procedure-based methods are among the
most cost-effective of contraceptive choices.
1.23  Moreover, most modern FP services, and hence most of the procedures, have been
and are provided through lower-level public health facilities. In 1988, almost all family
planning users had obtained modem methods from the public sector; 45 percent had
received services at CHCs, 37 percent at district hospitals or intercommunal polyclinics,
and 12 percent at provincial hospitals. Even by 1993 and 1994, only about 10-11 percent
of all modem-method FP users had obtained a method privately according to the VLSS
and ICDS, and the VLSS reported that they were largely users of pills and condoms.
Given Viet Nam's generously-staffed and extremely dense network of public health



- 8 -
facilities, and their low current case-loads at the lower levels, the provision of minor
outpatient procedures such as IUD insertions, mini-laps and vasectomies (nowadays
mainly done with the minimal no-scalpel technique) would entail minimal incremental costs
per family planning user.
1.24  Unfortunately this method mix, however cost-efficient, is far from optimal from
the standpoint of reproductive and women's health. Heavy use of IUDs is problematic in
the circumstances of 'Viet Nam for several reasons. The presence of the device may
promote or exacerbate existing RTIs and the insertion procedure itself carries an
additional risk of introducing infection unless done with the utmost skill and vigilance.
These general risks become a cause for serious concem where the incidence of RTIs is
already high, as in Viet Nam. All but the recently developed progestin-bearing IUDs often
induce heavy menstrual bleeding, which is undesirable where high percentages of women
are already anaemic, as in Viet Nam. In addition, the IUD does have a significant, though
small, failure rate; and armong those pregnancies that occur with the device still in place, a
high proportion will spontaneously abort at any stage of gestation. This additional burden
on women's health is particularly undesirable where the abortion rate is already high, as in
Viet Nam.
1.25   Heavy use of withdrawal and rhythm is also problematic. Though these methods
appear to carry little or no health risks in themselves, their failure rate is high among all
but the most committed and well-supervised users. This in turn is likely to result in high
rates of abortion. The usefulness of sterilization is limited. It is an essential part of any FP
program for older couples who decide that they have completed their families, but it is
inevitably restricted to this category of user and cannot be used for the spacing of births so
important to matemal and child health. The major gap in the program method mix at
present is thus the lack of effective, non-invasive, easily reversible methods that can be
used for birth spacing purposes and by younger couples who have not completed their
families. Pills and injectables are the most important methods in this category, followed by
the condom, which also protects against RTIs, and Norplant.
1.26  Over the years, Viet Nam had thus developed a cost-efficient and initially effective
family planning prograrn, which however suffered from some important deficiencies.
Perhaps because of these, the program's progress appears to have slowed in recent years.
The 1993 Multiround Survey (MIS) reported only an insignificant further rise in the total
CPR to 54 percent, with a small rise in the modem methods CPR to 41 percent and a
small decline in the non-modern methods CPR to 13 percent. Corresponding values for the
much smaller-sample 1992-93 VLSS were a somewhat higher total CPR of 57 percent,
which however included much multiple use of traditional methods with modern methods.
The 1994 Intercensal Demographic Survey (ICDS) reported a sharp rise in the total CPR
to 65 percent, but the C]PR for modern methods increased only marginally to 44 percent,
while the CPR for non-rnodern methods jumped to 21 percent. Thus overall, the use of
modern methods of contraception appears to have risen by only six points between 1988



-9-
and 1994, from 38 percent to 44 percent, with an equivalent increase occurring in the use
of non-modern methods from 15 percent to 21 percent.
1.27  The pattern oif FP method mix did not evolve significantly in any desirable
direction during the six years between 1998 and 1994. The 1992-93 VLSS showed much
the same method mix as in 1988, except for slightly increased pill and condom use and
some multiple use of traditional methods in combination with them. The 1993 MRS also
reported a broadly similar mix, though the range of methods had widened. Use of the IUD
had risen a little to 65 percent of contraceptors; the share of sterilization had remained
constant at 6 percent; use of pills and condoms had risen to around 3 percent each;
another fraction of a percent were using injectables, Norplant or diaphragms; and the share
of traditional methods had fallen a little to 21 percent.
1.28  The 1994 ICDS did report some changes, but these cannot be viewed as positive
overall. Similar percentages of contraceptors as in 1993 were using sterilization (6
percent) and the pill i(3 percent) and injectables and diaphragms/jelly (a fraction of a
percent in total); condoms had doubled their share to 6 percent; the percent using IUD had
dropped to 51 percent, but this was due largely to the much increased percentage relying
on rhythm and withdrawal (32 percent combined). The proportion of married women
using the IUD barely changed across all surveys from 1988 to 1994, remaining always
between 33 and 35 percent. Thus family planning in Vietnam remains heavily focused on
quick and simple minor surgical procedures with important health drawbacks, and a
substantial and growing fraction of users continue to resort to no-cost but ineffective
traditional practices.
1.29  During these same six years from 1988 to 1994, fertility fell more than might have
been expected from the above trends in contraceptive use and mix, but still less than
would have been desirable. The TFR reported for the previous year declined from just
under 4 in the 1988 VNDHS to 3.5. according to the 1993 MRS and to 3.3 according to
the 1992-3 VLSS. The 1994 ICDS reported an average TFR for the years 1989-1993 of
3.3 and a value of 3.1 for 1993 itself It is suspected that at least part of this decline was
due to the use of pregnancy termination services, since overall the method mix has
changed to favor the less effective non-modern methods whose failures appear to provide
an important fraction of termination requests (see para. 1.32). However even a TFR of 3.1
is still much above replacement level, which at Viet Nam's very low level of mortality
would be a TFR of under 2.2 births per woman.
1.30  Pregnancy termination plays a considerable role in the regulation of fertility in Viet
Nam. The term "pregnancy termination" comprises two separately defined procedures in
the country. More than half of all termination procedures performed in the country today
are recorded as "menstrual regulation" (MR); these safer and less traumatic procedures are
performed within a couple of weeks of a missed period, often without a pregnancy test,
and require only a special syringe and no dilatation of the cervix. Only later procedures,



- 10 -
using standard abortion techniques, are recorded as "abortions" in Viet Nam. Both types
of procedure are legal and available from public health facilities, as well as from the
private sector. Neither type is considered as a family planning method, but only as a back-
up in case of contraceptive failure.
1.31   Taking both types of pregnancy termination together and classifying both as
abortions, over a million abortions are now performed annually in Viet Nam according to
service statistics. This amounts to about two abortions for every five births. The
corresponding total abortion rate (TAR), an analogous measure to the TFR, has been
estimated from these figures at 2.5 abortions per woman over her reproductive lifetime.
This would be the third highest level of abortion recorded in the world, surpassed only by
Romania and the former Soviet Union where contraceptives have been notoriously
difficult to obtain. Such a high level is unexpected in a country which (unlike Romania and
the former Soviet Union) has been actively promoting and providing family planning for
many years, and has achieved a total CPR of 65 percent.
1.32  Further data on abortion come from the 1988 VDHS and the 1994 ICDS, in which
women were asked if they had ever had an MR or abortion. However these data must be
treated with caution, since severe underreporting is almost always expected in direct
questioning of women on their abortion experience, and seems to have occurred even in
the relatively unthreatening circumstances of Viet Nam. In 1988, 7 percent of ever married
women reported having had a termination, with a roughly fifty-fifty split between the two
procedures. In 1994, this percentage had risen to 13 percent, with these women reporting
an average of 1.25 procedures. Nearly 60 percent claimed to have been using
contraception at the time of conception; of those women reporting a recent termination
(since 1991) following a method failure, nearly 60 percent had been using withdrawal or
rhythm. Given that only 20-30 percent of contracepting women were users of these two
methods, it appears that the failure rate of these methods is an important contributor to the
need for pregnancy termination.
1.33   According to the 1988 VDHS, 74 percent of all terminations in public facilities
took place at district hospitals or polyclinics, another 18 percent at provincial hospitals,
and four percent each at CHCs and central hospitals. Generally speaking, MRs tend to be
performed at the lower levels of the health system while women would be progressively
referred up for terminations at later stages. In many ways, therefore, the patterns of use of
termination procedures match those for the standard FP outpatient procedures of IUD
insertion and sterilization.
1.34  The overall picture therefore is of a family planning program whose performance is
faltering because it is no longer adapted to its clients' needs. The small family norm is
becoming ever more entrenched in Viet Nam, the proportion of all women wanting any
more children declined from 35 percent in 1988 to 28 percent in 1994, while the average
family size preferred by young married women fell from 2.6 in 1988 to 2.3 -- or close to



- 11 -
replacement -- in 1994. The total CPR has risen to the very high level of 65 percent of
married women, indicating that couples are translating these desires for small families into
action. Yet the use of modern methods has stagnated in recent years, while use of
traditional methods is growing fast. Despite the heavy promotion of the IUD in the public
program and the general lack of alternatives in the public sector, it has proved impossible
to raise the level of use above a plateau of one third of married women. Similar though
less intense promotion of sterilization has failed to raise the level of use above a plateau of
6 percent of couples. Where couples have had access through the private sector to the pill
-- and even the not-then-licensed injectables -- use of these methods rises. Levels of
pregnancy termination are extremely high. It is hard to avoid the conclusion that couples
are resorting to traditional methods and (partly as a result of their use) to termination
because they lack a suitable choice of temporary and reversible modern methods. While
such a combination may reduce fertility, it cannot be considered a desirable or sustainable
approach to family planning from the viewpoint of family health and welfare.
1.35  Financing of Family Planning. Funding for population and family planning
activities in Viet Nam is made up of a mix of domestic and donor funding. In the past, the
share of donor financing was as much as 30 to 40 percent, but this has now fallen to
around 20 percent. Much of the contribution of donors has been in kind, such as
contraceptive commodities, equipment and technical assistance; UNFPA alone has
provided US$2-3 million worth of contraceptives per year in recent years. Total donor
support for 1994 is estimated at about US$5.6 million.
1.36  Domestic population/FP funding largely flows through the national budget of the
NCPFP, which in 1994 reached about 228 billion dongs or US$20.7 million. Local
governments at provincial and district level, and commune authorities, are also expected
to contribute funds to their respective PCPFPs, DCPFPs and CPFPBs; in 1994, provincial
budget funding for population/FP amounted to 23 billion dongs, or around one-tenth of
central funding, though funding at lower levels is difficult to track. Altogether, total
government spending on population and family planning may have amounted to somewhat
over 251 billion dongs or nearly US$23 million. Thus annual domestic public expenditure
per capita for the five to six million current family planning users would come to about
US$3.50 - 4.50. Donor support would add in another US$1 or so, making total public
spending about US$4.50 - 5.50. According to the VLSS, private spending might amount
to an additional total US$3 million, or another US 50 cents per user, yielding a grand total
of public plus private annual spending on family planning of US$5-6 per FP user. These
are extremely low levels of spending compared with a typical annual FP program cost of
US$10-20 per user in the developing world, and no doubt reflect the extremely cost-
efficient FP method mix developed in Viet Nam.
1.37  A substantial fraction of domestic population/FP funding is transferred through the
NCPFP and its subsidiary committee network to the health system, to finance the delivery
of FP services. In 1994, a formal system of "responsibility contracts" was established,



- 12 -
whereby the PCPFPs, DCPFPs and CPFPBs enter into annual contracts with health
service authorities at the corresponding level. Funds flow down from NCPFP to each
subsidiary CPFP level and are then released out to health authorities. The contracts specify
targets to be achieved by the service providers and the budget that will be provided to
reach the given targets. Targets are specified in terms of basic numbers, such as numbers
of acceptors for a given method or of different types of procedures to be performed
annually. Corresponding budgets are calculated on the basis of a standard unit cost per
procedure etc., and appear to cover only the incremental costs to the health system of
providing these FP services. Achievement of targets is monitored regularly, often monthly,
and it appears that the quarterly release of funds to health authorities for each quarter is
adjusted according to their actual previous performance.
1.38  Other items funded through the NCPFP and the subsidiary CPFP network include
incentives for adoption of a method (paid to both acceptors and the collaborators who
motivate them), allowances for collaborators, training and management costs of the CPFP
system, equipment and contraceptive commodities, in-service FP training of health
workers, and the costs of conventional IEC activities. Only about 7 percent of these funds
are spent at the central level, with the rest being disbursed through the lower levels of the
system. In addition, just under one quarter of the NCPFP budget goes to administrative
costs of the entire CPFP system plus infrastructure costs.
B. CURRENT ISSUES AND NEEDS IN THE SECTOR
Family Health
1.39  The health sector in Viet Nam today faces many challenges. These are laid out in
the Staff Appraisal Report for the National Health Support Project (Report No. 14971-
VN), which is designed to support Government in meeting and overcoming them. For
family health, however, the major and overriding issue is the need to revitalize the state
primary health care system, defined according to WDR principles as the commune and
district-level network of facilities. It is at these levels that many of the most cost-effective
public health and clinical services are delivered, such as short-course chemotherapy for
tuberculosis, treatment of diarrhea, malaria, and respiratory infections, and immunisations.
These basic functions, which are almost all of a preventive or palliative nature, are not in
general provided in a technically satisfactory and cost-effective manner by the private
sector. According to what survey data exist, Viet Nam's developing private sector, which
is still largely centred on curative care, self-medication and pharmacies, is not so far
playing an important role in this type of basic health care. The private sector does appear
to be substituting for public provision of some curative care, but even this is largely in the
form of self-medication with purchased drugs, and therefore of unknown and dubious
quality. Thus, though the private sector will have an increasingly important role to play in
Viet Nam's health sector, there will in the foreseeable future be no practical alternative to
the public PHC system for the maintenance and further improvement of basic and cost-



- 13 -
effective primary health programs such as maternal care, family planning and
immunization, as well as a safety net of minor curative care for the very poor. There is
therefore an urgent need to restore operating conditions and staff morale at these grass-
roots facilities to normal working order, through upgrading of physical premises, supplies,
staff salaries and staff skills.
1.40   Adequate primary health services are not needed only to maintain Viet Nam's
current low levels of mortality and to improve health among all sections of the community
in a cost-effective manner. They are also essential to the effective delivery of family
planning. As described above, over 80 percent of users of modern methods had obtained
services at either commune or district level in 1988, and this pattern does not appear to
have changed significantly by 1994. In addition, experience worldwide has demonstrated
that in the long run, family planning is most acceptable and most effectively practiced
when FP services are well embedded in a health care system which delivers trustworthy
maternal and child health (MCH) care. Such integration of family planning into family
health care, which was recently strongly and unanimously endorsed at the Cairo
International Conference on Population and Development (ICPD) in 1994, has been a
principle of Viet Nam's family planning program from the beginning. It urgently needs
reinforcement now that the country's formerly successful PHC system has come under
pressure.
1.41   Within the family health care sector, there are also some key issues and needs
regarding particular health conditions and service delivery constraints. Some aspects of
maternal and infant health care require special attention and improvement, notably specific
antenatal interventions such as anaemia prophylaxis and treatment and tetanus toxoid
(TT) vaccination and better facilities for first-level referral of complicated deliveries.
CDD and ARI programs for children have still a long way to go in terms of both
coverage and effectiveness. Child nutrition interventions through health services, including
growth monitoring and the promotion of optimum birth spacing and breastfeeding
practices, are relatively underdeveloped at the primary level. Regarding other aspects of
reproductive health, the high incidence of both RTIs and abortion is acknowledged to be
a serious issue for women's health, though far too little is known about the underlying
causes of either.
1.42   Outstanding service delivery issues relate mainly to quality, outreach and areas
where normal patterns of service delivery are inadequate. Quality problems are due
mainly to the severe decline in financial support for the PHC system, which has resulted in
low staff attendance and morale, and in lack of adequate regular refresher training to keep
skills up to date and maintain interest; the consequent declines in facility utilization have
set up a vicious circle whereby skills are further eroded through lack of practice and staff
morale and involvement in their work sink even lower. Improved inputs and resources are
needed to break this cycle. Another aspect of quality concerns the current organization of
primary health service delivery, which is excessively fragmented and verticalized by



- 14 -
specific intervention, so that mothers must visit several different facilities or providers to
obtain a complete package of maternal or child care, or even a full range of contraceptive
services. The design of primary health care delivery needs to be adjusted to make the
convenience and needs of the client the central principle.
1.43  Health outreach problems also originate from changes in commune organization
and health financing patterns. The old system of village health workers, usually primary
nurses, broke down with the collapse of the cooperative system, but has not been replaced
by any equivalent network because of the pressures on public health expenditure. The lack
of health outreach is all the more felt since the CHC system itself has been deteriorating at
the same time.
1.44  There is also a serious and well-established national deficit of one important
grassroots health worker, namely the secondary midwife who is a key member of the CHC
establishment. Over sixty percent of CHCs are now estimated to be without a secondary
midwife (see Annex 1). The prospects for remedying this deficit in the short and medium
term are dim, and therefore strategies for supplying the lack need to be identified and tried
out.
1.45   All these deficiencies in service delivery have particularly severe consequences in
those areas of Viet Nam where coverage and access to services is inherently difficult
because of remoteness or scattered and sparse patterns of population settlement; these are
mostly mountainous regions where Viet Nam's disadvantaged ethnic minority groups are
concentrated (see paras. 4.18-4.26). New and innovative approaches to service delivery
need to be tried in such areas.
Population and Family Planning
1.46  Viet Nam's family planning program must, for the foreseeable future, continue to
be mainly state-financed and managed, given the embryonic state of development of the
private FP sector and the highly cost-effective broad societal benefits to be derived from
effective fertility regulation across all groups in society. It has enjoyed a very successful
and cost-efficient first phase, using an approach which was based on low-cost minor
surgical procedures delivered at low-level health facilities, and backed by an extensive
campaign of social mobilization to promote small families and to recruit and maintain
uptake of family planning services. However this approach was never optimal from the
health standpoint and seems now to be reaching its natural limits, with growth in
prevalence of modern contraceptive methods stagnating in recent years. Yet fertility is still
much above the replacement level required to balance Viet Nam's very favorable mortality
and to reduce population growth to the minimum needed for successful national
development. There is also a particular need to increase the availability of modern and
effective temporary methods among younger women and for birth spacing purposes,
aspects of family planning which are very important for reproductive and child health but



- 15 -
which have to date been underemphasized in the Viet Nam program. The high incidence of
pregnancy termination, moreover, is a very strong indicator of unsatisfied demand and
need for appropriate and effective contraception, and in itself is potentially very damaging
to women's health. For all these reasons, the major overriding issue in population and
family planning is the urgent need to develop a new and broader approach to family
planning which will be suitable for a maturing program like Viet Nam's, which will
stimulate faster progress in contraceptive use and fertility reduction, and which will better
promote reproductive health. Though not as cost-efficient as the current procedure-based
program, this approach represents the only way of achieving the country's broad
demographic and health objectives. Costs can still be held down by the continued use of
Viet Nam's PHC system at the lower levels to deliver the bulk of these broader-based
services.
1.47  Government is keenly aware of these problems and issues, and has formulated a set
of policies and strategies aimed at developing a broader approach to family planning in
Viet Nam. These are set out in the Aide-Memoire presented to the Cairo ICPD conference
in September 1994 by the Government of Viet Nam. The major principles of the family
planning program are:
(a)   a reaffirmation of Government's position that population and family
planning are an important part of the national development strategy
and a prominent factor in socio-economic development, playing a
fundamental role in raising the quality of life for all; hence approval of the
integration of population and development, with population placed in the
context of poverty alleviation, building of an equitable, civilized and happy
society, and ensuring of sustained and sustainable economic growth and
development;
(b)   adherence to the principle of voluntary participation in family planning,
based on full information, combined with responsibility of each couple
and individual for the numbers, spacing and timing of their children;
(c)   recognition of the right of every person to reproductive health care;
(d)   advocacy of the integration of family planning with maternal and child
health care in order to reduce maternal and childhood mortality;
(e)   recognition of the role of abortion as not a method of family planning in
itself, but rather a response to women's right to choice and to safe
abortion; and
(f)   recognition of the need to reduce the incidence of abortion to a
minimum through a strengthened family planning program.



- 16 -
1.48   Strategies to implement these policy principles overlap considerably. They share a
common approach to family planning as a broad-based program which seeks to satisfy
client demand and ensure health benefits through provision of a wide range of
contraceptive methods, good-quality clinical services, extensive IEC and counseling, and
constant concern for family health. The strategies that focus on family planning itself
include:
(a)   efforts to diversify and increase the choice of contraceptive methods
available to couples, so that they have more options among modern and
effective short-term, reversible and spacing methods; this would include
intensive promotion of oral contraceptives (both the combined formula for
general use and the progestin-only formula for breastfeeding mothers),
condoms, injectables, Norplant, and the latest designs of IUDs,
(b)   expansion of IEC programs and materials to inform couples through a
variety of media about the advantages of small families and optimum birth
spacing and timing, and about the contraceptive choices open to them,
besides correcting mistaken beliefs and fears about particular methods;
(c)   refresher training for health providers, in order to bring their knowledge of
all contraceptive methods fully up to date and to correct mistaken beliefs
about particular methods;
(d)   better training in counseling for health providers to ensure that they
promote appropriate use of all available methods and give correct
information on the advantages and disadvantages of each method;
(e)   improved remuneration, training and working conditions for collaborators
and motivators, coupled with a gradual phasing-out of incentives;
(f)   improved systems of supervision and monitoring to ensure that providers,
motivators and collaborators are performing well and observing the major
policy principles set out above; and
(g)   improved access to family planning services for groups that are currently
underserved, such as the populations of many remote and isolated areas.
1.49  These strategies will also work in general to improve reproductive care and the
integration of family planning with maternal and child care, as well as to reduce abortion.
Additional priority areas for the improvement of reproductive health care overlap with
the PHC needs outlined in paras. 1.39-1.45 above; they include:
(a)   safe delivery facilities for normal deliveries at commune level or, in isolated
areas, the best possible care within the community;



- 17 -
(b)   safe care and emergency transport for complicated deliveries at district
level;
(c)   care of anaemia in pregnancy;
(d)   high-quality services for IUD insertion and pregnancy terminations, to
prevent post-procedure infections; and
(e)   adequate treatment of common RTIs at the commune and district level.
1.50  Special additional strategies to improve the integration of family planning with
maternal and child health care again overlap with PHC needs as outlined in paras. 1.39-
1.45; they include:
(a)   adjustment of health service organization at commune and district level to
ensure that integrated MCH/FP packages of care are available to clients;
(b)   widening of the role of collaborators to include promotion of maternal and
child health in addition to family planning; and
(c)   refresher training for health providers that will emphasize integration of
MCH and family planning.
1.51   Special additional strategies to reduce abortion include:
(a)   action to ensure that every woman discontinuing an FP method (except to
conceive) is provided with another method before leaving the facility or
provider; and
(b)   action to improve post-abortion and post-partum counseling and services
so as to ensure that an FP method is provided immediately after abortion
and as soon as required after a birth.
1.52  Many of these policy principles and strategies build on existing policies, guidelines
and best practices in Viet Nam. The challenge will be to improve and revitalize current
average performance within a context of limited financial resources and an ailing primary
health care system.
C. BANK EXPERIENCE
Lessons from Previous IDA Experience
1.53  IDA has experience with only one social sector project in Viet Nam to date,
namely the Primary Education Project (effective January, 1994). The key lesson that has



- 18 -
emerged during the early stages of this project's implementation is the need to ensure that
staff of the implementing agency are fully trained in IDA procedures before project start-
up, particularly in procurement, and that they receive additional follow-up assistance
during the initial implementation period. RMV implementation specialists are expected to
play a key role in this process. Because of the general inexperience of NCPFP with self-
implemented donor projects, it will be even more important than usual to ensure that initial
project activities are carefully planned well in advance. Retrospective financing from the
project will be available to provide any needed extra technical assistance.
1.54  However the NCPFP has benefited in these respects from the use of a PHRD
Grant for the preparation of the project; this was executed by IDA but with RMV handling
all local expenditures. The NCPFP Project Unit used PHRD funds to: conduct an
extensive facilities survey, covering both infrastructure and utilization, in five project
provinces; hold project planning meetings at provincial, district and commune level;
conduct a management review of the NCPFP, including a brainstorming workshop for
NCPFP department directors on strategic planning issues; procure local consultants for
preparation of specific components; procure a vehicle, computers and other office
equipment for the PU; and finance some of the PU's operating costs. In the process, PU
staff acquired invaluable familiarity with IDA procurement, contracting and reporting
procedures. Initially progress was slow, but with due assistance from RMV and
international consultants, the PU staff quickly became effective partners in execution.
Country Assistance Strategy
1.55  IDA's goals in Viet Nam, as laid out in the November, 1995 Country Assistance
Strategy (CAS), are to promote broad-based economic development and to combat
poverty. IDA's strategy in Vietnam largely reflects Government's own broad strategy for
development (see para. 1 .5). It is made up of five elements: strengthening of
macroeconomic management; support to the transition to a market economy; a leading
donor role in infrastructure development; assistance to development and implementation
of a poverty-alleviation strategy; assistance to development and implementation of a
strategy for sustainable use of natural resources. This IDA strategy is to be pursued
through a combination of adjustment lending, investment projects, economic and sector
work, and technical assistance.
1.56  A key element in the strategy for poverty alleviation is to assist in the provision of
basic social services to poor households, in the belief that sustainable social service
delivery systems should be developed as the basis for human capital accumulation and
national social protection. The Population and Family Health project is seen in the CAS as
contributing to the poverty-alleviation strategy through: a) its development of sustainable
family health and family planning systems; b) its targeting of specific components to
particularly vulnerable groups; and c) its assistance towards resolving the urgent problem
of rapid population growth. In addition, it complements the Primary Education Project,



- 19 -
another key contribution to the poverty alleviation strategy; on the one hand educated
girls are more likely to reduce their fertility but require good-quality FP services in order
to do so, and on the other hand reductions in fertility and population growth will enable
future investment in education to focus more rewardingly on quality improvements rather
than just expanding coverage to meet the needs of ever-increasing cohorts of children. The
National Health Support Project is seen as a third key contributor to the poverty-
alleviation strategy. Both the Population and Family Health project and the National
Health Support Project also respond directly to the findings of IDA's "Poverty Assessment
and Strategy Report"; in particular to the finding that insufficient resources are going to
finance highly cost-effective public health and clinical services.
1.57  In addition to its impact on poverty alleviation, the Population and Family Health
project will also contribute to the strategy for sustainable use of natural resources. The
rapid growth of population in Viet Nam has put severe pressure on agricultural and forest
land. Conservation and long-term sustainable use of these resources will not be possible
without the reduction of population growth to a minimum, which in turn will require the
reduction of fertility to replacement level.
Rationale for IDA Involvement
1.58  IDA has for some years been engaged in a dialogue with Government on major
issues in the population, health and nutrition sectors. This dialogue began in 1992 with the
preparation of the "Viet Nam Population, Health and Nutrition Sector Review" and was
sustained through the work carried out for the "Viet Nam Poverty Assessment and
Strategy Report" (1995) and the preparation of the National Health Support Project and a
nutrition project. Building on the advantage of this comprehensive sectoral context, the
proposed project would provide an opportunity to focus on the specific needs of the
population/family planning sector.
1.59  The project would support the new approach to population and family planning
programs in Viet Nam which is outlined in Government's position paper presented at the
Cairo conference. This approach, which seeks to strengthen the integration of family
planning into the MCH and reproductive health context and to broaden the FP method
mix substantially, requires investment on a massive scale and with in-depth technical inputs
across the sector. The established donors in the field, notably UNFPA, UNICEF and GTZ,
have made invaluable contributions to the FP program to date. However, IDA is in the
unique position of being able to supply or mobilize both financial and technical resources
on the requisite scale and with the requisite scope.



- 20 -
2. THE PROJECT
A. OBJECTIVES AND SCOPE OF THE PROJECT
Overall Purpose And Scope Of The Project
2.1    As described in Chapter I, Government has identified current issues and needs in
the family health and family planning sectors and has formulated strategies to strengthen
and revitalize current primary health and FP programs. The overall purpose of the
Population and Family Health project is to support this strengthening and revitalization,
thereby improving the health status of the population and bringing about a further
reduction in fertility and population growth. Table 2.1 lays out a matrix relating specific
project activities to the policy principles and strategies discussed in Chapter 1.
2.2   The project will operate at two levels, the first national and the second in a
selected group of 15 provinces. At national level, the focus will be on overall nation-wide
strengthening of the family planning program, with attention also to related family health
interventions. For the selected 15 provinces, the focus will be on strengthening of the
PHC system at commune and district levels, with a particular emphasis on MCH and
family planning services. Similar support to another group of 15 provinces is being
provided through Component I of the contemporaneous National Health Support Project.
The two sets of provinces to be covered include all those with the highest levels of
poverty and FP/MCH needs which are not already covered by other donor projects (see
Annex 3). Thus overall, more than half of Viet Nam's total 53 provinces, including most of
the neediest, would receive support to upgrade their grass-roots health care system under
the two projects combined. It is expected also that Government would undertake similar
strengthening in the remaining less needy provinces if experience in the two projects
provides a successful model.
Broad Project Goals
2.3   The Population and Family Health project is intended to support two major policy
aims of Government. The first policy aim is to achieve the demographic objectives of the
population and family planning program. These are, for the year 2000:
a national population growth rate of 1.7 percent per annum
a population size of no more than 82 million
Originally, objectives of a national TFR of 2.9 or lower, and a national CPR (all methods)
of 63 percent had also been set. Since these have been met, new objectives are in place
(see para. 2.7 below).

The project will primarily address this aim through the national components focused on
family planning, but investment in selected provincial PHC systems would be expected to
improve family planning performance still further in the covered provinces. It and the
matching National Health Support Project investment should also have an added impact at
national level, as a model and stimulus for similar investment in the rest of the country.
2.4   The second major policy aim of Government is to improve the health of mothers
and children in line with Government's current objectives for the year 2000 of:
a national IMR of 30
a national IMMR of 70
The project's contribution to realization of this aim would be expected to apply primarily
to the 15 provinces selected for upgrading of primary health care. However some national
impact would again occur through their role as a national model, plus the beneficial effect
of the broader-based approach to family planning to be implemented through the project's
national components.
2.5   Based on these major Government policy aims, the project's objectives are:
(a)   to increase utilization of family planning and related family health services;
(b)   to improve the quality and range of family planning and related family
health services;
(c)   to enhance NCPFP's management, planning and policy formulation
capabilities; and
(d)   to expand the knowledge base on which policy and technical guidelines
would be founded.
The first two objectives will be addressed through both the national and the provincial-
level components. The third and fourth will be addressed entirely at the national level.
Specific Project Objectives and Indicators
2.6    Within this broad framework of project objectives, a matrix of specific objectives
and indicators at both national and provincial level has been established for the purposes of
project monitoring and impact evaluation. Indicators cover project inputs, activities,
specific outcomes and broad impact, with specification of how each indicator would be
measured. The matrix is shown in full in Annex 4 and has been integrated into project
reporting formats and management and supervisory arrangements.



- 24 -
2.7   Within this matrix, ten key indicators have been selected to measure project impact
and achievement under the project objectives (a), (b), and (c) in para. 2.5. They are
related to corresponding key specific project objectives, which are linked to the policy
principles, strategies and specific project activities laid out in Table 2.1, as well as
Government's own objectives for the year 2000 as given above. Achievement of objectives
(d) will be necessary in order to reach objectives (a) through (c) and hence is indirectly
measured at the same time. All key indicators and specific objectives relate to broad
outcomes or impact of the project, and all pertain to the year of completion of the project.
They are as follows:
Demographic and Health Outcome
(1)   A national decline in the TFR to below 2.5 by 2002.
(2)   A national increase in the CPR for modern methods to 56 percent by 2002.
(3)   A national reduction of 30 percent in the TAR by 2002.
(4)   A national decline in the IMR to 25 by 2002.
Service Performance and Quality
(5)   A national increase in the share of temporary/short-term and supply-based
methods (pills, condoms, injectables) to 25 percent by 2002
(6)   A national improvement in access to an adequate range of family planning
services by 2002.
(7)   An improvement in utilization of CHC facilities for pre-natal care in the
project provinces by 2002.
(8)   An improvement in the met need for life-saving obstetric care in the project
provinces by 2002.
(9)   An increase in the percent of infants who are fully immunized in the project
provinces.
(10)  A national improvement in the management information score (see Annex 4
for description of the score).
Current base measures for indicators 6-10 are not available, and hence specific objectives
cannot yet be set. The baseline survey or baseline studies will provide these measures, and
corresponding objectives will then be set for 2002 through consultation between MOH,
NCPFP and (where appropriate) local authorities in the project provinces and elsewhere.



- 25 -
During negotiations, Government provided assurances that the key indicators and
objectives listed in para. 2.6-2.7 and the matrix of indicators shown in Annex 4 will
be used to measure, monitor and evaluate implementation progress and project
impact [para. 6.1 (c)]. Government also provided assurances that baseline surveys
and studies substantially in accordance with the outline given in Annex 14 will be
carried out, and their results used to complete the list of key project objectives as
described in para. 2.7, by June 30, 1997 [para. 6.1 (d)].
2.8   The project will be implemented over a period of seven years, from 1996 to 2002.
B. STRUCTURE AND CONTENT OF THE PROJECT
2.9   The project is composed of five components, of which four are national in scope
and focused on family planning and the fifth covers the primary health care system
(commune and district-level) in 15 selected provinces. These components consist of:
(a)   Provincial Service Delivery.  Strengthening of PHC services in 15
provinces, with a focus on family health and family planning services;
(b)   IEC. Strengthening of family planning and family health IEC programs
and activities;
(c)   Contraceptive Supplies. Support for the much-increased supplies of
contraceptive commodities required for increases in prevalence of modern
methods and use of temporary and birth spacing methods;
(d)   FP Management and Institutional Development. Strengthening of the
management and planning capabilities of NCPFP and its subsidiary
committee network; and
(e)   Service Delivery Model Initiatives. Provision of a fund for studies and
pilots of improved service delivery approaches and interventions in key
areas of family planning and related family health issues.
Component 1: Provincial Service Delivery:
(US$74.8 M equivalent estimated total project cost)
2.10  This component will strengthen the primary health care system in 15 selected
provinces of Viet Nam. These provinces consist of Lam Dong, Dong Thap, Bac Thai, Hai
Hung, Thua Thien Hue, Lao Cai, Nam Ha, Hai Phong, Dac Lac, Tuyen Quang, Lai Chau,
Vinh Long, Thanh Hoa, Tra Vinh and Kien Giang (see Map 1). They were selected
according to criteria based on relatively low health and FP status, adequate management
capacity, no substantial FP donor presence, and regional spread (see Annex 3). These
criteria also resulted, by and large, in the selection of the country's poorer provinces. Nine



- 26 -
out of the 15 project provinces have significant proportions (between 15 and 80 percent)
of disadvantaged ethnic minorities among their population (see para. 4.18).
2.11   On the basis of a standard package of selected services to be provided by the
upgraded facilities, three standard packages of inputs have been developed for upgrading
of CHCs, DHCs and provincial MCH/FP centres in the 15 selected provinces (see Annex
5); intercommunal polyclinics would be classified on the basis of size and functions as
equivalent to either a CHC or a DHC and would receive the corresponding package. The
National Health Support Project is supplying exactly the same standard package to 2819
CHCs in the 15 provinces covered under its Service Delivery Component; its district-level
package for 155 DHCs is similar but not identical, covering fewer specialized higher-level
FP services and more specialized higher-level health services.
2.12  The inputs supplied under each package include: civil works to the extent
necessary to provide the basic package of services, covering mainly building renovation
and/or extension, plus some re-building where required (US$24.9 million); supplies of
additional furniture, vehicles and equipment to bring each facility up to a complete
specified standard (US$20.3 million); annual additional supplies of essential drugs and
medical supplies to bring each facility up to a complete standard list (US$16.1 million);
and refresher training for all commune and district-level health staff, including training of
trainers at national and provincial level (US$4.5 million). Facility upgrading would be
provided to 440 health posts, about half of all CHCs in the project provinces (amounting
to 1,616 CHCs), and all 184 district-level and 15 provincial-level facilities. During
negotiations, Government provided assurances that the selection of CHCs to receive
physical facility upgrading will be carried out according to criteria agreed with IDA
and substantially in accordance with those laid out in Annex 5 [para. 6.1 (h)l. The
project will finance costs of civil works; their maintenance during the life of the project;
incremental furniture, vehicles, equipment and essential drugs and medical supplies;
development of standard training curricula; training of trainers; and refresher training
courses for all commune and district-level staff, During negotiations, Government
provided assurances that all CHCs, DHCs and MCH/FP centers in the 15 project
provinces will be appropriately staffed and all CHCs will be provided with adequate
funding to pay staff salaries [para. 6.1 (g)]. Government also provided assurances
that it would furnish IDA by September 30, 1996, and have an exchange of views
with IDA on, a comprehensive description of the policy and implementation
guidelines it will employ to ensure the effective implementation of: (i) cost recovery
with respect to the essential drugs to be provided by the project in those communes
of project provinces where applicable; and (ii) provision of essential drugs and
supplies free of charge in mountainous and remote communes in project provinces.
The policy and implementation guidelines would be reviewed by the Government
and IDA each year thereafter and would be an explicit focus of the mid-term review
[para. 6.1 (r)].



- 27 -
2.13   Three additional key aspects of this upgrading package are the long term
maintenance of the upgraded buildings and equipment, the adequacy of the logistics
system to be used for storing and distributing essential drugs, as well as contraceptives
(see para. 2.14), and the expansion of outreach systems. Maintenance costs during
implementation will be supported by the project out of Government's contribution (see
paras. 3.6-3.7) through a corresponding annual budget for maintenance of project
buildings, once upgraded. During negotiations, Government provided assurances that
all CHCs, DHCs and MCH/FP centers physical facilities upgraded under the project
will be adequately maintained during project implementation according to the
guidelines specified in Annex 5 Ipara. 6.1 (i)l.
2.14  To assure adequate storage and distribution of essential drugs and contraceptives,
an efficient and cost-effective national logistics system is to be developed (see Annex 8).
It would be based on contracting out of all logistics services, except storage at the central
and provincial levels, to specialized Government agencies or the private sector. The costs
of contracting out would be covered by a 7.5 percent service charge on all commodities
handled. This system would be introduced initially for the essential drugs and
contraceptives procured under the project and then expanded to cover all health and
family planning supplies.
2.15  To strengthen PHC service delivery still further, two model outreach systems are
to be tested under Component 5 during the first half of the project period (see para. 2.25).
If evaluated as successful, they would be extended to cover the 15 project provinces under
this component (US$9.0 million). The first model is a network of village-level health posts
in remote mountainous areas and the second a strengthened hamlet-based collaborator
network. The project will finance construction, equipment and essential drug supplies for
the health posts, and equipment, retraining and allowances for the collaborators. During
negotiations, Government provided assurances that in the event that the pilot test of
a strengthened community-based outreach system for family planning and family
health described in Annex 11 is evaluated as successful, an action plan for its
implementation will be submitted to IDA for approval and then will be implemented
as approved in the project provinces [para. 6.1 (1)].
2.16  Civil works for all facilities except health posts would be phased over the first
three years of the project, with the provinces of Lam Dong, Dong Thap, Bac Thai, Hai
Hung, and Thua Thien Hue covered in the first year of the project and the remaining ten
provinces covered in the second and third years. Provision of furniture, vehicles,
equipment and essential drugs and medical supplies would commence from the second
year of the project, with furniture and equipment phased in where necessary according to
completion of civil works. Curricula development and training of central and provincial
trainers would be carried out in the first year; refresher training courses would begin in the
second year and would be phased to correspond with completion of civil works and
delivery of furniture and equipment where necessary.



- 28 -
Component 2: IEC
(US$16.8 M equivalent estimated total project cost)
2.17  This component will strengthen and expand IEC channels, programs and activities
for family planning and family health programs, in order to deliver a set of key messages
which are based on the IEC strategy already developed by NCPFP's IEC Department (see
Annex 6) and tailored to the new approach to family planning outlined above. These
messages will cover: the use of family planning to delay, space and limit childbearing
through appropriate modern methods; the prevention and treatment of RTIs; aspects of
pre-natal and emergency obstetric care; and population issues such as the small family
norm and gender bias. As an additional strategy, a social marketing initiative for the pill
will be launched.
2.18  The IEC  program  will focus both on strengthening of the interpersonal
communication channels that have been historically so important in the Viet Narr. FP
program and on making full use of the ever-increasing influence of mass and print media.
Its major elements would include: equipping all collaborators with IEC bags and (in
remote mountainous areas) bicycles; strengthening the interpersonal communication and
counseling skills of health providers; providing an ample supply of simple reference and
instructional print materials for all health providers and collaborators; and designing and
implementing a full range of IEC programs and activities through mass media, local
campaigns and folk media. All collaborators would also be trained in interpersonal
communication and counseling, but this would be financed out of the NCPFP regular
budget. The social marketing initiative for the pill would focus primarily on urban areas,
which contain about 20 percent of Viet Nam's population. It would include: a community-
based door-to-door promotion and home-resupply program for poor urban
neighborhoods; a standard social marketing program for pharmacies in better-off
neighborhoods; and a national mass media campaign to promote the pill which would
cover both urban and rural populations.
2.19  The project will finance costs of: IEC materials development, production and air or
screen time; training of IEC trainers; IEC training courses for health providers and IEC
staff, transport and equipment for collaborators; IEC special campaigns, workshops,
events etc.; and social marketing program costs other than pills themselves, which will be
supplied under Component 3.
Component 3: Contraceptive Supplies:
(US$25.3 M equivalent estimated total project costs)
2.20  This component will finance the purchase of the bulk of contraceptive commodities
required for an increased modern-method CPR and broadened method mix over the four
years 1997-2000. Condoms are excluded because they have never been supplied through
donor financing. Government has always supplied them directly to the program through
the regular NCPFP budget and will continue to do so for the future. Table 2.2 shows the



- 29 -
desired changes in the method mix to be achieved over the life of the project, and Annex 7
gives details of the projections of contraceptive use and costs on which this component is
based. Broadly speaking, overall contraceptive prevalence would rise from a current
estimated CPR for modem methods of 44 percent to 56 percent in 2002. The percent of
users relying on permanent or semi-permanent methods (IUDs, sterilization, Norplant)
would change only a little from 58 percent to 60 percent. However the 10 percent now
using temporary methods which require large volumes of supplies (pills, condoms,
injectables) would rise to 25 percent by 2002, while conversely the 30 percent currently
using "natural" methods (mainly rhythm and withdrawal) would fall to 15 percent. Within
categories, the use of new, improved types of IUDs and progestin-only pills would rise.
2.21   At present, the total national bill for contraceptive commodities of about US$2.5 -
3 million (excluding condoms) is met largely by UNFPA. The project will finance all such
costs as calculated above (excluding condoms) for the four years 1997-2000, including the
7.5 percent logistics charge. For the years 2001 and 2002, it is assumed that Government,
with or without the assistance of donors, would meet the bulk of commodity costs.
However the project will provide a small reserve fund for those years in case of
unexpected needs.
