Component 4: FP Management and Institutional Development
(US$6.7 M equivalent estimated total project cost)
2.22   This component will strengthen management, planning and policy formulation
capabilities in NCPFP and its subsidiary network of CPFPs, including upgrading of
management skills and improvements in reporting systems. For the upgrading of
management skills, a series of workshops would be held as a follow up to the management
review undertaken during project preparation (see para. 1.54). The workshops will
identify organizational needs in the light of strategic planning priorities for the NCPFP and
its subsidiaries, and plan the corresponding skill and training needs. Appropriate curricula



- 30 -
will then be developed and training of trainers carried out. Actual training will be funded
under the NCPFP regular budget.
2.23 For the improvement of the FP program management information systems (MIS),
an overall strategy has been developed (see Annex 9), according to which the MIS would
support strategic planning activities at national and provincial levels, operational activities
at commune level, and a coordinating function at district level. The MIS would be fully
computerized at national and provincial level, but not below for the present. The project
will support costs of computers and associated software; other MIS and office equipment;
planning workshops; development of management and MIS training curricula; training of
management and MIS trainers; and MIS training courses for selected NCPFP, PCPFP,
DCPFP and CPFPB staff. The component will also cover the costs of the project
management structure and of monitoring and evaluation activities.
Component 5: Service Delivery Model Initiatives
(US$6.0 M equivalent estimated total project cost)
2.24  This component will provide a basis for policy and strategy review and adjustment
in the areas of family planning and family health service delivery. It will provide funds for
development of models and pilot testing of new or innovative approaches and models for
service delivery, with supporting studies, wherever there are issues, gaps or needs in
existing systems. Proposals for topics will be developed, reviewed, and financed if
acceptable, over the period of project implementation as needs arise. Arrangements for the
administration of component funds are given in Annex 10. During negotiations,
Government provided assurances that detailed proposals for all activities to be
financed under the service delivery model initiatives component will be submitted,
through a Service Delivery Model Initiatives Committee to be established by August
31, 1996 and described in Annex 10, to IDA for prior review and approval, and
thereafter will be implemented as approved by IDA [para. 6.1 (k)l.
2.25  An initial indicative list of topics has been developed, and is given in detail in
Annex 11. It includes:
(a)   testing of a model for a strengthened collaborator system (including the
renaming of the collaborators as Family Health Workers), with smaller
numbers, larger allowances, full-time working hours and tasks expanded to
encompass the promotion of family health, in selected communes of the
fifteen project provinces; the cost of expanding the new model to provide
entire coverage of the project provinces (equivalent to about one-third of
the country's population) would be supported under Component I (see
para. 2.15), if the model is evaluated as successful;
(b)   testing of a model outreach system to supplement CHC primary health care
services in remote mountainous areas (where most disadvantaged ethnic
minority populations live), involving establishment of village-based health



- 31 -
posts staffed from the CHC and providing FP/family health promotion and
minor curative services; the model would be tested in remote mountainous
areas of the fifteen project provinces and then extended to cover all such
areas in the provinces under Component I (see para. 2.15) if evaluated as
successful;
(c)   studies and model testing for ways of mobilizing the private and NGO
sectors to provide family planning and reproductive health services through
a variety of approaches, including credit schemes, updated training for
providers, incentive programs for providers in remote areas (see para.
4.24), support to establishment of professional associations for private
providers; supplies of tailormade IEC materials; and a systematic review of
constraints on private practice followed by action to alleviate them;
(d)   a pilot test of the acceptability and impact of charging for contraceptives;
the results would be used to introduce a phased system of charges during
the second half of the project;
(e)   studies and testing of improved approaches to safe motherhood services,
including key aspects of safe pregnancy and delivery such as reduction of
anaemia and vitamin A deficiency, safe home deliveries, and management
of high-risk pregnancy and obstetric complications; the emphasis would be
on understanding the patterns and causes of current problems and on
improving current approaches;
(f)   studies and testing of improved approaches or protocols in key problematic
aspects of family planning and reproductive health, including the very high
incidence of MR/abortion, the quality and acceptability of IUD services,
and prevention and treatment of RTIs; the emphasis would be on
understanding the patterns and causes of current problems and on
improving current approaches; and
(g)   testing of model approaches and strategies for improving access to good-
quality family planning and family health services for disadvantaged ethnic
minorities, including (beyond the health post model described above)
special participative baseline and evaluative studies, participative design of
models and strategies and piloting of selected approaches, such as special
incentive programs to attract private and NGO providers to minority areas
and special training programs for traditional midwives.
During negotiations, Government provided assurances that: (a) a pilot scheme of
charges for contraceptives will be carried out by June 30, 1999; (b) an action plan
will be prepared on the basis of its results by August 31, 1999 for review and



- 32 -
comment by IDA; and (c) the action plan will thereafter be promptly carried out
[para. 6.1 (j)l.
2.26 During negotiations, Government provided assurances that a time-bound
action plan for all training activities under the project will be provided to IDA for
review and comments by June 30, 1996 [para. 6.1 (q)l.

3. PROJECT COSTS, FINANCING, PROCUREMENT
AND DISBURSEMENT
A. COST ESTIMATES
3.1   Summary of Project Costs.  The total cost of the project is estimated at
Vietnamese Dongs 1,426 billion or US$129.6 million equivalent, including contingencies
and taxes. A summary of costs by project component is given in Table 3.1, and a summary
by category of expenditure in Table 3.2. Costs by component, category of expenditure and
year are given in Annex 12.
3.2   Contingency Allowances. Project costs include a contingency allowance for
unforeseen physical additions at 3 percent of base cost (US$3.8 million equivalent) and for
inflation at 14.0 percent of base cost (US$15.5 million equivalent) in accordance with
Bank-wide and regional guidelines.
3.3   Taxes and Duties. Project cost estimates for technical assistance and training
have been calculated net of taxes. Taxes levied internally on local purchasing are estimated
at about US$4.3 million (3.3 percent of total project cost). Taxes on imported goods and
consultant services contracts will be fully met by Government from its own resources.
3.4   Foreign Exchange Costs.  Direct and indirect foreign exchange costs are
estimated at 53 percent of base project cost. The estimated foreign exchange component
for the various expenditure categories is as follows: civil works 30 percent; equipment 72
percent; drugs 95 percent; contraceptive supplies 95 percent; foreign technical assistance
90 percent; local technical assistance 10 percent; overseas training 95 percent; local
training 20 percent; service delivery model initiatives 18 percent; maintenance 20 percent;
operating costs and logistics 10 percent.
3.5   Incremental Recurrent Costs.  The project investments in provincial PHC
systems and the national FP program will add no new regular staff in either case; hence
there will be no incremental salary costs. Incremental supplies of drugs and medical
supplies and contraceptives provided under the project are viewed as investments. In the
case of drugs and medical supplies, the aim is to restore public confidence and hence
former utilization rates for PHC facilities through a major upgrading effort; while in the
case of contraceptives, the aim is to bring about a major shift in the FP method mix
towards supply-based methods. Both types of commodity will be fully funded out of the
IDA credit, ADB concessional loan or KfW grant. They will nonetheless require to be
sustained after the life of the project; this issue is addressed in paras. 4.30-4.33 and Annex
16.



- 34 -
3.6   There are four types of incremental costs generated by the project that will be
classified as incremental recurrent costs and included as such in the total project cost. The
first type is maintenance of civil works undertaken under the project (5.6 percent of the
base cost of civil works) and of equipment purchased under the project (7.5 percent of the
base cost of equipment), amounting altogether to US$4.2 million (total including
contingencies) over the project period. Maintenance costs for civil works cover materials
and labor while those for equipment cover spare parts and labor. The second type is the
incremental budget for collaborator allowances (6 percent of the total base costs,
amounting to US$8.7 million including contingencies) required from 1998 onwards, if the
strengthened collaborator system to be piloted under Component 5 in five project
provinces is successful, and hence is extended throughout the fifteen project provinces.
The third type is logistics costs (4 percent of total base costs, amounting to US$5.6
million including contingencies) for storing and distributing drugs and medical supplies
and contraceptives. The fourth type is operating costs (1 percent of total base costs,
amounting to US$1.1 million including contingencies) covering travel, supplies and
communications expenses for management and training activities needed during the life of
the project. Maintenance and logistics costs will be funded 100 percent out of the
Government contribution; for outreach worker allowances and operating costs, 70 percent
will be funded out of the IDA Credit and 30 percent out of the Government contribution.
The first three types of incremental costs will require sustaining after the life of the
project: this issue is addressed in paras 4.30-4.33 and Annex 16.



- 35 -

B. FINANCING PLAN
3.7       The estimated  total project cost of US$129.6 million  equivalent, including  taxes,
will be financed by:
(a)    a Government contribution of US$18.6 million equivalent including taxes;
(b)    an IDA credit of SDR33 .6 million (US$50.0 million equivalent) net of
taxes;
(c)    co-financing from an ADB concessional loan of US$41.0 million; and



- 37 -
(d)   co-financing from a KfW grant of DM. 30 million (approximately US$20
million).
3.8   The structure of project financing is laid out in Annex 12, and may be summarized
as follows:
(a)   the Government contribution of US$18.6 million equivalent (14.4 percent
of total project cost including taxes) will finance logistics costs,
maintenance costs, and a portion of the overall costs of civil works,
outreach workers allowance and operating cost for all components except
Component 5;
(b)   the IDA credit will finance 39.9 percent of total project cost net of taxes,
and would cover: 33.2 percent of the costs of provincial service delivery
(covering five project provinces) under Component 1; 82.8 percent of the
costs of Component 2 (IEC); 11.8 percent of the costs of Component 3
(contraceptive supplies); 92.8 percent of Component 4 (management &
institutional development); and 33.3 percent of the costs of Component 5
(service delivery model initiatives);
(c)   the ADB concessional loan will finance 31.6 percent of total project cost,
and would cover 49.4 percent of the costs of Component I (covering ten
project provinces); and 66.7 percent of the costs of Component 5 (covering
the private/NGO sector development subcomponent); and
(d)   the KfW grant will finance 15.4 percent of total project cost, and would
cover 79 percent of Component 3 (covering contraceptive commodities for
the years 1997-2000, not including logistics costs).
3.9   The contribution from KfW will be administered as parallel co-financing. Funds are
available immediately.
3.10 The contribution from ADB will be administered as parallel co-financing. ADB
would formulate, negotiate and approve a separate project with Government, covering the
elements and costs described above. The project is expected to be submitted to the ADB
Board by end-1996 and to start implementation by the beginning of 1997. ADB's
contribution to the project has therefore been designed in such a way that no funds for
activities covered by them will be required before January, 1997.
3.11  Project Preparation Financing.  Funds from the Japan Policy and Human
Resource Development (PHRD) Fund to the amount of JPY 62.5 million (approximately
US$ 640,000 equivalent) were used for project preparation, under execution by IDA. The
PHRD funds were used for the Project Unit of NCPFP to: conduct an extensive facilities



- 38 -
and infrastructure survey in five project provinces; hold project planning meetings with
their provincial, district and commune networks; engage international and local
consultants to assist in technical preparation of specific project components (in the fields
of IEC, training, MIS, management and ethnic minority research), as well as in the drafting
of project proposals; make study tours of World Bank-supported population and health
projects and national family planning programs in neighboring countries; purchase office
equipment for the PU; and support PU's incremental operating costs.
3.12  In addition, ADB has provided funds for a Project Preparation Technical
Assistance (PPTA) package. This package was designed to supplement and extend the
assistance to preparation given through the PHRD fund. It included similar facilities and
infrastructure surveys in the remaining ten provinces, a multi-visit international
consultancy in private/NGO sector development, and repeat visits of international
consultants to assist the NCPFP in finalizing preparation of the training, MIS and
management components. PPTA activities began in June, 1995, with repeat consultant
visits plus the first visit of the private/NGO development specialist, and are scheduled to
be completed early in 1996.
C. PROCUREMENT
3.13  Procurement arrangements for the project are summarized in Table 3.3.
3.14  Civil Works.  Civil works (estimated cost about US$19.7 million, including
contingencies), will be undertaken following national competitive bidding (NCB)
procedures satisfactory and acceptable to the Bank. In remote and mountainous areas
where packaging would not be possible to follow NCB procedures, relatively small civil
works contracts, each valued at about US$30,000, will be awarded by obtaining at least
three quotations from local contractors up to an aggregate amount of US$3.9 million.
Most civil works included in this project would be small and simple, consisting of repair or
renovations of existing small buildings at CHCs and parts of the DHCs plus some minor
additions such as extra rooms or minor operating theaters, and would be scattered over
the provinces being financed under IDA. Owing to the nature and size of these works,
NCB procedures are appropriate since no foreign contractors are likely to be interested in
them. However, NCPFP will group these works in suitable packages so as to minimize
the number of contracts being awarded under this component, with a view to managing
their execution effectively and efficiently. Further, it would be desirable for NCPFP to use
suitable postqualification criteria to be included in the bidding documents prior to inviting
bids, in order to determine the capacity of the individual contractors to undertake more
than one package while awarding the contracts to them. The contractors may form joint
ventures with other contractors if they wish to enhance their financial and technical
resources. These works will be carried out through the Provincial Project Management
Units (PPMUs) under NCPFP's overall guidance and supervision. NCPFP has drafted
the required bidding documents following the Bank's Standard Bidding Documents for



- 39 -
Procurement of Civil Works (Small Contracts), January 1995, adjusted to suit local
requirements.

3.15  Goods.  The project provides for the procurement of materials, equipment,
vehicles, drugs, and medical supplies.  Inclusive of contingencies, the costs of the
equipment are estimated to equal about US$16.4 million and of drugs and medical
supplies equal to about US$14.8 million. These equipment items and drugs would be
procured following international competitive bidding (ICB) procedures in accordance
with the Bank's Guidelines, January 1995. In respect of equipment and drugs to be
procured by ICB, domestic bidders will be eligible for a margin of preference in the bid
evaluations of 15 percent of the C.I.F. costs of the competing imports or the existing rate
of customs duty, whichever is less. To the extent practicable, contracts for goods will be
grouped in bid packages costing at least US$150,000 each. Contraceptives amounting in
value to US$20.0 million equivalent will be covered under KfW financing arrangements.
IDA will finance contraceptives to the value of US$3.0 million. Contracts for equipment
costing less than the equivalent of US$150,000 each, such as surgical equipment, hospital
beds, delivery beds, delivery tables, diagnostic tables, etc., add up to an aggregate of
US$8.4 million will be procured through NCB procedures satisfactory and acceptable to
the Bank. NCB procurement of surgical equipment is recommended because no foreign
supplier manufactures this type of equipment in the smaller sizes needed to accommodate
Viet Nam's smaller hand and pelvic sizes. Small-value items, or groups of items costing
less than US$25,000 per contract subject to an aggregate amount of US$4.9 million, will
be procured by prudent shopping, requiring quotations from at least three reputed
suppliers. NCPFP has drafted the bidding documents following (a) the Bank's Standard
Bidding Documents for Procurement of Goods, January 1995 for the procurement of
goods and   (b) the Bank's Standard Bidding Documents for Procurement of
Pharmaceuticals and Vaccines, September 1993 as amended to reflect therein the changes
as per the Bank's Guidelines, January 1995 for the procurement of drugs and
pharmaceuticals.
3.16  Training, Technical Assistance, Studies &  Pilots, Outreach  Workers
Allowance and Operating Costs. IDA will finance all or part of the costs (inclusive of
contingencies) of the training (US$5.8 million), technical assistance (US$5.1 million),
operating costs (US$1.1 million), studies & pilots (US$8.0 million) and outreach workers
allowance (US$8.7 million) components, to the extent of US$19.9 million as against the
aggregate of US$28.7 million provided for these components in the project. However, the
maintenance of civil works and equipment (US$4.2 million) and logistics (US$5.6 million)
charges included in the project will be entirely financed by the Government of Viet Nam.
The training component includes the retraining of health workers in job-related knowledge
and skills, IEC counseling etc. and will be carried out by the provincial secondary medical
schools or national institutions. NCPFP will award these assignments, with the exception
of the technical assistance component, to its own agencies in the provinces on a sole
source basis and enter into "responsibility contracts" with them to carry out these
assignments. However, before entrusting these assignments, NCPFP will provide to IDA
essential details as necessary about them, including copies of the draft "responsibility



- 41 -
contracts" for its review, to ensure that they comply with the Bank's Guidelines. The
project supports the employment of consultants to undertake technical assistance
assignments. These consultants will be selected and appointed in accordance with the
Bank's Guidelines: Use of Consultants by World Bank Borrowers and by The World Bank
as Executing Agency. NCPFP will prepare a draft letter of invitation and the contract
following the Bank's Standard Form of Contract for Consulting Services, June 1995.
3.17  Contract Reviews. All civil works contracts in excess of US$150,000 (including
the first two civil works contracts regardless of their respective estimated cost), all goods
contracts in excess of US$150,000 and all consulting contracts in excess of US$100,000
for firms (US$50,000 for individuals) will be subject to prior review by the Bank. With
respect to all consulting contracts, irrespective of value, prior review will be required for:
(a) terms of reference; (b) single source contracts regardless of value; (c) all consultant
contracts and assignments of a critical nature (such as environmental safety or public
health considerations) regardless of value; and (d) contract amendments valued at more
than US$100,000 for firms (US$50,000 for individuals), or those that raise the total
contract value to more than US$100,000 for firms (US$50,000 for individuals). This will
allow prior review for approximately 80 percent of the procurement process under the
project. NCPFP would use the Bank's latest Standard Bidding Documents as follows: (a)
Procurement of Works (Smaller Contractors), January 1995 and as adapted to Vietnam's
local requirements to carry out NCB procurement; (b) Procurement of Goods, January
1995 for materials and equipment; (c) Procurement of Pharmaceuticals and Vaccines,
September 1993 as amended to reflect the changes as per the Bank's Guidelines of
January 1995; and (d) Standard Form of Contract for Consulting Services, June 1995.
NCPFP has already drafted and submitted all documents to the Bank, for use under this
project.
3.18   Table 3.4 provides schedules for the preparation of bidding documents for civil
works, goods and consulting services, procurement actions, construction and selection of
consultants under the project components. Project activities are expected to commence
early in 1996 and conclude in 2002.
D. DISBURSEMENT
3.19  The proposed IDA credit of US$50.0 million equivalent will be disbursed over a
period of seven and a half years, with disbursements to be completed by the closing date
of June 30, 2003. The proposed disbursement schedule is shown in Table 3.5 and Figure
1. Little accumulated experience is available for Viet Nam, so the profile for the East Asia
& Pacific Region is shown as a standard. The proposed schedule has relatively high levels
of early disbursements because of concentrated early procurement of the equipment,
supplies and civil works necessary for facility and FP program upgrading. It is considered
feasible since draft bidding documents are already finalized and RMV staff will provide
support to the staff of the NCPFP PMU and the provincial PMUs in handling procurement



- 42 -
and disbursement procedures during the early implementation period. Arrangements are
also being made for advance training in procurement and disbursement.

3.21   Special Accounts. In order to accelerate disbursements, a Special Account with
an authorized allocation equivalent to US$2.0 million, based on four month's average
expected expenditures, will be established in a bank acceptable to IDA. The first
withdrawal will be limited to an amount equivalent to US$800,000 until the aggregate
amount of withdrawals from the Credit Account plus the total amount of all outstanding
special commitments entered into by IDA shall be equal to or exceed the equivalent of
US$16 million. The PMU in NCPFP would manage and operate the Special Account. All
documentation relating to the Special Account will be kept in the PMIU and made available
for review by IDA. Applications for replenishment of the Account, supported by
appropriate documentation, will be submitted monthly or when the account is drawn by 20
percent of the Authorized Allocation, whichever occurs first. For withdrawal outside the
Special Account (applications for direct payment or for issuance of Special Commitments)
a minimum application value of 20 percent of the Authorized Allocations will be observed.
3.22   Statements  of Expenditure.   Full documentation  will be  required  for
disbursements against contracts for goods in excess of US$150,000, contracts for civil
works in excess of US$150,000, and consulting contracts in excess of US$100,000 for
firms and US$50,000 for individuals. Disbursements for all other items below these levels
will be made against Statements of Expenditures (SOEs). All documents supporting the
SOEs will be retained by the PMNU in NCPFP and made available for random sample
review by IDA supervision missions.



- 45 -
4. PROJECT MANAGEMENT AND IMPLEMENTATION
A. PROJECT MANAGEMENT ARRANGEMENTS
4.1   The proposed project will be implemented by the NCPFP and the local
governments of the project provinces.  An organogram of the project management
arrangements is shown in Annex 13. During negotiations, Government provided
assurances that the project management structure as laid out in Annex 13 will be
established, staffed and maintained throughout the project implementation period
Ipara. 6.1 (m)l. As a condition of effectiveness, Government will provide evidence
satisfactory to IDA that: (a) the Project Steering Committee, the Project Executive
Committee and the NCPFP Project Management Unit have been established and
the Project Director appointed; and (b) the Provincial Project Management Units in
the five provinces included in the first phase of Component I have been established
and the corresponding Provincial Project Managers appointed (para. 6.2).
4.2    A ministerial-level Project Steering Committee (PSC) will be established to
provide overall direction and guidance to the project. It will be chaired by the Chairman,
NCPFP, and will include high-level representation from MOH, the MPI, SBV and the
Ministry of Finance. The Committee will meet as often as required, and not less than once
every three months.
4.3   The technical implementation of individual components will be assigned to the
corresponding existing Departments of NCPFP. The Directors of these Departments will
form the members of an Executive Committee (EC), which will be established under the
PSC and will also be chaired by the Chairman, NCPFP. The EC will meet every two
months. A Project Management Unit (PMU) will be established to coordinate and
administer the day-to-day implementation of the project, including the handling of all
procurement and disbursement. Its Director will be seconded from within NCPFP, and will
take the rank of Department Director. Its Deputy-Director will be seconded from MOH.
The PMU will act as secretariat to the EC. The 16 staff of the PMU will include both staff
seconded from within NCPFP and hired contractual staff as needed to provide particular
technical expertise.
4.4   At provincial level, a Provincial Project Management Unit (PPMU) will be set up
in each of the 15 project provinces. The PPMUs will coordinate day-to-day
implementation of project activities in their provinces, including the management of civil
works (see Annex 5). Each PPMU will be composed of four staff, who will be seconded
from within the PCPFP or hired as contractual staff, as needed. The mandate of the
PPMIJs will include close coordination with the corresponding provincial health bureaus.
During negotiations, Government provided assurances that all necessary steps will
be taken to ensure that each of the project provinces implements its activities under



- 46 -
the project and is provided, promptly as needed, the funds, facilities, services and
other resources required for that purpose [para. 6.1 (n)l.
4.5   Coordination between NCPFP and MOH is critical for the success of the project,
given that the actual delivery of family planning and family health services is the
responsibility of MOFH.  Several mechanisms are in place to ensure the necessary
collaboration:
*  the NCPFP is structured such that one of the Vice-Chairpersons is also the Vice-
Minister of Health.  Such high level representation reinforces the importance
placed by the two ministries on coordination.
*  the Project Steering Committee, which will provide advice and guidance on policy
and major project issues, will have a representative from MOH and
*  the key position of Vice-Director of the PMU will be filled by an MOH official,
who will facilitate coordination in the day-to-day execution of the project.
In addition, throughout the preparation of both the Population and Family Health Project
and the National Health Support Project (to be implemented by MOH) several
collaborative activities took place as follows:
o Population and Family Health project planning meetings which were held at the
national, provincial and district levels included participants from both ministries;
- a number of joint meetings were held with representation from NCPFP, MOH and
World Bank missions.
The design of the matching provincial service delivery components of the two projects
reflects this collaborative process.   Close cooperation between the two Project
Management Units is expected to continue during implementation.
B. PROJECT ACCOUNTS AND AUDITING
4.6   The total project accounts will be maintained by the PMU in NCPFP. Separate
accounts for appropriate project components and activities will also be maintained by the
local government of each project province. All will be kept in accordance with sound
accounting practices. The project accounts will reflect all financial transactions during the
project implementation period for both IDA credit and counterpart funding in two books,
one by project components and the other by expenditure categories. The project account
will be separated from all routine budget accounts and other foreign-financed project
accounts.



- 47 -
4.7   Project accounts will be audited in accordance with the Guidelines for Financial
Reporting and Auditing of Projects Financed by the World Bank (The World Bank, March
1982). During negotiations, Government provided asssurances that audits of
project-related records and accounts, including the Special Account, will be
undertaken in accordance with appropriate auditing principles consistently applied,
by independent auditors acceptable to IDA, and that audited accounts and financial
statements, in a format agreed with IDA, and including separate audits of
Statements of Expenditures, will be sent to IDA within nine months of the end of
each Government fiscal year [para. 6.1 (a)].
C. PROJECT MONITORING, EVALUATION AND REPORTING
4.8   Project Monitoring and Evaluation. Key project indicators are listed in para.
2.7, while a full matrix of input, intermediate and impact indicators for the project is given
in Annex 4, together with a note of how they would be obtained. Overall, most indicators
of project inputs would be obtained through either the project MIS or the national FP
program MIS, while most indicators of intermediate and ultimate impact would come from
special population-based surveys.
4.9   Three major surveys will be carried out under the project for the purposes of
project evaluation, namely the baseline survey, the mid-term review survey, and the final
evaluative survey (see Annex 14). The baseline survey will be a population-based sample
survey, carried out at the beginning of the project. It will assess the FP and family health
status of the population. Information will be gathered on levels, trends and patterns of:
fertility; FP knowledge, attitudes and use; childhood morbidity and mortality; reproductive
health; childbearing and childfeeding practices; and access to and use of major PHC
services such as maternal care, immunization and micronutrient supplementation. The
questionnaire and the plan of tabulation and analysis will be structured around the matrix
of project indicators, with particular emphasis on the ten key indicators.
4.10  The baseline survey will be scaled to yield significant results for both the country
as a whole and appropriate groupings of provinces; in addition, the 15 project provinces
will be oversampled to, provide key measures of PHC service access and utilization for
individual provinces. As an addendum to the baseline survey, similar smaller surveys will
be conducted among the most numerous ethnic minority groups. These will include
qualitative focus-group studies to elicit more in-depth information on attitudes,
preferences and key health and FP issues in a consultative and participative framework.
4.11  The results of 1he mid-term review survey will form one of the inputs into the
Mid-Term Review (see paras. 4.15-4.17), and fieldwork and analysis would be scheduled
accordingly. The survey will consist of a repeat of the baseline survey, but with the
questionnaire limited to a few core topics. These would be chosen either to monitor
background trends in key indicators or to assess project impact in those areas where



- 48 -
significant progress could be expected by the project midpoint. Again, results would be
disaggregated to national, provincial grouping, individual project province and individual
major ethnic group levels. Fieldwork and analysis would be designed to yield quick and
robust results, rather than an in-depth exploration of the data.
4.12  The final evaluative survey will be carried out just before project completion. It
will be a full repeat of the baseline survey. A comprehensive in-depth analysis of its results
will be produced, making full use of the data from the two earlier surveys for comparison.
This analysis would assess trends in health and FP status and services over the life of the
project and the contribution of the project to them. The fieldwork and analysis would be
scheduled to allow the results to be used in the Implementation Completion Report (ICR).
The survey report and the ICR will be fully reviewed and discussed by Government, IDA
and project co-financiers at a Project Completion workshop.  During negotiations,
Government provided assurances that an Implementation Completion Report,
based in part on the results of the final evaluative surveys and studies, will be
provided and discussed with IDA within six months after the closing date of the
project [para. 6.1 (p)].
4.13  In addition to these three major surveys, smaller special surveys would be carried
out as required, in such areas as IEC or training assessments. Some of these are described
in Annexes 5, 6, 11 and 14.
4.14 The NCPFP PMU will be responsible for the management of all monitoring and
evaluative activities under the project. Surveys and special studies would be contracted
out as required to other Government agencies with special expertise in the necessary
fields, such as the General Statistical Office (GSO) and the Centre for Population and
Human Resource Studies in MOLISA.
4.15  Project Reporting. The PMU will provide six-monthly project progress reports
to IDA and co-financiers according to the format given in Annex 14. This format is based
on the matrix of project indicators shown in Annex 4. The focus would normally be on
routine project monitoring and hence on input indicators from the project MIS. Results of
major or smaller surveys would be summarized in these progress reports, but also written
up in full separate reports. In addition, the PMU will produce a report on the results of the
Mid-Term Review and an Implementation Completion Report. During negotiations,
Government provided assurances that six-monthly project progress reports will be
submitted to IDA by June 30 and December 31 of each year, beginning December
1996, substantially in accordance with the format laid out in Annex 14 (para. 6.1
(b)). Government also provided assurances that an annual review of the project
work program and budget for the next fiscal year will be provided to IDA for review
and comments by August 31 of each year, beginning 1996, and the work program
will be subsequently implemented in a manner satisfactory to IDA [para. 6.1 (fl).



- 49 -
4.16  The Mid-Term Review. At the mid-point of the project (approximately end-
1999), a Mid-Term Review will be carried out by the PMU according to the outline given
in Annex 14. The purpose of this Review would be to assess overall progress in the
project up to its mid-point, and hence the prospects for meeting the original objectives of
the project in full, on time, and within the original budgetary envelope of the project. IDA
and the project co-financiers will then review the findings of the Review with Government.
On that basis, joint decisions will be made on any major changes or restructuring in the
project that appear necessary.
4.17  The Mid-Term Review will consist of three parts. For the first part, the PMU
would conduct a comprehensive internal stocktaking of progress in project
implementation. This would be based largely upon the project MIS. The second part will
be an external review, by another Government agency or a selected private consultant, of
the progress of the project in meeting its objectives. This would be based partly on the
results of the internal stocktaking and partly on data from the mid-term review survey
described above. For the third part, the PMI will synthesize the first two parts into an
overall assessment of project status. This assessment would include recommendations
for changes, restructuring or remedial action in the remainder of the project. It will be
embodied in a Mid-Term Review report, which will be submitted to IDA and the project
co-financers early in the year 2000 for review and discussion at a Mid-Term Review
workshop. During negotiations, Government provided assurances that a project
midterm review report substantially in accordance with the outline given in Annex
14 will be provided to IDA by August 31, 1999 and reviewed with 1DA by December
31, 1999 [para. 6.1 (e)I.
D. PROJECT IMPACT ON SOCIAL ISSUES
Impact on Ethnic Minorities
4.18  According to the 1989 census, the population of Viet Nam was made up of 87
percent Kinh (ethnic Vietnamese) and of 13 percent ethnic minorities, who belonged to
more than 50 different ethnic groups with distinct cultures. These ethnic minorities vary
enormously in size; the two largest groups, Tay and Thai, have populations of over one
million each, while some of the smallest are numbered only in the hundreds. They are
found in all regions and provinces of Viet Nam, ranging from less than one percent to 96
percent of provincial populations in 1989 (see Annex 15). Subdivision of some provinces
since 1989 complicates estimation, but roughly speaking, minority groups make up 50-80
percent of the population in three out of the 15 project provinces, and 15-30 percent in
another six. A few of the minority groups, such as the Han Chinese, are either fully
integrated into mainstream Kinh society or enjoy an equal or superior standard of living.
However most are relatively disadvantaged. They mainly inhabit the mountainous, forested
and remote parts of the country, often living in small and scattered settlements to which
access is extremely difficult.



- 50 -
4.19 Government has implemented several policies and programs aimed at improving
the standard of living and meeting the special needs of these disadvantaged groups. In the
family planning area, the national "one or two-child" policy has been modified to suit their
minority status by allowing three children to couples belonging to mountain-dwelling
minority groups. In health, special programs have been set up to try to improve the supply
of health staff for minority communities, including special scholarships for minority girls
and boys wishing to become doctors, nurses or midwives. However it is acknowledged
that health and FP services still remain extremely deficient in many minority areas.
Consistent with the objectives of Operational Directive (OD) 4.20 on the protection of the
interests of indigenous peoples and ethnic minorities, the project will therefore include
special strategies to ensure equity in health and FP status between minority groups and
their Kinh neighbors (see Annex 15).
4.20   As a preparatory step, two preliminary studies of ethnic minorities were
undertaken during project preparation. The first was a desk study which collated available
statistics and general information on ethnic minorities in Viet Nam. The second was a
participative field study carried out by an anthropologist with long experience in the field
in Thailand and Viet Nam, including one of the project provinces. It collected original data
in a second project province through quick field surveys of health and FP status and access
to services, and also held participative discussions with minority communities on their
needs and preferences in family planning and family health. In addition, this study
compiled the scanty information available on these topics for the country as a whole.
4.21   Available demographic data indicate that all of Viet Nam's minority groups except
the Han lag behind the Kinh in terms of the demographic transition to low levels of fertility
and mortality. According to the 1989 census, all the nine most numerous groups excepting
the Han had TFRs above 4.0, with most above 5.0. By contrast, the Kinh TFR was
estimated at 3.6 and the Han TFR at only 2.9. Similarly, all nine groups had IMRs higher
than the estimated Kinh level of 39, except for the Han at 31; the range was from 41 to as
high as 106, with most minority IMRs well above 50. Available socio-economic data show
higher levels of poverty and illiteracy lying behind these demographic disadvantages.
According to the 1993 VLSS, average annual expenditure, a proxy for income, was much
lower for all minority groups (except the Han) than for the Kinh; typically expenditure was
more than a third lower and sometimes almost two thirds lower. According to the 1989
census, literacy levels among the population aged 5 years and over were lower among all
ethnic minorities, even the Han, than among the Kinh, of whom 87 percent were literate.
There was however a considerable variation among minority groups; some had literacy
rates of 80 to 83 percent, while others had rates as low as 10-20 percent.
4.22  The findings of these two preliminary studies also indicate that among at least
some ethnic minorities, demand for PHC and modern FP services is quite as high as
among the Kinh. There may be a greater preference for temporary and short-term
contraceptive methods over permanent or long-term methods, but few other cultural



- 51 -
features have been found which would require adapting of regular FP and PHC services to
suit local cultural attitudes and practices. The major disadvantages of these groups appear
to arise from lack of access to such services, because of the difficult and remote terrain in
which they tend to live.
4.23   Special strategies are needed under the project in order to improve access and
quality of services among ethnic minorities up to the standard of their Kinh neighbors. The
first requirement is a better data base. Baseline surveys will therefore be carried out early
in the project among the most numerous ethnic minority groups in Viet Nam. These
would assess current health and FP status and access to services, relative to neighboring
Kinh communities. On this basis, a full set of strategies would be designed to remedy any
disadvantages found. Annex 14 gives an outline of the scope and methodology which will
be followed for these surveys.
4.24  It is already clear that the major focus of such strategies overall will be the
improvement of access to good-quality family health services, with emphasis on extension
of outreach in remote mountainous areas. As already described in paras. 2.15 and 2.25, a
new system of village-based health posts would be piloted in these areas under Component
5 and then expanded to cover all such areas in the 15 project provinces under Component
1, if successful. In addition, under the accompanying pilot of an upgraded collaborator
system providing a range of family health services, the new Family Health Workers in
remote mountainous areas would be furnished with bicycles; additional transport would be
provided for DHC mobile teams serving remote mountainous and delta areas; and special
programs of recruitment and training of paramedical staff belonging to ethnic minorities
would be developed and implemented. Other possible approaches to improving access to
services might include special efforts to train traditional midwives to supervise home
deliveries safely, and special incentive programs for private or NGO health practitioners or
pharmacies willing to settle in or tour remote areas. Baseline studies for the 15 provinces,
the formulation of appropriate special strategies, and the piloting of selected approaches
will all be funded under Component 5 (paras. 2.24-2.25); extension of the health post and
collaborator pilots (if successful), plus DHC mobile team transport and special training
programs, would be funded under Component I (para. 2.15).
4.25   At national level, all components focusing on family planning will support a
widening of the method mix and a new emphasis on temporary and short-term methods.
Hence preferences for such methods among minority groups should automatically be
catered for under the project. However, a special national FP strategy for ethnic minorities
will also be formulated. It will aim to improve access to FP services and adapt IEC
materials and activities, including training in counseling, as needed (see Annex 15).
4.26  In order to monitor and evaluate the implementation and impact of these special
strategies for disadvantaged minorities, special follow-up surveys will be carried out for
the most numerous minority groups (see Annex 14). During negotiations, Government



- 52 -
provided assurances that the project will be carried out in accordance with a
strategy satisfactory to IDA, which will: (a) be designed on the basis of studies and
surveys undertaken by qualified and experienced experts under terms of reference
and timing satisfactory to IDA; and (b) include provisions for the informed
participation of and consultation with a variety of representative communities
benefiting under the project, in particular communities with ethnic minorities, to
ensure that the design and implementation of the project adequately addresses the
needs and cultural preferences of such communities [para. 6.1 (o)].
Impact on Women
4.27  The proposed project is intended to support strengthening of primary health care
and family planning programs in Viet Nam, with a focus on improving maternal, family
and reproductive health services. As a result, women will benefit from better access to
higher-quality services offering a wider range of contraceptive methods, improved care in
pregnancy and delivery, and better treatment of reproductive tract infections. As a result,
the need to resort to abortion should decline and the number of unwanted or mistimed
births should be reduced. Women would therefore be able to care for their children and
families more effectively, as well as to increase their income-generating activities and their
participation in development. The impact of the project on women is thus expected to be
very favorable.
E. ENVIRONMENTAL ASPECTS OF THE PROJECT
4.28  Impact on the Environment. The project will have no effect on the environment.
Protocols will be developed for the safe disposal of syringes at all health facilities covered
under Component I of the project, and their implementation will be monitored as part of
project supervision.
4.29  Resettlement Issues. There are no resettlement issues.
F. PROJECT SUSTAINABILITY
Financial Sustainability
4.30  Provincial PHC Services. Component 1 of the project will deliver a package of
inputs to upgrade PHC service delivery in 15 provinces. The inputs are civil works,
equipment and furniture, essential drugs and medical supplies and refresher training;
outreach systems might also be strengthened but this is not certain and hence will not be
taken into account here. The incremental recurrent cost burden to be sustained after
project implementation will comprise the maintenance of the upgraded civil works and
equipment (which would be shared between the health and population/FP budgets), and
annual drug and medical supplies plus logistics costs. These costs will be met out of the



- 53 -
regular health budget and would amount to approximately US$2.7 million per annum by
2002, or well under one percent of the projected health budget for 2002 (see Annex 16 for
details). Incremental costs on this scale should not by themselves be difficult to sustain.
4.31   The National Health Support Project will provide a similar upgrading package
over the same period to another 15 provinces, raising total coverage to more than half the
country's population. However this would still only raise incremental recurrent costs for
the 30 provinces together to around US$5.4 million or less than 1.5 percent of the national
health budget. Taking a broader perspective, if the provincial upgrading package should
succeed in improving service quality and utilization, Government might consider a similar
upgrading program in the country's remaining provinces. At most, however, the recurrent
cost burden from a similar investment program in the rest of the country would about
double that generated by the two projects, to consume altogether no more than 3 percent
or so of the national health budget. It does not appear, therefore, that the upgrading of
Viet Nam's PHC services could create any major problem of sustainability.
4.32   Family Planning Program. Under the project, the contraceptive prevalence rate
for modern methods is projected to rise by about 10 percentage points. Numbers of users
will rise proportionately even more, since the population of reproductive age is also
increasing. At the same time, the contraceptive method mix is projected to broaden and
become more commodity-intensive. Thus total national costs for contraceptive
commodities plus logistics costs (excluding condoms) are projected to rise from a current
level of around US$3 million to around US$7.5 million by 2002. The increase of about
US$ 4.5 million per year may be viewed as the recurrent cost burden generated overall by
the project's activities, even though only a minor proportion of these contraceptive costs
would be financed directly by the project by 2002. This increase plus the population/FP
budget's share of maintenance costs would be equivalent to about 8.5 percent of the
projected population/FP budget for 2002, a significant but sustainable increment.
4.33   Taking a wider perspective, however, the annual total contraceptive bill of US$7.5
million is the amount that would need to be sustained after the closure of the project,
equivalent to about 13 percent to the projected total budget for population and family
planning in 2002 (see Annex 16 for details). This scale of resource allocation for
contraceptive supplies is not excessive by the standards of family planning programs
worldwide. It should therefore be sustainable by the Government of Viet Nam even if all
these supplies were to be financed by Government itself and provided free of charge
through public programs. In fact, a proportion of users already obtain their contraceptive
supplies from the private sector and the importance of both the private sector and social
marketing is likely to grow substantially during the life of the project. A program of
charges for public program users is also planned to be introduced during the second half of
the project. It is moreover very likely that donor support to contraceptive supplies would
continue to be significant after the year 2000, outside the umbrella of the project itself



- 54 -
The sustainability of the expanded contraceptive costs should therefore present no major
problems.
Institutional Sustainability
4.34  The project has been designed to avoid any serious problems of institutional
sustainability after project closure. No permanent salaried staff have been added to either
provincial health services or the FP program, and no major changes are to be made in their
organizational structure or their tasks. Though some new approaches are to be tested
under the project, such as health posts and strengthened outreach systems, they will be
evaluated thoroughly for viability and sustainability before being expanded. The project
should result in increased health and FP workloads, due to the planned upgrading of
facilities, services and IEC, but the network of facilities and staff is currently so dense that
their capacity to accommodate such increases is expected to be ample. There will be a
massive temporary increase also in the IEC program, but the incremental activities are
designed primarily to support the restructuring and upgrading of the FP program under the
project itself Therefore, the level of IEC activities and expenditure would revert to the
pre-project norm  after project closure.  Other elements of the project, such as
management training and MIS upgrading, would improve the efficiency of program
management and administration.  No major issues of institutional sustainability are
therefore anticipated.



- 55 -
5. BENEFITS AND RISKS
A. BENEFITS
5.1   There will be a range of overlapping benefits from the project. First, at the
national level, the proposed strengthening of Viet Nam's family planning program should
result in improved information, education and communication about family planning,
better quality of family planning services, and a wider choice of methods for couples
including those best suited to temporary use and for the purposes of birth spacing. In turn,
these improvements should lead to greater demand for family planning and to more
effective and prolonged use of contraception; hence to a reduction in the incidence of
abortion, improved birth spacing and timing practices, smaller family sizes, lower fertility
and reduced population growth. An average seven million contracepting couples would
annually be beneficiaries of this strengthened FP program.
5.2   The benefits that would flow individually to women, children and famnilies would
be improvements in maternal, child and reproductive health and in family welfare. The
additional benefits that would flow to the entire economy and society of Viet Nam,
comprising more than 80 million people by 2002, would include the reduction of long-
term pressure on agricultural and forest land, on other natural resources, and on state and
family budgets for the social services required for the care and education of child
dependents.
5.3   Second, for the 15 project provinces where primary health care services would
also be strengthened under the project, there will be additional benefits related to these
interventions. As a result of the improvements in working conditions resulting from
upgraded premises and adequate supplies of equipment and drugs, combined with
improved knowledge and skills resulting from refresher training, primary health providers
in these provinces should be enabled and motivated to deliver good-quality primary health
services to the rural population. These would focus on maternal, child and reproductive
health care (including family planning) and also provide treatment for major infectious
diseases and minor ailments combined with screening and referral for more serious
conditions.
5.4   This should lead to a number of health benefits to communities in these provinces,
including reductions in morbidity and mortality from the communicable diseases of
childhood, diarrheal and respiratory diseases, malaria, TB, reproductive tract infections,
and conditions related to complications of pregnancy and delivery for mothers and to
birth for infants. The entire population of the 15 project provinces, amounting currently to
over 20 million or 28% of the total population of Viet Nam, would benefit from access to
improved PHC services. Beneficiaries of the core PHC interventions, covering maternal
and young child care plus FP, would amount to an average 5.4 million annually, made up
of around 740,000 women requiring maternal care, around 1.7 million children under three



- 56 -
years of age and around 3.0 million contraceptors. These benefits should be particularly
important to the poorest sections of rural communities, since they are the least able to
purchase adequate replacement services in the private market. In addition, if the package
of inputs provided by the project in these provinces (and likewise by the National Health
Support Project in 15 other provinces) is successful in revitalizing their PHC systems, this
should provide a powerful stimulus and model for similar strengthening in the remaining
half of the country.  The project's studies and pilots of the private sector (plus
complementary policy studies under the National Health Support Project) would also lead
to a better knowledge base for action to regulate and improve private sector services.
5.5   Without the project (and the accompanying National Health Support Project)
Government is highly unlikely to be able to mobilize sufficient domestic or external
funding for major upgrading of the FP program (including social marketing) or of primary
health care in the provinces. Without such investment, the FP program would probably
stagnate at its current level of modern method coverage and with its current method-mix.
Through lack of choice and information, couples would increasingly resort to ineffective
traditional methods. As a result, fertility decline would be slower and abortion rates
would remain undesirably high.
5.6   Without major investment, the PHC system is likely to deteriorate still further to a
point where even the major preventive programs decline in coverage and effectiveness.
Since no effective private sector alternatives have developed for these types of health
interventions, there will be a risk of resurgence of communicable disease epidemics and a
stagnation or rise in child and maternal death rates. Such trends have already been
observed in other countries or population groups where PHC systems have deteriorated.
At the same time, curative care would continue to be dominated by self-medication from
pharmacies of unknown quality and effectiveness and to a lesser extent by largely
unregulated private providers. In such conditions, clients are unlikely to obtain the most
cost-effective medical care for their illnesses, and rates of morbidity and mortality could
not be minimized.
B. RISKS
5.7   Technically, the packages of family planning and PHC interventions to be delivered
under the project present little risk. The technologies and techniques for providing
effective and good-quality FP and PHC programs and services are well-understood and
well-tested around the world. Viet Nam's experience in both sectors is long and generally
successful, demonstrating unquestionable ability to implement efficiently and sustained
commitment to the goals of better health and reduction of fertility and population growth.
The risks of the project relate more to the rapidly-evolving social and economic context of
Viet Nam and the ability of the mature FP and PHC programs to change, as well as to the
country's inexperience with IDA projects and procedures.



- 57 -
5.8   For the FP program, the major overall risk is that despite high-level NCPFP
commitment and the fresh inputs and strategic directions supported by the project, the
expected broadening of the method mix and shift towards a client-oriented approach may
not be achieved as rapidly as projected. One constraint might be the recognized difficulty
in changing attitudes and practices among entrenched program managers and health
providers. It might also prove harder than expected to correct current client
misconceptions and shift client preferences towards less familiar methods. These risks will
be minimized within the project by: intensive IEC activities; extensive training in
appropriate counseling for all providers and motivating staff, management training and
inputs for the CPFP network to promote fresh attitudes and thinking; and the use of the
results of studies and pilot interventions financed under the service delivery model
initiatives to support strategic rethinking and adoption of innovative approaches.
5.9   For the provincial strengthening of primary health, the major risk is related to
the project's basic assumption that the utilization rates of public health facilities have fallen
primarily because of poor service quality arising from resource constraints. It is possible,
however, that public confidence in the state PHC system may not be retrievable at this
point even if services are dramatically upgraded. Thus utilization rates might not recover
despite the project's investments. If the private health provider sector develops sufficiently
fast and effectively to provide a viable alternative to either the state system or self-
medication from pharmacies, a further dimension to this risk would be added. However
experience elsewhere has shown that state PHC systems continue to be attractive,
particularly to the very poor, if access and quality of services can be assured. The project
would minimize this risk by providing the complete package of inputs needed to achieve
the required levels of access and quality.
5.10  Finally, there is a general risk that project implementation may prove to be slow
and unsatisfactory, particularly in the area of civil works, because of Viet Nam's
inexperience with IDA and ADB project procedures, coupled with the limitations of
NCPFP management capabilities. This risk would be minimized by the management
training and support to be provided to NCPFP and the subsidiary CPFP network within
the project, and by a special civil works monitoring unit in the NCPFP PMU. Extensive
training in IDA and ADB procedures is also planned prior to project implementation. The
positive experience of NCPFP's use of the project's PHRD fund (see para. 1.54),
combined with Viet Nam's successful management of PHC and FP programs in the past,
despite adverse conditions, suggests that this risk is not unduly high.



- 58 -

RECOMMENDATION

(c)   the key indicators and objectives listed in para. 2.6-2.7 and the matrix of
indicators shown in Annex 4 will be used to measure, monitor and evaluate
implementation progress and project impact (para. 2.7);
(d)   baseline surveys and studies substantially in accordance with the outline
given in Annex 14 will be carried out, and their results used to complete
the list of key project objectives as described in para. 2.7, by June 30, 1997
(para. 2.7);
(e)   a project midterm review report substantially in accordance with the outline
given in Annex 14 will be provided to IDA by August 31, 1999 and
reviewed with IDA by December 31, 1999 (para. 4.17);
(f)   the project work program and budget for the next fiscal year will be
provided to IDA for review and comments by August 31 of each year,
beginning 1996, and the work program will be subsequently implemented
in a manner satisfactory to IDA (para. 4.15);
(g)   all CHCs, DHCs and MCH/FP centers in the 15 provinces will be
appropriately staffed and all CHCs will be provided with adequate funding
to pay staff salaries (para 2.12);
(h)   the selection of CHCs to receive facility upgrading will be carried out
according to criteria agreed with IDA and substantially in accordance with
those laid out in Annex 5 (para. 2.12);



- 59 -
(i)   all CHCs, DHCs and MCH/FP facilities upgraded under the project will be
adequately maintained during project implementation according to the
guidelines specified in Annex 5 (para. 2.13);
(j)   (a) a pilot scheme of charges for contraceptives will be carried out by June
30, 1999; (b) an action plan will be prepared on the basis of its results by
August 31, 1999 for review and comment by IDA; and (c) the action plan
will thereafter be promptly carried out (para. 2.25);
(k)   detailed proposals for all activities to be financed under the service delivery
model initiatives component will be submitted, through a Service Delivery
Model Initiatives Committee to be established by August 31, 1996 and
described in Annex 10, to IDA for prior review and approval, and
thereafter will be implemented as approved by IDA (para. 2.24);
(I)   in the event that the pilot test of a strengthened community-based outreach
system for family planning and family health described in Annex 11 is
evaluated as successful, an action plan for its implementation will be
submitted to IDA for approval, and then will be implemented as approved
in the project provinces (para. 2.15);

established, staffed and maintained throughout the project implementation
period (para. 4.1);
(n)   all necessary steps will be taken to ensure that each of the project provinces
implements its activities under the project and is provided, promptly as
needed, the funds, facilities, services and other resources required for that
purpose (para. 4.4);
(o)   the project will be carried out in accordance with a strategy satisfactory to
IDA, which will: (a) be designed on the basis of studies and surveys
undertaken by qualified and experienced experts under terms of reference
and timing satisfactory to IDA; and (b) include provisions for the informed
participation of and consultation with a variety of representative
communities benefiting under the project, in particular communities with
ethnic minorities, to ensure that the design and implementation of the
project adequately addresses the needs and cultural preferences of such
communities (para 4.26);
(p)   an Implementation Completion Report, based in part on the results of the
final evaluative surveys and studies, will be provided and discussed with
IDA within six months after the closing date of the project (para. 4.12);



- 60 -
(q)   a time-bound action plan for all training activities under the project will be
provided to IDA for review and comments by June 30, 1996 (para. 2.26);
and
(r)    Government would furnish IDA by September 30, 1996, and have an
exchange of views with IDA on, a comprehensive description of the policy
and implementation guidelines it will employ to ensure the effective
implementation of: (i) cost recovery with respect to the esssential drugs to
be provided by the project in those communes of project provinces where
applicable; and (ii) provision of essential drugs and supplies free of change
in mountainous and remote communes in project provinces. The policy
and implementation guidelines would be reviewed by the Government and
IDA each year thereafter and would be an explicit focus of the mid-term
review (para. 2.12).
6.2   As a condition of effectiveness, Government will provide evidence satisfactory to
IDA that: (a) the Project Steering Committee, the Project Executive Committee and the
NCPFP Project Management Unit have been established and the Project Director
appointed; and (b) the Provincial Project Management Units in the five provinces included
in the first phase of Component I have been established and the corresponding Provincial
Project Managers appointed (para. 4.1).
6.3    As a condition of disbursement for allowances for outreach workers under the
strengthened collaborator system, an action plan, satisfactory to IDA, for implementing
the strengthened system on the basis of the results of the pilot test will be provided to IDA
(para. 3.20).
6.4   Subject to the conditions in paras. 6.1, 6.2 and 6.3 above, the proposed project
constitutes a suitable basis for an IDA Credit of SDR 33.6 million (US$50 million
equivalent) to the Socialist Republic of Viet Nam with 40 years of maturity, and 10 years
of grace.



- 61 -                       Annex I
THE PRIMARY HEALTH CARE SYSTEM IN VIET NAM
Health Services
1.           Health services in Viet Nam are organized on a four-tiered system. At the
apex is the Ministry of Health (MOH), and below are the provincial, district and commune
level health services.
Central Level
2.           The MOH is the main national authority in the health sector and directly
manages faculty schools, national production units of drugs and medical equipment, and
all centrally-based hospitals (19% of all hospital beds and 7% of all health/medical
workers). The MOH, at the central level, is responsible for setting policy and technical
directions for the entire country.
3.           The MOH is organized into 13 functional departments responsible for
different activities such as training, family planning, maternal and child health, equipment
and construction, and finance, planning and policy. The MOH is assisted in its activities by
a number of central specialty institutes which function as tertiary care referral centers such
as the Institute of Hygiene and Epidemiology, Institute for the Protection of Mothers and
Newborn, the National Institute of Nutrition, and a number of other institutes responsible
for research, training and patient care in the areas of pediatrics, tuberculosis, pharmacy
and traditional medicine. In addition to the MOH, there are a number of ministries which
provide parallel health services (e.g. Ministry of Defense).
4.           Over the years, Viet Nam has also developed a number of self-contained
vertical health programs, including malaria, TB and ARI control, and CDD, with
structures running from the central to the district level. These structures, though useful in
focusing attention and resources on important public health programs, are proving
inefficient in terms of duplication of resource allocation. Given the complex structure and
overlapping functions at the central level, there is a broad recognition of the need to
streamline the overall management of health services, as well as coordinate and integrate
the vertical programs for better cost efficiency.
Provincial Level
5.           The next level is the provincial health services which is responsible for the
center for preventive medicine stations, the local production of medical supplies, and
referral laboratory facilities. The provinces also run provincial hospitals and in some
cases, specialized hospitals (30% of all hospital beds). The provincial general hospitals are
intended to be referral centers and typically have all seven departments (internal medicine,



- 62 -                       Annex I
obstetrics and gynecology, surgery, pediatrics, infectious diseases, traditional medicine and
emergency ward). Manpower at the provincial level is 35% of the nation's total.
However, in practice, few of the patients cared for at these hospitals come from outer
communities and the vast majority reside in the general vicinity of the hospitals. The
hospitals therefore serve basic curative needs which could be effectively attended to at
lower levels of the health care delivery system.
6.           One other important component of the provincial health services are the
secondary medical schools. These schools train middle level health workers for their
provinces, such as assistant doctors, secondary midwives, nurses and secondary
pharmacists.
District Level
7.           The third level is the district level which is in charge of all medical facilities
that are intermediaries between the provincial and commune levels. The three components
of the district health services are the District Health Office which has surveillance and
program management responsibilities; the preventive medicine services dealing with EPI,
CDD, malaria control, vitamin A deficiency etc.; and the District Hospital which provides
first referral facilities to the commune level. The latter two components are now merged
into the District Health Centers which deal with both preventive and curative health
services.
8.           The district hospitals (18%  of all hospital beds and over 30%  of all
health/medical staff) have excess bed capacity because, in the past, financial allocations
were made based mainly on the number of beds they had. As a result, bed occupancy
rates average only 50%.
9.           Also, though most district health resources (manpower, finances, drugs,
equipment etc.) are concentrated in the district hospitals, the support these hospitals
provide to the lower levels vary in both quality and quantity. There is therefore an urgent
need to improve the quality of services and focus the range of services provided at this
level in order to facilitate its functioning as a first referral facility.
10.          There is also a hybrid facility which overlaps several communes known as
the inter-commune polyclinic (ICP). The ICPs (5% of all hospital beds) were envisaged as
centers to provide basic services at a higher level of sophistication (with some specialties
such as ophthalmology and dentistry), take referrals from CHCs and give supervision and
other technical services, including training, to CHCs. However, the ICPs do not appear to
be fulfilling this role, and in practice function as either CHCs or DHCs depending on their
capacity. The Government is currently initiating discussions on the future role of the
ICPs.



- 63 -                      Annex I
Commune Level
11.         At the commune level, the unit of service delivery is the commune health
center (CHC). Viet Nam has developed a wide network of commune health centers at the
grassroots level and CHCs account for 28% of all beds and 22% of health/medical staff in
the country. 93% of all communes have a CHC and the remaining communes (known as
'White communes') are to be provided with CHCs over the next three years. However,
observations indicate that much work needs to be done on the quality of available CHC
infrastructure. Many of these facilities are run-down and lack the most basic equipment
and drugs. Also, though in theory the CHCs are to be staffed by 3 to 5 health workers, in
practice over 60% of them lack even a secondary midwife.
12.         Each commune consists of a cluster of hamlets or villages, which is the
basic social unit in the country. Unfortunately, an outreach system (the brigade or
primary nurse system) which was in existence during the years of the war, has collapsed
and there is therefore no household level primary health system in Viet Nam.
Private Health Sector and its role in Primary Health Service Provision
13.         It is important to note the rising presence of the private sector in the
provision of health services in Viet Nam. The government has permitted private practice
since 1988, and this has resulted in the emergence of numerous pharmacies, and to a lesser
extent clinics, in both urban and semi-urban areas. A major issue facing private practice is
regulation to (a) ensure that standards of practice are set and enforced and (b) distinguish
between official and private practice among workers in the public sector.
14.         About 30% of health workers were forced into retirement due to economic
difficulties including demilitarization. Most of these retirees have returned to their
villages, and if well organized could be used to provide community-based health care,
especially in rural areas. It will however be necessary to establish specific role and
responsibilities for them in their communities.

- 65 -                          Annex 2
THE FAMILY PLANNING SYSTEM
1.          The population and family planning program in Viet Nam has a dual
organizational structure which runs in parallel but is interactive and coordinated at the
different levels. The service delivery function is lodged in the MOH, Department of
MCH/FP, and all clinical family planning services are provided through the health system
(see Annex 1).
2.          In 1984, as part of an effort to improve the Government's capacity to
manage and implement the population programs, the Council of Ministers created the
National Committee for Population and Family Planning (NCPFP). In 1989, the status of
NCPFP was further elevated to that of the apex body in advising the Government on
policy formulation and program development. It is also responsible for intersectoral
coordination in the implementation of family planning programs of various ministries,
agencies, levels of government and mass organizations. The Minister of Population and
Family Planning acts as Chairman of the NCPFP. He is aided by three coordinating Vice-
Chairmen who are appointed by the Ministry of Planning and Investment, Ministry of
Finance and Ministry of Health.
3.          The NCPFP network extends downwards and is replicated at the
provincial, district and commune levels. In addition, the NCPFP also has a collaborator
network at the hamlet level which is responsible for outreach work in the field of
population and family planning. In recent years, the CPFP network has been involved in
the distribution of non-clinical contraceptives (condoms, resupply of pills), and it is
estimated that 27% of couples received contraceptives from sources outside the health
force. Also, the collaborator network is the only formal outreach system at the grass-
roots level, and though currently not equipped to provide community-based primary health
care, the collaborators have the potential to be developed into primary health outreach
workers.
