- 67 -                         Annex 3
CRITERIA FOR SELECTION OF PROVINCES FOR PHC UPGRADING
The selection of provinces followed three major principles:
1.          Provinces with unfavorable fertility, family planning and health
indicators would be given priority in coverage. The three fertility and health indicators
taken into consideration were the total fertility rate, infant mortality rate and contraceptive
prevalence rate.
2.          To provide more equitable distribution of funds, provinces with
substantial previous or current donor commitments were excluded, though they
would still participate in those components of the project that are meant to be nationwide
(such as IEC and management development). In effect, the scoring led to the exclusion
of all provinces covered by the UNFPA third cycle, the UNFPA fourth cycle, AusAID-
assisted provinces, GTZ-assisted provinces, as well as those selected for coverage under
the National Health Support Project.
3.          Among the remaining provinces, efforts would be made to cover as much
of the rural population as possible.
In addition to the above principles, consideration was given to the geographical spread
of the provinces, and their management capacity. Viet Nam has seven geographical
regions which differ in terms of various factors such as terrain, ethnic composition and
access to health and family planning services. By choosing project provinces from the
different regions, it would be possible to design and implement a project which reflects the
differing needs of the regions. This criterion therefore allows for replication of the
project, if successful, since the 15 project provinces could serve as models in the specific
regions. The provinces selected to be covered under the project were also judged to have
above-average management capacity. This was considered to be particularly important
given that the Population and Family Health Project is the first of its kind in terms of size
and scope; the success of such a project therefore depends heavily on the management
capacity at both the central as well as provincial levels.



- 68 -                          Annex 4
MATRIX OF PROJECT INDICATORS
A.    THE RATIONALE FOR PROGRAM INDICATORS
1.          There is a need for a consistent set of indicators to monitor and evaluate
the Population and Family Health Project at various times in the project cycle. Within the
broad framework of project aims and objectives, a matrix of specific objectives and
indicators at various levels has been established for the purposes of project monitoring and
impact evaluation.  Indicators cover four broad categories: (1) project inputs; (2)
measures of capacity and process; (3) program outputs; and (4) behavioral and
demographic impacts. The matrix is shown in full in Table 7 and should be integrated into
project reporting formats and management and supervisory arrangements. Minor changes
may be made, as needed, in the matrix during project implementation. Arrangements
should also be made to regularly review these indicators of project performance and to
implement improvements in project design based on the updated information.
2.          Within this matrix, ten key indicators have been selected to measure project
impact and achievement regarding key specific project objectives. These are linked to the
policy principles, strategies and specific project activities, as well as the Government's own
objectives. These indicators correspond to the objectives and management processes of
the Project.  Most of the key indicators measure demographic impacts or program
outputs, because ultimately these are the broad outcomes that we hope to affect over the
duration of the project. These indicators attempt to give some sense of the impact of
project activities on the lives of individual people in the population. They are not intended
to provide step-by-step guidance for the management of programs at the CHC or mobile-
team level. A wider range of indicators (see Table 7) is required for that purpose.
3.           At a minimum, calculation of key indicators should be carried out prior to
the beginning of the project, at mid-term, and following completion of the project.
Current levels of the indicators (where known) and objectives are shown in Table 1.
Adjustment may be required after information from the baseline surveys and baseline
studies becomes available.

Total Fertility Rate (TFR).
4.           Definition:   The TFR measures the average number of children that
would be born to a woman during her lifetime if she replicated the childbearing experience
of all cohorts in a given period.
Background: The TFR directly represents one of the two operational
objectives of the project, to reduce population growth by the year 20002.

i: the five-year age groups of women of reproductive age (15-19 to 45-49); t: a given year.
6.            Data source and data collection:  To be collected in three project
surveys: (1) baseline survey, (2) mid-term review, and (3) final evaluation survey. Results
from these surveys should be compared to other data collection efforts, such as population
censuses, inter-censal demographic surveys, and other sources.
7.            Coverage of data collection: The whole country.
8.            Disaggregation:  Key indicator will be based on national-level data.
However, the following should also be calculated: (1) province-level TFRs (2) urban/rural
TFRs, and (3) TFRs for major ethnic minority groups.
9.            Frequency of Data Collection:   Three data collection efforts are
required: at baseline, mid-term, and at completion of the project.



- 73 -                           Annex 4
Modern-Method Contraceptive Prevalence Rate (CPR-MM)
10.          Definition: The CPR-MM measures the percentage of women of child-
bearing age (15 to 49) who are using, or whose partners are using, any form of modem
contraception.
11.          Background:  Improvements in this indicator, which is sensitive to
program activities, imply that the birth rate and population growth rate will be reduced. It
also measures the effectiveness of project activities, including the acquisition and
disbursement of modem methods of contraception.
12.          Method of Calculation:
No. of women aged 15-49 who are currently
contracepting (or whose partners are contracepting) using
a modem form of contraception in a given year  * 100
Number of women aged 15-49 in the same year
Where,
Modern forms of contraception are defined as IUDs (copper-t or multi-load), female or
male sterilization, contraceptive pills, injectables, Norplant implants, condoms, and
diaphragms/jelly/spermicide.
13.          Data source and data collection:  To be collected in three project
surveys: (1) baseline survey, (2) mid-term review, and (3) final evaluation survey. Data
collection efforts should concentrate on gathering information on current use of
contraception (though data on ever-use is also useful). Results from these surveys should
be compared to other data collection efforts, such as population censuses, inter-censal
demographic surveys, and other sources, although project evaluation will be based on data
collected in the project surveys and special studies.
14.          Coverage of data collection: The whole country.
15.          Disaggregation:  Key indicator will be based on national-level data.
However, the following should also be calculated: (1) province-level CPR-MMs (2)
urban/rural CPR-MMs and (3) CPR-MMs for major ethnic minority groups.
16.          Frequency of Data Collection: Three data collection efforts are required:
at baseline, mid-term, and at completion of the project.

- 75 -                            Annex 4
Access to Family Planning Services.
24.          Definition: the percentage of women of reproductive age with easy access
to family planning methods.
25.          Background: access to family planning services can be measured directly
by distance to fixed facilities or travel time to them. To be accessible, the following
criteria must be met: the women must have access to at least one of the following: (1) a
community distributor of family planning services; (2) a family planning field worker in the
rural village or urban neighborhood, with visitation at least once every two weeks, or (3) a
fixed service point not more than one kilometer away.
26.          Method of calculation
% of women of No. of women of reproductive age who have access to
RA who have  =    family planning services as defined in given year    * 100
access to FP        Total # of women of reproductive age ( 1549)
in the same yr
27.           Data source and data collection: Data for this indicator will be collected
in three project surveys: (1) a baseline survey, (2) mid-term review, and (3) final
evaluation survey. The data will be based on standard questions on whether or not
specific family planning services are available to women of reproductive age. If services
are available, the women will be asked a series of additional questions in a service delivery
module. Issues to be addressed include the source (including name) of closest supplier of
family planning services and supplies, proximity of service provider to questionnaire
respondent, and the frequency of visitation by mobile team members and whether mobile
team visits are predictable or ad-hoc. Questions should also address whether women have
access to services that are better than the closest supplier. Service availability modules
that have been developed by Macro International, Inc. for the Demographic and Health
Surveys (see the questionnaire for Namibia, for instance) could provide a useful starting
point in the design of this component of the Vietnam surveys.
The usefulness of this indicator can be greatly enhanced by combining
women's responses from the survey with facility records. For instance, for women who
report that a CHC is easily accessible, records from this facility could also be examined.
Useful records would include: a listing of contraceptives that were available in the facility
by week (or alternatively, a listing of stock-outs); a listing of medical personnel that could
prescribe various contraceptives by week; listing of contraceptives actually prescribed; etc.



- 76 -                             Annex 4
28.           Coverage of data collection: The whole country.
29.           Disaggregation:  The key indicator will be reported for province-level
data, with reporting to the national level. Calculations for lower levels (districts) may
provide important information about where to enhance mobile team efforts. Urban/rural
indicators, as well as indicators for major ethnic minority groups, should also be
calculated.
30.           Frequency of data collection: Three data collection efforts are required:
at baseline, mid-term, and at completion of the project.
B.   MATERNAL HEALTH INDICATORS:
Met Need for Life-Saving Obstetric Care (LSOC).
31.           Definition: this indicator measures the proportion of women estimated to
have direct complications that are managed in facilities that can provide the basic services
that are required for obstetric complications
32.           Background: five major conditions are responsible for the majority of
maternal deaths in developing countries world-wide: hemorrhage, sepsis, eclampsia and
hypertensive disorders of pregnancy, obstructed or prolonged labor, and complications
from  abortion.  These conditions are generally unpredictable emergencies for which
adequate emergency care is essential. Even when they can be predicted, the same level of
care will be needed. The provision of such care provides indicators that are more practical
and immediate than estimation of maternal mortality rates or ratios.
33.           Method of Calculation
No. of complicated deliveries (1) in past year that
Met Need   were managed in hospitals or facilities (2) that
for LSOC =
could provide ALL of the services listed in (3) * 100
Estimated number of direct obstetric complications among
pregnant women (4) in the same year.
Simplified to:
No. of complicated deliveries (1) in past year that
Met Need     were managed in hospitals or facilities (2) that
for LSOC =  could provide all of the services listed in (3)  * 100
15% * number of life births in year



- 77 -                            Annex 4
Where, (a) A "complicated case" is a woman who is diagnosed as having any one (or
more) of the following conditions: ante-partum or post-partum hemorrhage, prolonged or
obstructed labor, post-partum sepsis, complications of abortion, pre-eclampsia or
eclampsia, ectopic pregnancy, or ruptured uterus. If the woman has more than one of
these complications, select the most immediately life-threatening one; (b) Managed "in
hospitals or facilities that could provide ..." includes both women admitted with the
complication and women who develop the complication in the facility; (c) Basic services
that MUST be provided are: parenteral antibiotics, oxytocics, and sedatives; manual
removal of the placenta; removal of retained products of conception; and assisted (vaginal)
delivery. This condition assumes that both skilled staff and essential equipments and drugs
are available at the facility at all times. This is a minimum standard. More comprehensive
facilities should be able to provide all of the above services, plus obstetric surgery and
blood transfusions; (d) The denominator is based on two assumptions: that a roughly
constant proportion of pregnancies, around 15 percent, involve major complications and
that most of these require emergency care.
Table A.4.4.
A Listing of Basic Life-Saving Obstetric Services
Parenteral antibiotics
Oxytocics
Sedatives
Manual removal of the placenta
Removal of retained products of conception
Assisted (vaginal) delivery
34.          Data source and data collection:
Numerator: facility records from DHC and higher levels.
Denominator: estimate of live births from demographic survey.
Special studies will need to be carried out in order to calculate this indicator. The studies
will be done at three points in time: (1) as a baseline at the beginning of the project; (2) at
mid-term; and, (3) at completion of the project.



- 78 -                           Annex 4
35.          Coverage of data collection: Key indicator will be calculated at the
province-level. Data should be collected for the whole country. Alternatively, the 15
project provinces can be compared to a representative sample of 15 provinces which are
not having improvements in the health infrastructure financed by the World Bank or Asian
Development Bank.
36.          Disaggregation: Calculation for major ethnic minority groups.
37.          Frequency of Data Collection: Three studies are required: at baseline,
mid-term, and at completion of the project.
Total Abortion Rate (TAR).
38.         Definition: The TAR measures the number of abortions a woman would
have in her lifetime if current levels of abortion persist. This rate is a sum of current age-
specific abortion rates, which may themselves be of interest if there are specific age
patterns of concern in abortion.
39.          Background: The TAR takes into account the probability of becoming
pregnant and the probability of terminating each pregnancy throughout the reproductive
life cycle. It also indicates how widely and effectively contraceptive services are used. It
may also be useful to calculate a Total MR Rate.
40.          Method of Calculation:
49              45-49
TARi,t = ZASARi., = 5 * YASAR i,
i=15             i=15-]9
Where,
ASARj,t: the number of abortions in time t to 1000 women of a particular age i, i.e.
age specific abortion rates for women aged i in time t;
ASARi,j =    Total no.of abortions to women of age group i in time t x 1000
Number of women in age group i in time t
i: the five-year age groups of women of reproductive age (15-19 to 45-49).
t: a given year.
41.          Data source and data collection:  To be collected in three project
surveys: (1) baseline survey, (2) mid-term review, and (3) final evaluation survey.



- 79 -                            Annex 4
42.           Coverage of data collection: The whole country.
43.           Disaggregation:  Key indicator will be based on national-level data.
However, the following should also be calculated: (1) province-level TARs, (2) urban/rural
TARs, and (3) TARs for major ethnic minority groups.
44.           Frequency of Data Collection:   Three data collection efforts are
required: at baseline, mid-term, and at completion of the project.
Utilization of CHC facilities for antenatal care services.
45.          Definition: Percentage of pregnant women attended at least once for
pregnancy at the CHC-level.
46.           Background:  Prenatal care services contribute substantially to lower
infant mortality and can effectively alleviate chronic conditions of pregnancy (anaemia,
hypertensive disorders, infections).
47.          Method of Calculation:
No. of pregnant women who were seen at a CHC
% of pregnant in past 5 years for reasons related to their pregnancies * 100
women attended = Total # of pregnant women in past five years
Simplified to,
No. of pregnant women who were seen at a CHC I
% of pregnant in past 5 years for reasons related to their pregnancies * 100
women attended = Total number of births in past 5 years + (25%
of total number of births in past five years)
48.           Data source and data collection: The data for this indicator is to be
collected in three project surveys: (1) baseline survey, (2) mid-term review survey, and (3)
final evaluation survey. The indicator measures pregnancy-related utilization of prenatal
care in the past five years. The long time interval allows the estimation of statistically
significant estimates based on a large number of pregnancy cases. It is also useful to
evaluate whether women who were pregnant in the last year utilized the services,
particularly if quality improvements had been implemented in the past year, but the results
should be interpreted with caution because of the smaller sample sizes.
49.           The coverage of data collection: The whole country.



- 80 -                           Annex 4
50.          Disaggregation: The key indicator will report province-level data, with
reporting to the national level.  However, the following indicators should also be
calculated: (a) urban/rural (b) by age group of the mother (c) by parity of the mother and
(d) by major ethnic minority group.
51.          Frequency of data collection: Three data collection efforts are required:
at baseline, at mid-term, and at completion of the project.
C.    INFANT AND CHILDHOOD INDICATORS:
Infant Mortality Rate (IMR).
52.          Definition: The number of deaths of infants under I year of age per 1,000
live births in a given year.
53.          Background: The IMR, a well-defined demographic measure, is a good
indicator of the general health status of a given area or population.
54.          Method of calculation:
IMR =    No. of deaths to infants under age one in a given year * 1000
Total number of live births in the same year
55.          Data source and collection: The data are to be collected in three project
surveys: (1) baseline survey, (2) mid-term review survey, and (3) final evaluation survey.
56.          Coverage of data collection: The whole country.
57.          Disaggregation: National- and province-level IMRs are required. The
following should also be calculated: (1) urban/rural IMRs, (2) IMRs by sex, and, (3)
IMRs by major ethnic minority group.
58.          Frequency of data collection: Three data collection efforts are required,
(I) at baseline, (2) at mid-term, and (3) at the completion of the project.
Proportion of one-year-olds who were fully immunized as infants.
59.          Definition: Proportion of one-year-olds were fully immunized as infants.



- 81 -                             Annex 4
60.           Background:  UNICEF recommends that infants receive 8 injections
against six major causes of disease in the first year of life. This indicator measures the
proportion of infants (per 100) who are immunized before their first birthdays against:
> diphtheria, pertussis, and tetanus (3 injections);
> poliomyelitis (3 injections);
> measles (1 injection);
>tuberculosis (linjection).
61.           Method of calculation:
No. of children in a given year who are currently exactly aged 1 who
were given all 8 doses of 6 kinds of vaccine in the
% fully     previous year                                    * 100
immunized = No. of children in a given year who are currently
exact aged I
This indicator is based on one-year olds to avoid truncation bias, i.e. to catch infants after
they have had the opportunity to have received the full immunization schedule during
infancy.
62.           Data source and data collection: Special studies will need to be carried
out in order to calculate this indicator. The studies will be done at three points in time: (1)
as a baseline at the beginning of the project; (2) at mid-term; and, (3) at completion of the
project. The numerator will be based on health cards and other facility records from
facilities within province. The denominator will be an estimate of one-year-olds from a
demographic survey.
63            Coverage of data collection: The key indicator will be calculated at the
province-level. Data should be collected for the whole country.
64.           Disaggregation: By urban/rural; by sex; and by major ethnic minority
group.
65.           Frequency of Data Collection: Three special studies are required: at
baseline, mid-term, and at completion of the project.



- 82 -                           Annex 4
D.    INSTITUTIONAL CAPACITY INDICATORS:
Management information score.
66.          Definition: This indicator, the management information score, is a
measure of the access to current information on key areas of program functioning,
covering such areas as facilities and equipment, personnel, commodities and logistics,
finance, and service statistics
67.          Background: A measure of institutional capital.
68.          Method of calculation: The management information score is defined as
a single number, from 0 to 13, which indicates the availability of the following 13 items of
information in the central program office. All must be complete and no more than one
year old to score an affirmative point.
Management Information Score
1.    Infrastructure/Inventory
-     Number, type and location of all service delivery points
-     Equipment Inventory, specify location and condition
-     Vehicle inventory, specify location and condition
2.    Human Resources/Training
-     Number of staff by current assignment
-     Number and location of Vacant Positions
-     List of scheduled training courses and number of trainees by type
3.    Procurement
-     Quantity of each commodity procured
-     Quantity of each commodity disbursed to lower levels
-     Inventory levels for each commodity at distribution point
4.    Administrative
-     Funds budgeted by major budget line item
-     Expenditures by major budget item by district
5.    Service Delivery
-     Number of family planning and health visits by District
-     Number of new family planning acceptors by method and District



- 83 -                           Annex 4
This score implies that information of each type contributes to a more effective program.
Note that all of this information does not have to be present for the score to be calculated.
Most programs will be lacking at least some types of information, but even if a program
lacked every one of these types, it could still be assigned a score, namely zero.
69.                Data source and collection: The list of 13 items should be
available in the central office of the National Committee for Population and Family
Planning (NCPFP), based on information collected from provinces and lower levels.
70.          Coverage of data collection: Management information score required at
national-level.
71.          Disaggregation: None required, but it would be useful for lower-level
officials (province-level down to service delivery points) to have access to the same set of
data for their respective units to assist in the management of programs.
72.          Frequency of data collection:   At a minimum, the management
information score should be estimated at baseline, mid-term, and at the completion of the
project. An annual compilation of the score, coupled with a plan to improve data
management for the areas that are deemed weak, would be ideal.

PACKAGE OF INPUTS FOR UPGRADING OF
PROVINCIAL PHC SYSTEMS
Introduction
1.             One of the objectives of the project is to strengthen the delivery of family
planning/reproductive health/primary health through basic health facilities. The grassroots
health system in Viet Nam consists of the commune health center (CHC) which is the first
point of contact between the community and the health force; and the district health center
which acts as a first referral facility for the CHC. In addition, provincial MCH/FP centers
play a significant role in the training and supervision of the lower levels. The project,
therefore, provides inputs to all three levels in an effort to strengthen service delivery.
Commune Level
Civil Works
2.             The commune health facilities can basically be divided into two categories -
(a) out-patient facilities and (b) in-patient facilities.  Under the project, the options
available for facility strengthening are:
Categories of Facilities     No intervention         Renovation            New Construction

- 87 -                            Annex 5
3            The area which will be renovated in the CHCs for out-patient services
would be 70sq.m and the area of CHCs which would be renovated with in-patient services
would be 95sq.m. When considering new construction, the area of the CHC would be
kept to a minimum (initial estimates were about 70sq.m) without compromising the
delivery of services. This implies that when a CHC would need in-patient services, all
efforts would be made to ensure that health service delivery norms are maintained and
additional area would be provided if necessary. A total of 1616 CHCs would be
renovated/constructed in the 15 provinces under the project.
4.           In addition to the commune health centers, the project would also be
supporting the construction of health posts at the commune level on a pilot basis, in the
high mountainous and island areas of the country (which are currently not being reached
by the health system). These health posts will consist of a room with examination capacity
and area for a health worker to reside on the premises. The estimated area is about
27sq.m and the number of such health posts would be 440.
Furniture, Basic Medical Equipment, Essential Drugs and Medical Supplies
5.           All CHCs in the 15 project provinces (3190) would receive a package of
furniture, basic medical equipment and essential drugs and medical supplies which would
support the family planning and family health service delivery functions of the health
center. The actual quantities would be based on the results of the infrastructure survey of
existing furniture, equipment and drugs and medical supplies in the CHCs. This would be
further fine-tuned at the provincial level, based on any changes (supplies from Government
or other donors) that may occur in the interim period.

District Level
Civil Works
6.             The role of DHCs as a first-referral facility for providing basic health care
to a community clearly rmandates investments in specific areas of the DHCs which are
primarily used in its function as a support facility for CHCs. All DHCs in the 15 provinces
will be eligible for renovation. The proposed renovation includes out-patient area for the
provision of family planning, maternal and related reproductive health services; in-patient
area for the provision of family planning, maternal and related reproductive health
services; operation theater for obstetric and surgical services; central sterile supplies



- 92 -                          Annex 5
department; and storage facilities for drugs and equipment. The total number of DHCs
which will be renovated under the project is 184.
Furniture, Medical Equipment, Essential Drugs and Medical Supplies
7           All DHCs in the 15 project provinces (184) would receive a package of
furniture, medical/surgical/obstetric equipment and essential drugs and medical supplies
which would support the family planning and family health service delivery functions of
the health center. The actual quantities would be based on the results of the infrastructure
survey of existing furniture, equipment and drugs in the DHCs. This would be further
fine-tuned at the provincial level, based on any changes (supplies from Government or
other donors) that may occur in the interim period. The package of inputs would also
include equipment and drugs and medical supplies that would be invested in the district
mobile units which provide technical and managerial supervision for the CHCs.
Provincial Level
8.          Though the provincial level is a higher level facility which is not directly
involved in the delivery of FP/MCWprimary health services, the project has included
inputs for the 15 provincial MCH/FP centers. This is because of the important role that
MCH/FP centers play in providing training support for MCH/FP activities. Also, many of
these provincial MCH/FP centers support the technical activities of the DHCs by sending
provincial mobile units on a periodic basis to the district level. Given these important
functions of the MCH/FP centers in supporting grassroots and first-referral MCH/FP
activities, it was deemed necessary to provide a package of inputs to the MCH/FP centers
which would complement the inputs at the district and commune levels.
9.           The civil works, fumiture, equipment and drugs and medical supplies that
is to be provided at the MCH/FP centers are quite similar to the inputs at the district level.
These inputs would ensure adequate capacity at the MCH/FP centers to discharge its
functions as a training and supervisory support unit for the lower level facilities.

Implementation Of Civil Works
Pre-Contract Stage
10.           The implementation of the works was discussed with the MOH Deparment
of Medical Equipment and Construction (DMEC) and officials at the Provincial
Department of Construction (DOC).  The pre-contract procedures are confirmed as
follows:
(a)    (a)    NCPFP/PMU  will be responsible for initiating, monitoring and
ensuring correct implementation procedures at each stage. The PMU
issues guidelines, bid instructions and the model drawings to the Provincial
PMU, who must then implement the civil works accordingly.
(b)    (b)    The Provincial PMU selects local consultants, using procedures
acceptable to the World Bank and they in turn do their own detailed
survey/inspection of the existing facilities and prepare design proposals,
based on the PMU models, for NCPFP/PMU approval on receipt of which:



- 96 -                          Annex 5
(i)    A Provincial Bidding Commnittee is formed, representing the PMU,
MOH DOC, MOF, and specialists necessary with the Chairman from
the PCPFP service;
(ii)   The Provincial PMU initiates the preparation of the bidding documents,
to include - working drawings, bills of quantities, specifications and
contract requirements which must again be approved by NCPFP/PMU.
(iii)   Tenders/bids are then invited by public advertisements in National
newspapers and television in accordance with WB guidelines
Contract Packages
11.          Contractors are registered in Viet Nam according to their resources and
ability. Accordingly, facility proposals will be combined into contract packages by
District, to encourage the larger contractors to compete. This is usually more cost
effective and quicker in construction.
(a)   Contractors are to be post qualified. For bid invitation and documents are
issued on payment of a deposit. An adequate tender period must be allowed to
ensure fair and open tendering.
(b)   On their return, the bids are examined and the lowest evaluated and responsive
bid is recommended. The results are submitted to the NCPFP/PMU for approval
(World Bank approval is necessary above a threshold of US$200,000) and on receipt,
the contract is awarded.
(c)   The Contract is signed between the PCPFP/PM`U (side A) and the successful
contractor (side B)
Contract Supervision
12.          Contract Supervision is the responsibility of the Provincial Engineer, DOC
whose representative visits the site daily to ensure proper progress, implementation and
material tests to specification and reports monthly to the PMU Contruction Manager. The
consultant, who prepared the designs and bidding documents also visits the site regularly
to ensure compliance with the design. The provincial PMU monitors the work and reports
to the NCPFP/PMU. A weekly report signed jointly by the DOC supervisor and the
contractor is sent to the Provincial PMU who will normally report monthly to the
NCPFP/PMU:



- 97 -                           Annex 5
(a)   stage payments are agreed e.g. on completion of Foundations, walls, roof, etc
and
(b)   when a stage is reached and a payment is due:
(i)    A meeting is arranged to confirm and certify payment at which the
following are represented - Side A, User, DOC, Supervisor and
Contractor;
(ii)    On completion of the works, the Committee meets to certify
satisfactory completion and works are handed over to the User;
(iii)   A Maintenance period of 12 months is included in the Contract Articles
during which a retention is held pending successful contract
completion. For this project, the recommended retention rate is 5%.
Project Monitoring
13.          In order to fulfil its role of monitoring the civil works component, the
NCPFP/PMU will require a Construction Manager with a staff of three - an architect and
two construction engineers, one of which should have specific experience in construction
valuation. In addition, one architect/construction engineer should be employed at the
Provincial PMU to assist the supervision at District level.. These staff can be employed on
contract for the duration of the civil works implementation.
Civil Works Documentation
14.          Incomplete filing and records can considerably delay reimbursements
during implementation so the mission wishes to stress that all procurement of consultants,
contractors and materials should be clearly demonstrated to be open, fair and in full
compliance with World Bank Guidelines. Accordingly, files must be up to date and
complete with all letters, meeting minutes, records and approvals to be typed, authorised,
dated, stamped where necessary and signed. A copy of the following procurement
documentation, etc. should be available on file for reimbursement clearance and particular
attention is drawn to the correct sequence of NCPFP/PMU approvals necessary

Maintenance And Repair Strategy
15.         Buildings are seen in terms of capital cost only, whereas a building has a
lifespan - if unmaintained perhaps 25/30 years - if well maintained perhaps 60/75 years.
The graph in Annex 4 shows maximum theoretical deterioration at minus 70% and when
applied to the building life cycle, the total actual building costs are as follows:
Unmaintained building over two generations (60 yrs) - Initial construction cost + one



- 99 -                          Annex 5
70% replacement i.e. 10 million + 70% 10 million = 17 million - Cost of same building
properly maintained for same period - Initial construction cost + 0.21% pa = 11.5
million. This represents a powerful economic arguernent for the proper preventive
maintenance of building stock.
16.         The maintenance of health/MCH/FP facilities has been the responsibility of
the Provincial People's Committee but in practice there has been no specific budget for
maintenance, so that there has been little upkeep of buildings - preventive maintenance -
for many years, other than emergency short-term repairs when absolutely necessary. A
strategy for preventive maintenance was drawn up during project preparation, including the
appointment of a senior experienced architect/engineer building as a member of the staff of
each Provincial Project Management Unit. This staff member would be responsible for the
program of upkeep and maintenance of all the health facilities, including services (water,
electricity, and siteworks), and would be provided a regular maintenance budget. The annual
budget for maintenance of civil works would be equivalent to one percent of the cumulative
value of investment in civil works under the project, and the annual budget for maintenance of
equipment would be equivalent to two percent of the cumulative value of equipment purchased
under the project. The actual maintenance work would be contracted out.
CLINICAL TRAINING SUB-COMPONENT
17.          Support to clinical training under the proposed Population and Family
Health Project was finalised by the NCPFP, in consultation with the MCHIFP Department
of the Ministry of Health, with additional discussions with the Science and Training
Department of the Ministry of Health, the School of Public Health and the Institute for the
Protection of Mothers and Newborn.
Objectives of the Clinical Training Component
18.         The focus of clinical training will be on the grassroots and local level staff,
ensuring that they have the knowledge and the skills to provide high quality infonnation
and services to potential and actual family planning clients as well as to integrate provision
of family health with family planning services. Upgrading training will be given to clinical
staff to improve their specialized knowledge and implementation capabilities. Training
activities will be monitored, evaluated and updated to ensure their continuing relevance to
programme needs.



- 100 -                          Annex 5
19.          The specific objectives are:
(a)   to improve the quality of FP/family health service delivery and to offer
a wider range of contraceptive choice to clients; and
(b)   t:o improve the effectiveness and efficiency of MCH/FP training
interventions at the field level
20.          7 main areas of support are proposed for clinical training under the
Population and Family Health Project:
1)    Retraining of CHC staff in MCH/FP skills;
2)    Retraining of District-level supervisors of CHC staff,
3)    Competency upgrading in specific FP and reproductive health interventions;
4)    Managernent of training at the provincial level;
5)    Specialist training for doctors in new FP methods;
6)    Regular updates for MCH/FP service leaders;
7)    Special MCH/FP training program for CHC staff in remote and mountainous
areas.
A summary of the training program is given in the following table.
Coordination Between the National Health Support Project and the Population and
Family Health Project
21.       Both the National Health Support Project and the Population and Family
Health Project are intended to include support for the retraining of CHC staff in basic
MCH/FP knowledge and skills within their respective provinces and for the training of
district-level supervisors of CHC services. Common training packages are planned to be
used for this training in the 15 provinces covered by the National Health Support Project
and in the 15 provinces covered by the Population and Family Health Project. The
development costs for the MCH/FP retraining program have been included under the
Population and Family H[ealth Project, whilst those for the Supervisory Training package

Special MCH/FP training programs for CHC Staff in remote and mountainous areas:
22.         There iis a severe shortage of health staff working in the remote
mountainous areas of the country.  In these areas, a significant proportion of the
population are from minority groups with low levels of basic education. These low levels
preclude candidates from the minority groups from qualifying for entry to the Secondary
Medical Schools for formal health training. CHCs in these areas, if staffed at all, may have
a staff member seconded from the DHC, often not from the region at all and not
conversant with the local language. To improve basic health services in these areas, there is
the need both to develop a strategy for increasing the number of health workers drawn from
the minority groups, as well as to upgrade the skills of those staff already in service. Details are
given in Annex 15.



-105-                             Annex 6
OUTLINE OF  4lc STRATEGY AND INPUTS UNDER THE PROJECT
A.    Goal
I .         The goal of IEC is to support the program's service delivery and to create
long term demand for population and family health services. While social and cultural
conditions may require many issues to be addressed, IEC will remain focused on a limited
set of priority objectives based on the requirements of service delivery.
B.    Objectives
2.           By the end of the project period, the IEC component will seek to achieve
the following major objectives for the project (secondary objectives are not included here
and may differ from province to province):
In plains/'delta areas, 90 percent of eligible couples should:
(i)   know about at least 3 modem contraceptive methods (from among
ills, IUDs, injectibles, implants, condoms and sterilization);
(ii)  be favorably inclined toward using a modem method; and
(iii)  should have tried at least one modem method.
In other areas, this proportion should be at least 60 percent.
3.          In plains/delta areas, 90 percent of women of reproductive age should
know how to prevent and recognize reproductive tract infections (RTI) and, should the
need arise, know where to seek treatment. In other areas this proportion should be at
least 60 percent.
4.          In plains/deltas areas, 90 percent of pregnant women should know the
benefits of obtaining antenatal care, should have sought such care at least twice during
their pregnancy, and should be aware of the steps to be taken in case of an obstetric
emergency.
5.          There will also be some secondary objectives for the IEC component.
These are secondary because changes being sought will take place over a long period of
time or because other agencies are playing the lead role.  Such objectives include
promoting the small family norm among youth in secondary and vocational school
systems, educating the young about safe sex supporting the promotion of immunization
and good nutritional practices, as well as other MCH activities.



- 106 -                                      Annex 6
C.      Strategy
6.               The development of IEC strategy is based on defining target groups relevant for each
objective, determination of wvhat has to be communicated to each group, development of messages and the
choice of media to deliver the messages to the groups being targeted. Target group definition will rely on
KAPB survey (including media habits) to be conducted at the start of the project and a multimedia
strategy will be chosen on the basis of media data obtained from the survey. Pending such data, key
elements of the IEC strategy under the project are presented in the matrix below.
7.              Description of institutional capacity strengthening required for effective implementation
is described in a later section.

D.    Implementatioin: Message and Materials Development
8.           The matrix presents only the broad features of the IEC  strategy.
Implementation programs will need to be drawn up for each objective-target group-
message combination that sharpens the features for greater impact. For example, in
targeting women with one child, women who are breast feeding the child would be
addressed differently compared to women who have stopped breast feeding. To the
former, the message concerning pills would advocate the progestogen-only pill, while to
the latter the low-dose combined pill would be promoted. Again, messages for women in
minority groups would be different, as also for women who are in remote areas and
therefore less likely to be literate. In some urban areas such as Ho Chi Minh city, there
may be local campaigns to alert teenagers about the need for safe sex.
9.           The next step would be to create and produce the materials. This requires
answering questions about what the target groups know, think and feel about the topic
(KAPB data is useful here for segmentation, plus informal observation or small qualitative
studies to really understand why people are holding the beliefs or practising the behavior
that KAPB is showing), what symbols, language, phrases and other culture-related
elements can be used in communication to establish closer links with the target group, and
what opportunities or limitations are imposed by the media that will be carrying the
message.
E.    Implementation: Field Activities
10.          IEC activities in the field -- at the grass roots level -- are vital. Large
sections of the population are not reached by mass media. Even when they are, grass-
roots activities generate some excitement and stimulate discussion of the topic which has
already been seen or heard on TV or radio. The impersonal nature of mass media,
therefore, is replaced by a local, topical event and word-of-mouth channels.
I1.          This component provides for field activities through collaborators' home
visits, wall paintings in communes, traditional drama for remote areas and special events
tied in with local radio and TV. For example, as part of a "campaign approach" a district
may ask all collaborators to launch an awareness drive for pills during a 6-week period,
follow this by having wall signs painted in communes over the following four weeks and
climax the campaign by having the district mobile teams carry a rented TV and video
cassette player to show films and spots on pills.
12.          An important requirement for this approach is that there should be close
coordination between national IEC planning and provincial / district field activities. This
will require an annual IEC plan which describes the combination of messages, target



- 108 -                               Annex 6
groups and media on which attention will be focused in specific parts of the country
during specific periods of the year.
13.           To enable close coordination between national, provincial and district
levels, the  project supports  training  and  planning  meetings.    Supervisory  and
implementation capability in the field is also enhanced by the project providing for
mobility, rental of TVs and VCRs, painting of wall signs, folk theater for remote areas and
special IEC events.
F.    Implementation: Contracted Assistance
14.           External technical assistance would be contracted for the following aspects
of implementation:
a. training national and provincial staff:  international experts coming in the early
stages;
b. material development and production:    local IEC / advertising agency with
international affiliation being retained from
the public or private sector in Vietnam on an
annual contract;
c. special field events and activities:    folk drama groups, field promotion agencies
from the provinces.
15.           While provision has been made for short term overseas training for lIEC
staff, it is essential that training take place within the country as much as possible. There
are two reasons for this: first, the quality of training will benefit from trainers being
exposed to the realities of the country and second, a much larger number will be trained
and a sizable core group of IEC staff can be formed. This training is scheduled in the early
stages of the project so that benefits can flow during the project period.
16.           Assistance for material development and production will be provided by
contracting an agency from the public or private sector. Capacity for this purpose has
increased dramatically in the country in the last two years and is rapidly moving toward
high standards because of several partnerships that have been signed between Vietnamese
companies and international advertising agencies. The vital role that such an agency can
play is in increasing the impact of the materials by making them more interesting and
relevant to the target group. For the sake of continuity in thinking and accumulation of
experience, it would be desirable to contract an agency for an initial period of two years
and then renew based on performance.



- 109 -                             Annex 6
17.          Groups specializing in rural media will be contracted for field campaigns.
For example, if wall signs have to be painted in 5 districts in a province, it would be easier
to contract the Youth Union in that province, send them a design created by the private
sector agency contracted at the national level and let them paint it with modifications for
local relevance.
G.    EEC Training
18.          Training of IEC under the project will have two objectives. First, the capacity
to manage the component at the national level will be enhanced. Second, implementation
capacity will be strengthened at the provincial, district and commune levels. The underlying
premise to the training strategy is that, except at the commune level, IEC staff should be able to
develop and execute IEC plans and strategies to obtain end results, and that the conduct of EEC
activities should be left to commune level workers (such as through interpersonal
communication) or to specialists (such as for material development and production).
19.   EEC management capacity at the national level will be enhanced through inputs along
three channels:
(a)   contracting of international IEC experts to work with IEC  Department
managers in a "hands-on" relationship to develop and implement the JEC
strategy, as well as to develop the curriculum and materials for provincial,
district and commune level training (based on the training needs assessment).
Six man-months of such assistance has been provided during the first three
years of the project;
(b)    contracting of an IEC / advertising agency from the public or private sector in
Viet Nam to assist with materials development and production on a long-term
basis (initial contract of two years, subject to renewal based on performance);
(c)    short-term overseas training for selected national and provincial staff expected
to contribute to future capacity. Provision has been made for twelve persons
to obtain such training in the third and fourth years of the project.
20.   Training of provincial IEC staff will take place in the second year of the project. By
that time a core level of knowledge and skills will have been established at the national level by
the international experts and the first group of overseas trained staff having retumed. It is
envisaged that this training of provincial staff be carried out in two weeks by the Department's
national managers with the international experts being present as observers and resource
persons. It is important that the scheduling be timed in this manner because this is the last year
of intemational assistance provided by the project for this purpose.



- 110 -                             Annex 6
21.          District and commune staff will be trained in the second and third years. It is
expected that one persont from each district will be trained by the provincial IEC staff with
national support by way of curriculum, materials and instructors, if necessary. Each group of
10 district staff will be trained at the provincial headquarters over a two week period. This
period will also be utilized to conduct a field campaign near the provincial town so that
classroom discussions can reflect on practical experience shared by the group.
22           Commune level staff training will have a strong applications bias. Provincial
IEC and Training staff will conduct a 7-day program for groups of 20 workers and complete
the province's IEC training of 200 workers during 10 weeks spread over the second half of the
third year.
23.          Refresher training of provincial and district staff is planned in the sixth year, the
duration being 7 days for each group. Collaborator training has been described in a separate
section.
H.    Social Marketing
24.           Social marketing is based on the principle that concepts and techniques
normally used for selling commercial products can also be applied to promote socially desirable
goods and services. Typically, the target for socially marketed products are the poorer sections
of society and, therefore, the price set for such products is based on subsidy rather than full
cost or market value.
25.          Although social marketing in Viet Nam has been introduced recently, it has
demonstrated that the promotion of branded contraceptives in an aggressive commercial
manner is capable of delivering impressive results. This component aims to accelerate social
marketing activity in the following ways:
encouraging the promotion of pills, particularly to lower income urban women;
*     encouraging the availability of pills at pharmacies; and
*     encouraging the promotion of pills in rural area
26.          According to the 1994 ICDS, approximately 2 percent of married women in
the country currently use the pill. Given this low base of users, as a first step it is proposed that
women in urban areas be targeted for increased pill usage. Specifically, under the project the
following expansion strategy will be initiated:



- 111 -                            Annex 6
(a)    Bids will be invited from  social marketing agencies with demonstrated
experience to launch a new brand of low priced pills (VND 500 per cycle) in
five cities: Hanoi, Ho Chi Minh City, Da Nang, Hai Phong, and Hue.
(b)   Pills will be supplied at no cost and the social marketing agency will be
expected to provide the brand name, packaging, distribution to pharmacies and
advertising and promotion support. The agency will be given complete
autonomy to make decisions on strategy and implementation within agreed
budgets.
(c)    The agency will also be expected to recruit, train and deploy promoters (they
must be women) to slums and low income areas in these five cities with the
task of oonverting non-users of modem methods to the pill, where conversion
will be defined as the user's agreeing to visit a doctor, obtain a prescription and
start purchasing pills from the nearest pharmacy (when regulations permit, pills
can be delivered to the users home by the agency's delivery system).
(d)   Promoters will be paid a fixed monthly salary and allowance based on
continued usage by the clients they have converted. This allowance will be
structured so that it will be possible for the promoter to earn an additional 50
percent of salary per month with reasonable effort.
(e)    The social marketing agency will be reimbursed for all packaging, distribution,
promotion and advertising costs after adjusting for the revenue earned from
sales of pill. In addition, a service fee will be paid to the agency to cover part
of its administrative costs.
(f)    The arrangement will be subject to regular monitoring by Project Management
of the accuracy and profile of client data provided by the promoters.
(g)   The initial contract will be for two years and will be renewed subject to
performance; alternatively, new bids will be invited after two years.
(h)    Annual expenses for this sub component would include:
Recruitment and training, 20 promoters
Pharmacists' orientation
Salary, 20 promoters
Allowance, 20 promoters at 50 percent of salary
Delivery costs,
Packaging, advertising, pharmacy promotion



- 112 -                              Annex 6
Supervision fee for administration
Monitoring by Project Management
(i)    The terms of reference and the selection of the social marketing agency will be
decided vvith the help of the international experts contracted for [EC training.
27.           Since substantial support will be provided for advertising of the socially
marketed brand of pills, an effort will be made to obtain distribution of this brand in pharmacies
located in places other than the 5 cities allotted to the social marketing agency. It is proposed
to secure such distribution in the following way:
(a)    Bids will be invited from  organizations with distribution systems already
delivering products to pharmacies in provincial and district towns (other than
the 5 being covered by the social marketing agency). It is expected that during
1996-97 the number of such organizations will increase considerably.
(b)    The bid will require the organization to deliver pills to pharmacies on a regular
basis with proper attention to stock handling and shelf life, train pharmacists in
the dispensing of this product, conduct promotional activities aimed at
motivating pharmacists as may be planned from time to time and provide
regular reports on the sales through each outlet.
(c)    The project will provide pills to the organization at a price such that normal
commissions are eamed by the distributors without the price to the final
consumer exceeding VDG 500 per cycle. Since sales of pills in small towns are
likely to be small, a service fee may have to be provided to attract organizations
to this proposal. In addition, for supporting the national family planning
program, the organization can be given non-financial reward by way of public
recognition.



-113-                        Annex7
PROJ1ECTIONS OF CONTRACEPTIVE USE AND COSTS
Part L. Calculation of National Contraceptive Commodity Requirements 1997-2002
1.          This annex describes the underlying assumptions that have been utilized to
calculate the needed quantities of contraceptive supplies for the years 1997-2002. The
calculations were based on contraceptive prevalence and method mix projections that
conform to NCPFP goals for the year 2002. Specific details follow.
2.           Assumed Prices.
(a)   Contraceptive pills: US$0.30 per cycle. Annual number of cycles required
per woman= 13.
(b)    Multiload IUDs from China: US$0.75 per unit. Assumed life of RJD: 4.0
years.
(c)   Norplant: US$25.00 per unit. Assumed life of Norplant: 4.5 years.
(d)   Injectables: US$1.00 per dose. Four doses per user per year. (Purchase
4.5 doses per woman).
3.           CMW  of Reproductive Age. An estimate of the number of currently
married women of reproductive age (15-49) was provided for 1989 by the census of Viet
Nam. The size of the CMW population was projected annually to year 2002 through
assumption of a declining growth rate. The annual growth rate (1989-1990) was assumed
to be 2.6%, falling to 2.0% in 2001-2002.
4.           Contraceptive Prevalence Rate. Minimal change in the contraceptive
prevalence rate (CPR) is projected between 1994 and 2002, but the importance of modern
methods relative to traditional ones is projected to increase substantially. The CPR will
only increase from 65.0%/o to 66.0% whereas the CPR for modern methods will increase
from 43.8% to 56.1%. Only a minimal reduction in non-users is assumed (from 35.0% to
34%).
5.           Overall Method Mix. Based on the 1994 Inter-Censal Demographic
Survey, of current users of family planning, 58% use long-term methods (IUD, female and
male sterilization, and Norplant), 10% use short-term methods (pills, condoms, injectables,
diaphragm/foam/ jelly), while 30% use typically less-effective traditional methods (periodic
abstinence, withdrawal, and others). The method mix is projected to alter such that the
mix in 2002 will be 60)% long-term, 25% short-term, and 15% traditional. This is
consistent with an increase in the CPR for modern methods from 43.8% to 56.1% over the
same time period.



-114-                              Annex 7
6.              Long-Term  Methods. IUDs will continue to be the most prevalent form
of long-term family planning in Viet Nam, but their share among long-term methods will
decline slightly, from 89% in 1994 to 86% in 2002. New multiload IUDs will be supplied
to an estimated 20%  of IUD users. The proportion of long-term users with Norplant will
increase from  0%  to 2%  between 1994 to 2002. Full costs are assumed in the costing
tables. Female and male sterilization is projected to increase slightly, from 11% to 12% of
current users of long-term family planning services. The project will not incur additional
costs as a result of this change.
7.             Short-Term  Methods. Condom-use is currently the most prevalent form
of short-term method use in Viet Nam with 63% of the short-term users, following by
birth-control pills (33%), injectables (3%), and diaphragm/injectables (1%).  Increased
reliance on injectables and pills is assumed such that the method mix in 2002 will be: birth
control pills (36%), injectables (24%), condoms (39%), and diaphragm (1%). The project
will finance both injectalbles and oral contraceptives; condoms and diaphragms will not be
supplied.
8.             Detailed analysis for each of the methods is provided in the following tables
Contraceptive Requirements And Pricing

LOGISTICS ARRANGEMENTS FOR PROJECT COMMODITIES
1.           The Ministry of Health will provide logistics support for the project, and
for the National Health Support Project. This will include support for procurement,
warehousing and distribution of equipment, drugs and contraceptives financed under the
two projects.
2.           Separate support systems will be set up for equipment and for drugs and
contraceptives, since thieir requirements are different. Equipment is a one-time purchase
but needs installation and periodic preventive maintenance, while drugs and contraceptives
must be procured and distributed annually through the life of the projects.
3.           The existing logistics infrastructure will be utilised, since it has adequate
warehousing and manpower and has received inputs from both SIDA and UNFPA for
upgrading the facilities. MOH will use the first year of the two projects for ensuring that
the logistics system is in full working order, since the first consignments of equipment,
drugs and contraceptives will only be delivered in the second year.
4.           The role of MOH will be to develop this service organisation. NCPFP will
be involved at various stages, such as preparing technical specifications, both technical and
financial bid evaluation, and preparing distribution plans for commodities to be financed
under the project. The logistics system will be responsible for making compliance reports
to NCPFP for all support to the project, as input into the project MIS.
5.          The private sector will be involved to the extent feasible in the logistics
support system through a system of sub-contracting. It could play an important
complementary role in port-clearing and forwarding and transportation of commodities
from the central warehouse to provinces and districts.



120                            Annex 9
OUTLINE OF STRATEGY AND INPUTS FOR MIS
AND MANAGEMENT TRAINING UNDER THE PROJECT
Management Information Systems
MIS Goals within the Project
1.          The NCPFP Population and Family Health project is intended to strengthen
the overall management capacity within the Vietnam population and family health area.
This management improvement not only includes increasing the capacity of workers in the
field to better utilize information, but also increasing the range and quality of information
available for management decisions.
2.          The basic: goals of the MIS effort within the project will be to:
(a)   Strengthen managers' capacity for using information for planning,
prioritizing and monitoring population and family health activities through
training and availability of computer resources
(b)   Increase the quality of population and family health indicator data
(c)   Expand the use of information for management to include sources of
information not currently utilized for decisions (i.e training, [EC, service
delivery, etc.)
(d)   Broaden the range of information sources for information gathering and
validation (i.e use of surveys to correct population data)
(e)   Strengthen use of computers for data gathering and analysis at all levels for
decision support.
3.          The activities under the current project will significantly increase the
overall use of computerized information for management. Additionally, by focusing on a
decision support systenm (DSS) model, the project will increase the overall capacity to
effectively utilize informnation at all levels of the PFP/MCH system.
Overview of Population and Family Health MIS Project Activities
4.          The project will address the overall mix of activities necessary to increase
the management capacity of the population and family health system to deliver services,
and to strengthen the planning and prioritization capacity of its managers to better target
the population and family health resources available.



121                                        Annex 9
5.               It is recognized that the needs for information systems for the project goes
beyond the gathering of statistical information, which shows only the output/impact of
project activities. The MIS approach of the project will be to focus on the information
necessary for management of the inputs to the project and the improvement of the use of
information for management decisions.
6.             The activities in the project are directed at strengthening the overall capacity
for management of population and family health activities. Additionally, these activities
will increase the NC:PFP's overall capacity to implement National changes to the
population and family health system.

the MOH

Scope and Phasing of Decision Support Activities
7.           The MIS component of the Project will be a national effort to cover all
Provinces. Given the large number of Provinces, fifty-three, and the variety of training
which is necessary for new management skills, it is not possible to implement the MIS
system all at one time. The Project will phase the introduction of systems and training so
that the overall burden of implementation will be reduced.
8.           Project irnplementation of computer systems will be phased separately from
the management training and introduction of new reporting systems, such as training
information. This separation is feasible since many of the new systems will not be initially
computerized, and because the impact of changing the paper management systems for
planning will be significant from the beginning.
General Methodology for Implementing MIS
9.    The management information systems under the project will cover the range of
systems for processing all population and family health activities in Vietnam. Three major
areas are to be addressecd during the life of the project to support MIS efforts:
(a)   Management Skills training
(b)   Revision of information systems for population and family health
management data
(c)    Computer systems for increased processing/analysis of data
The MIS effort in the project will be implemented by the MIS team directly under the
Minister, NCPFP, in coordination with the Project Management Unit, Planning and
Finance Department, and the Centre for Research and Information. Given the scope of
activities to be implemented under the project it is not possible for all components to be
implemented simultaneously throughout the country. Hence, a phased plan has been
prepared which will allovv a constant improvement in the management capacity throughout
the life of the project.
10.          The project office will be responsible for reviewing quarterly the MIS plans
for the project, and for approving all modifications to current information systems, and for
approving management training plans. Contracts for software development, and staff for
programming will be approved through the project office as necessary.
11.          The first two years of the project will be targeted at developing new MIS
systems, both computerized and paper, and developing and implementing training



123                             Annex 9
programs for management skills. These systems will be tested in the first five provinces
prior to implementation on a national basis.
12.   The implementaltion of each of the systems will be determined based on the
necessary decisions to be made at each level. Training, in use of general management, and
specific systems, will be designed in conjunction with the MIS project efforts, and the
training department.

Management Training
25.          A substantial escalation is proposed under the project with the purpose of
improving curriculum and materials, as well as conducting the training in a programatic
manner so that all levels azre systematically covered. The following are the features of the
Management Training program:

development of a central team of six trainers who will serve as the core
full-time trainers for the program. They will be drawn based on their
specialization and training record from the leading academic institutions in

training activities have: commenced with the new curriculum. Refresher and subsequent
training will be based on these data.
