ADMINISTRATIVE ARRANGEMENTS FOR THE
SERVICE DELIVERY INITIATIVES COMPONENT
Institutional Arrangements
1.          A Service Delivery Initiatives Committee will be established within the
Project Management Unit (PMU) of the NCPFP to manage the Service Delivery
Initiatives component. Standing committee members will include the Director of the
PMU, the PMU Task Manager, and the PMU Family Planning Service Officer. A
designated representative of MOH will also join the committee for management of any
component activities concerning the development of private-sector involvement in
FP/reproductive health services.
2.          The tasks of the committee will be to solicit, develop, review and
administer implementation of proposals for financing under the component. It will
seek assistance in these tasks as appropriate from the PMU's Local Experts Task Force,
other local or international consultants, and other staff of the PMU, other departments
of NCPFP and of MOH.
Procedures for Processing Proposals
3.          The comiponent is intended to provide flexible and unstructured
financing. Although some activities have already been designed and costed in detail or
in outline during project preparation, it will also be open to innovative ideas and
proposals from all quarters.
4.          Thus, proposals for financing under the component can be originated and
developed in a variety of ways. They may be originated and prepared by the PMU
itself or by other departrnents of the NCPFP. They may be designed and submitted to
the committee by any external agency, including MOH. They may be solicited by the
committee from external agencies at large, with or without specific terms of reference
to guide proposal preparaltion.
5.          The committee will be responsible for development and preparation of
the final detailed design for the proposals that were decided upon during project
preparation and are outlined in Annex 11. In addition, it will, as appropriate, solicit
other proposals, prepare detailed terms of reference for desired topics, review proposals
that are submitted and decide on whether they should be componented. In all cases, the
proposals would specify ihe chosen procedure for implementing it and, if the proposal



-129 -                          Annex 10
involves pilots or testing of new approaches or service delivery systems, the
arrangements for evaluation of their success.
6.          Once proposals have been finalised and approved by the committee, they
will be submitted for review to IDA, or to ADB in the case of proposals relating to
development of the private/NGO sector, together with the procedure chosen for
implementing it. Upon approval by IDA or ADB, the committee will then manage
implementation of the proposal.
7.          Implementation again may be handled in a variety of ways, depending
on circumstances, at the discretion of the committee. The PMU may itself directly
implement the proposal, or arrange for its implementation through another Government
agency, such as MOH, MOLISA or GSO. This would be the case with the testing of
the health post and upgraded collaborator model systems, for example, where MOH
would implement the test and possibly MOLISA or GSO evaluate its success.
Alternatively, the committee may use a standard IDA or ADB bidding process to solicit
bids for the implementation of proposals, using the approved detailed proposals as
terms of reference for bidders. This might for example be the procedure chosen for
implementing some of the outlined studies, such as those on IUD and MR/abortion
determinants and service quality. If the latter process is followed, it would be subject
to the IDA or ADB procurement guidelines in force for all other procurement under the
project and would be implemented in the same way. The committee would then be
responsible for bid evaluation and final selection of consultants.
8.          The committee will be responsible for monitoring and supervision of
proposal implementation in all cases. When implementation is complete, and the
results and findings of surveys or evaluations are available, the committee will review
the overall success and implications of the proposal topic and report on it to IDA, ADB
and the Project Steering Committee.



-130-                            Annex 11
INDICATIVE LIST OF TOPICS TO BE FINANCED UNDER THE
SERVICE DELIVERY MODEL INITIATIVES COMPONENT
Model Strengthened Collaborator System
1.          The current collaborator system is the principal channel of community-
based outreach for tlhe promotion of family planning.  However it is in need of
strengthening. At present the system consists of a very large number of part-time workers
who are paid very small monthly allowances of 15,000 dongs per month (plus incentive
payments for recruitment of acceptors of selected methods) and focus almost entirely on
the promotion of family planning.
2.           A new model system will be tested under component 5, and extended if
successful throughout the 15 project provinces under Component 1; it will feature much
reduced numbers of collaborators, who will become full-time workers paid higher
allowances of 120,000 dongs per month and cover promotion of family health as well as
family planning. An imlportant FP function would be the community-based distribution for
temporary methods such as pills (resupply) and condoms. The new name of Family Health
Worker will be given to these workers. The outline of the new model system is as follows:
3.           Forty five communes will be selected within the 15 project provinces for
testing the new model system. Twenty two will be located in remote mountainous areas or
isolated islands; eighteen in the delta regions; and five in midland or mountainous areas
falling between the tvvo previous extremes. A community needs assessment/feasibility
study will be carried out in each study zone, in order to ensure full community
participation, determine local cultural attitudes and values, and assess the existing health
infrastructure and referral system.
4.          In the remote mountainous/island areas, 7 workers would be selected per
commune; in the midland/mountainous areas, 5 workers; and in the delta plains, 3
workers, making up 233 family health workers altogether in the test. Selection criteria
would be determined locally on the basis of the community needs assessment and in
discussion with the communities. Normally, the most effective workers of this type are
those closest in social status, culture, age and experience; they should also be
contraceptors themselves. Other important criteria might include sex, literacy or
educational level, marital and childbearing status, health training, and appropriate
commitment and skills in interpersonal relations.
5.           The tasks of the family health workers would be fine-tuned on the basis of
the community needs assessment. However a likely minimum package would include:



- 131 -                           Annex 11
(a)    motivation and recruitment of new  FP acceptors and support,
counselling and reassurance to continuing users; all supported by
adequate quantities of appropriate printed IEC materials which can be left
with clients, as well as by mass media messages
(b)   distribution of contraceptive supplies: using a simple checklist, workers
will initiate and resupply clients for short-term temporary methods such as
the pill and condoms; the low-dose combined pill would be supplied to
women who are breastfeeding or contraindicated for the use of oestrogen;
adequate quantities would be provided per visit (for example, six pill
packets) in order to reduce the number of visits needed per client; workers
with sufficient medical training could also be trained to give injectables
(c)   follow-up of contraceptive defaulters: workers would regularly follow up
both their own clients and those who obtained their method elsewhere
(d)   promotion and simple services for basic primary health care: workers
could promote, follow up on and perhaps provide simple basic care for
basic PH-C interventions such as EPI, CDD, growth monitoring and
appropriate maternal care; also disease control programs for malaria, TB,
iodine deficiency diseases etc.
6.           In order to carry out these tasks on a door-to-door basis, workers will be
provided where necessary with suitable transport such as bicycles or (in very remote areas
with difficult terrain and sparse settlements) motorcycles.
7.           Training of the family health workers would be fine-tuned on the basis of
the community needs assessments and resulting selection criteria for workers. Generally
speaking, the content of training would be kept very simple and concrete, using training
techniques appropriate to adults and oriented to the development of practical skills.
Training should last for at least 2 weeks, in order to allow adequate time for practical
training. Workers would be trained to:
(a)    provide accurate information on each contraceptive method they offer
(pills, condoms, injectables), including its proper use and side effects
(b)   discuss the advantages of family planning and birth spacing and describe
accurately all available methods, their correct use and their side-effects
(c)    provide advice on how to obtain clinical methods



-132-                             Annex 11
(d)   discuss the advantages of basic PHC and disease control interventions and
programs and provide accurate information on them
The training curriculurn would be prepared by revising the latest collaborator curriculum
developed with UNFPA support, in order to simply the material and add health topics.
8.           Medical back-up for the family health workers will be provided by CHCs.
To ensure that this back-up is effective, CHC staff in the study areas will be involved in
the planning of the new system and will be trained in technical supervision of the workers.
All CHCs in the study zones will be upgraded as necessary to provide good-quality
referral and back-up services.
9.           The objectives of supervision for the family health workers will be to:
provide support and back-up when problems arise; watch for any difficulties; and link the
family health worker system effectively to the mainstream FP program and to the health
system. Supervisors will also facilitate the supplying and restocking of the family health
workers and the collection of service statistics. Supervisors must possess appropriate
knowledge and experience and be able to take a supportive and encouraging attitude to
their subordinates. Full-time local CPFPB staff with these characteristics would be
recruited and trained, and supervisory instruments such as supervision manuals and
checklists would be developed and supplied to them. they would also be provided with
motorcycles in order to ensure adequate mobility for their operations.
10.          The family health workers will keep records of their work, including a
comprehensive record of each client's social, demographic and medical history plus the
management and services provided by the family health worker, and a record of stocks
held and IEC materials distributed by the family health worker. Data on the numbers of
new acceptors recruited and continuing users visited, plus the quantities of contraceptives
distributed, will be collected, compiled and submitted to the supervisor on a monthly basis.
11.          The test of the new system will last for two years. Its performance and
impact would be monitored and evaluated through both routine service statistics and
special community surveys, using data from neighbouring areas which continued with the
current system as comparators and controls. Indicators that might be employed to assess
trends attributable to the new system include: community knowledge of short-term
methods; the community CPR; changes in the method mix; appropriate PHC and disease-
control program measures.



-133-                            Annex 11
Model Health Post System for Remote Mountainous Areas
12.         As described in Annex 15, a model health post system would be tested in
60 communes in remote mountainous areas in the 15 project provinces, and expanded
under Component 1 to all such areas in the 15 project provinces if successful. For a
commune to be eligible for coverage by the system, it must fulfill the following criteria:
(a)   the CHC must have at least 3 health staff
(b)   the CHC must be at least 4 hours walking distance from the nearest DHC
(c)   there must be strong commitment from the Provincial People's Committee
13.         A health post would be set up in one village in each commune. It would
consist of two small rooms, one of which would be a consultation room/office and the
other a bedroom. Basic furniture, medical equipment, a selection of essential drugs, and a
bicycle for household visiting would be supplied.
14.         The health post would be staffed by one of the CHC health workers, on
either a rotational basis or as a permanent assignment, according to the choice of the
workers. The health worker in residence would provide basic IEC and services for
MCH/FP, including the distribution of appropriate contraceptives and some minor curative
care. If the new family health worker system is also being implemented in the commune,
the health post might be staffed by the family health worker.
15.         The success of the health post system would be evaluated at the end of a
two-year test period of operation. Neighbouring areas that did not receive health posts
would be used as comparators. Both routine health service statistics and special
community surveys alt the beginning and end of the test period would be employed for
monitoring and evaluation. Indicators of performance and impact might include trends in a
selection of common PHC program measures, such as EPI rates and appropriate maternal
care, as well as data on community satisfaction with, and utilization of, the health posts.
Model Approaches for Mobilization of the Private/NGO Sectors in Family Planning
and Family Health
16.   These model approaches are aimed at expanding the provision of good-quality
family planning and reproductive health services by the private and NGO sector. they do
not include social marketing programs, which are funded under Component 2.



-134 -                            Annex 11
A.    Private Providers
17.          Since information on the scope, structure and performance of the private
sector in Viet Nam is so limited, the first step in designing model approaches to mobilizing
private providers will necessarily be data gathering. Two initial surveys will be undertaken.
The first will be a market research survey among doctors and possibly also other health
providers such as midwives and nurses, which will aim to determine the factors that
motivate or hinder them  in establishing or expanding a private practice in family
planning/reproductive health. The second will be a consumer survey that would examine
the attractiveness to actual and potential clients of obtaining such services through the
private rather than the public health sector.
18.          On the basis of results from  these surveys, model initiatives and
interventions would be designed for testing. Possibilities already under discussion include:
(a)    Creation of a pilot loan fund: A fund would be set up to provide credit to
doctors for the establishment or expansion of private clinics. Eligible
doctors would be those who include family planning/reproductive health
services as part of their practice. Allowable uses might include small-scale
upgrading of premises, and basic equipment and supplies needed to provide
or upgrade these services.
(b)   Provide updated training: Different mechanisms would be tested for
providing   updated  training   for  private  doctors  in  family
planning/reproductive health.
(c)    Mobilize  private  providers  to  service  remote  areas: Different
mechanisms would be tested for mobilizing private providers to bring
family planning/reproductive services to areas which are underserved.
Possibilities might include: special support schemes for midwives or other
paramedical cadres who would be interested in providing such services in
rural areas; incentive programs to attract doctors to rural areas; and special
time-sharing programs allowing private providers to utilize public health
facilities as premises for their service delivery.
Provide IEC materials: Specially adapted family planning/reproductive health EEC
materials would be designed and supplied to private providers including pharmacists.
(d)   Establish professional associations: Assistance would be provided in a
small number of provinces for the establishment of professional
associations of private providers offering family planning/reproductive



- 135 -                           Annex 1 1
health services; the purpose would be to create a forum through which the
concerns of such providers could be shared and addressed, information
could be distributed, and training seminars could be held.
(e)    Alleviate constraints to private practice: Attempts would be made to
alleviate any constraints identified as affecting the private provision of
family planning/reproductive health services, through policy papers,
seminars and other activities.
B.    Non-Governmental Organizations
19.          Information on the activities and performance of the two existing NGOs in
the family planning/reproductive health field is also extremely limited. It is therefore
difficult even to develop an outline of possible ways to mobilize the potentially very
valuable contribution of this source. Emphasis should probably be on the provision of low-
cost but good-quality services to particularly poor and underserved populations. However
the first priority would be to amass adequate information on the NGO sector. Thus the
NGO strategy would consist of two stages; first to obtain a data base for planning and
second to develop initiatives, as follows:
(a)    Evaluate existing NGOs: Active NGOs would be studied to evaluate their
performance, their impact to date and the reasons why clients prefer them.
(b)   Expand NGO activities to underserved areas: On the basis of the evaluation
results, initiatives would be identified, designed and tested for expanding
NGO services, probably with a focus on underserved groups.
Pilot Test of Charging for Contraceptives
20.          This pilot test will examine the impact  and feasibility of introducing
charges for contraceptives provided under public family planning services. The aim of the
charges would not be to achieve full cost recovery, but rather to increase the perceived
value of family planning services and hence raise the level and effectiveness of
contraception use. The pilot would measure the impact of different levels and structures of
charging systems on the utilization of public family planning services. It will be carried out
during the first three years of the project and its results evaluated as part of the project
mid-term review.



-136-                            Annex 11
Model Approaches for Safe Pregnancy and Safe Motherhood
21.         Levels of maternal mortality appear to be anomalously high in Viet Nam,
given overall levels of life expectancy and health services. Important facets of matemal
health care, such as provision of iron and vitamin A supplements during pregnancy and
adequate delivery facilities for both normal and complicated deliveries, need improvement
throughout the country, but particularly in remote and underserved areas. These model
approaches would seek to improve the well-being of women during pregnancy and
delivery in a variety of ways. An initial list of models includes:
(a)    Reduction of Anaemia and Vitamin A Deficiency: Both anaemia and
vitamin A deficiency are very common among Vietnamese women of
reproductive age, and still more during pregnancy. They may lead to
various obstetric complications and compromise the health of infants born
to deficient women. Evaluative studies will be carried out to identify the
most successful model strategies for reducing these deficiencies. Such
strategies might include, in addition to provision of supplements as a part
of prenatal care, dietary supplementation with foods rich in iron and
vitamin A, treatment for underlying or resulting diseases (such as malaria
and general infections), and counselling and IEC on good nutritional
practices.
(b)   Safe Home Deliveries: Over forty percent of deliveries in Viet Nam still
take place at home, and 27 percent occur without assistance from any
health staff. Nearly two thirds of communes are without a secondary
midwife. Thus approaches need to be designed for improving home
delivery practices. An initial survey would be conducted to identify and
investigate any harmful practices, and to examine reasons for delivering at
home and/or without trained medical staff in attendance. On the basis of
the study results, model approaches would be designed and tested for
improving current home delivery practices and increasing utilization of
health facilities and staff for deliveries.
Management of High-Risk Pregnancy and Complications: About 15 percent of
deliveries world-wide suffer from obstetric complications (haemorrhage, sepsis, eclampsia,
obstructed labour) that are potentially life-threatening and require specialized management
and facilities. Various model approaches would be designed and tested for improving
access to and utilization of appropriate facilities. These might include establishment of
"maternity waiting houses" near District or Provincial hospitals for women known to be at
high-risk of complications, programs to promote private practice in isolated rural areas (as



-137-                            Annex 1l
above), and programs to establish or improve systems for emergency referral and transport
in obstetric emergencies.
Model Approaches for IUD, Pregnancy Termination and RTI Services
22.          Levels of IUD use and pregnancy termination are very high in Viet Nam;
one third of married women are fitted with an IUD and the average number of
terminations per woman is estimated at 2.5. Likewise, RTIs are known to be extremely
prevalent among Vietnamese women, though the precise rate has never been measured.
All are interconnected, in that RTIs may complicate both IUD insertion and pregnancy
termination, and also result from either procedure if sterile conditions are not achieved;
moreover, IUDs are often in practice the only modern contraceptive method available to
Vietnamese women and if they are unacceptable for any reason (including RTIs),
termination may be the only practical alternative or may become necessary through the
failure of the rhythm method or withdrawal. All require improvement in service quality or
access to effective treatment, and the prevalence rates of RTIs and termination, at least,
need to be reduced. Model approaches to achieve these aims in all three areas will be
developed on the basis of improved data.
A.    IUD Program Impact Evaluation
23.          The IUI) has for decades been and still is the dominant modem
contraceptive method in Viet Nam. Many clinical studies on this method have been
conducted, but no evalLiation of its program impact. Therefore as a first step, a study of
national scope would be conducted to assess the acceptability of the IUD among
Vietnamese women, the common problems encountered by acceptors and users, the
training needs of service providers, and how well equipped facilities are to handle
problems and complications. This study would have four parts:
(a)   Review of IUD Patient Records: a national representative sample of IUD
client records will be reviewed from all levels of facility where lUDs are
provided. Data will be extracted on: demographic characteristics of
acceptors; selection criteria used for IUD users; the client follow-up
schedule., complaints and problems reported by clients; how these were
managed; level and patterns of method failure; and main reasons for
discontinuation.
(b)   Interviews with Current and Past IUD Users: a representative sample of
past and current IUD users whose records were reviewed in part (1) of the
survey will be interviewed. Current users will be questioned on: what and



-138-                           Annex 11
who motivated them to accept an IUD; what was their experience of
insertion; what were provider attitudes and care; whether or not they
received accurate information and effective support; how often they have
been followed up; whether they have any complaints or complications; how
satisfied they are with the method; and whether they would recommend a
friend or relative to use the IUD. Past users will be questioned on their
experience with the IUD: in particular whether they had complaints or
complications, were happy or not with the method, why they discontinued,
whether they switched to another method, which method they changed to,
and whether they would recommend a friend or relative to use an IUD.
(c)   Questionnaires Administered to HUD  Providers: Service providers
would be surveyed to examine: their knowledge of and attitudes to the
IUD and other contraceptive methods; their training in insertion and
removal; their current level of skill in counselling, client selection for IUD
use, insertion and removal techniques. In addition, focus-group discussions
with groups of providers would be conducted to elicit in depth their
attitudes and beliefs about the IUD in an informal setting.
(d)   Focus-Group Discussions with Women in General: Wide-ranging focus-
group discussions would be carried out with unselected groups of women
in the community, with the aim of eliciting: general attitudes to the IUD;
whether they are correctly informed about the IUD; what are the common
myths and misconceptions about the IUD; and what are the common myths
and misconceptions about other methods.
24.         On the basis of results from this study, model approaches, protocols and
interventions would be developed and tested to improve IUD acceptability and service
quality: for example, through standardization of state-of-the-art IUD insertion and
removal techniques, follow-up care and RTI treatment; fine-tuning of selection and
screening criteria for IUD use; corresponding refresher training and improved pre-service
training; better supervision of providers; provision of appropriate IEC materials and
programs, etc.
B.    Evaluation of Pregnancy Termination Services
25.           Pregnancy termination has been made widely available in Viet Nam with
the intention of providing safe back-up services in the event of contraceptive failure.
Current levels of menstrual regulation and abortion are however much above expected
back-up needs in a situation of widespread access to modern contraception and need to be



-139 -                           Annex 11
reduced. Again, a number of clinical studies on this subject have been carried out, but
there is still a need for an assessment of the causes and program implications of the current
high termination rates. Therefore a national study will be conducted using both
institutional-level and community-level data. Its aims will be to establish the level of
pregnancy termination, to determine the reasons for seeking pregnancy termination, and
the consequences of current levels and patterns of termination. The study will be
composed of three parts, as follows:
(a)   Study of Service Records: From a representative sample of termination
client records, the following data will be collected: demographic
characteristics of women seeking termination; reasons for opting for
termination; contraceptive history. A sample of clients would also be
intervievved on: their experience of the procedure; the quality of services
received, including pre-termination and post-termination counselling; their
attitudes to termination and other birth control methods; and their
subsequent reproductive and contraceptive histories.
(b)   Study of Service Providers: A sample of providers will be surveyed on
aspects of service quality at all levels of facility where terminations are
done, including questions on: what kind of pre-termination and post-
termination counselling they offer, with particular attention to minimizing
or preventing repeat abortions through appropriate contraception; their
training and skill levels in termination techniques; schedules of follow-up
care; treatment of RTIs; and main complications and problems
encountered. In addition, focus-group discussions with groups of providers
would be conducted to elicit in depth their attitudes and beliefs about
termination in an informal setting.
(c)   Study of Community Attitudes and Practices: The community study
will include both a sample survey of married women and wide-ranging
focus-group discussions with unselected groups of community women. The
sample survey would aim to collect reliable data on the incidence and
patterns of pregnancy termination, including service location and cost of
the procedure to the client (this latter to be supplemented also by data from
MOH on the cost of providing the service). The focus-group discussions
would explore community perceptions of and attitudes to pregnancy
termination.
26.          On the basis of results from this study, model approaches, protocols and
interventions would be developed and tested to reduce levels of pregnancy terminati n
and improve service quality: for example, in addition to improved access to a wide ra ge



- 140 -                          Annex I I
of modem contraceptive methods, through standardization of state-of-the-art MR. and
abortion techniques, follow-up care and RTI treatment; fine-tuning of selection and
screening criteria for MRs; improved pre-termination and post-termination counselling
with emphasis on ensuring protection from a further unwanted pregnancy through
immediate contraceptive counselling and supply; corresponding refresher training and
improved pre-service training; better supervision of providers; provision of appropriate
[EC materials and programs, etc.
C.    Evaluation of RTI Services
27.         Although RTIs are universally acknowledged to be a major reproductive
health problem in Viet Nam, there is a severe lack of data on RTI incidence, prevalence,
patterns and causes. The first step in any attempt to reduce RTI levels must therefore be
data collection. A three-part study will be conducted in selected districts, representing a
variety of living conditions and cultures, to establish the exact extent and nature of the
RTI problem. Again, both institutional and community-level data will be collected, as
follows:
(a)   Study of Service Records: Client records from a sample of facilities at all
levels of care will be examined to gain a preliminary idea of the extent of
the problenm, as well as associated, pre-disposing and causal factors (with
particular attention to associations with the IUD and pregnancy
termination), and treatment given. A sample of clients would be surveyed
to provide corresponding information.
(b)   Study of Service Providers: A sample of providers will be surveyed on
aspects of service quality, including questions on: management protocols
followed for RTI diagnosis and treatment, with attention to management of
concurrent IUD or termination care; their training and skill levels in
diagnosis and treatment; schedules of follow-up care; and main
complications and problems encountered. In addition, focus-group
discussions with groups of providers would be conducted to elicit in depth
their attitudes and beliefs about RTIs in an informal setting.
(c)   Study of Community Attitudes and Practices: The community study
will include both a sample survey of married women and wide-ranging
focus-group discussions with unselected groups of community women. The
sample survey would aim to collect reliable data on the incidence and
patterns of RTIs, including associations with rUD and termination and their
experience of treatment at health facilities. The focus-group discussions



-141-                            Annex 11
would explore community perceptions of and knowledge about the
determinants of RTIs and the problems associated with them.
28.          On the basis of results from this study, model approaches, protocols and
interventions would be developed and tested to reduce the prevalence of RTIs and
improve service quality: for example, through development of preventive strategies;
standardization of protocols for state-of-the-art RTI diagnosis and treatment, and follow-
up care, including how to handle IUD use and termination; corresponding refresher
training and improved pre-service training; better supervision of providers; provision of
appropriate IEC materials and programs, etc.
29.          Strategies and Models for Minority Populations: The health post model
described above is one approach to the problem of improving access to good-quality and
appropriate family health services for disadvantaged ethnic minorities. However an overall
set of strategies and approaches needs to be developed. Other model interventions will be
developed and tested on the basis of sound base data and informed participation by
minority communities. Special participative baseline studies will be carried out, and a
variety of models and strategies will be piloted and evaluated. These might include special
incentive programs to attract private and NGO providers to minority areas and special
training programs for traditional midwives and healers. Overall evaluative studies will also
be carried out at the end of the project.

PROJECT MANAGEMENT ARRANGEMENTS
A.    Background
1.          NCPFP set up a national level Project Steering Committee (PSC) and
Project Unit (PU) in January 1994. In addition, provincial level project units were also
established in the five first-phase provinces. The PSC and PU worked closely with the
Bank missions in project preparation and design, thus initiating the project management
structure at an early stage.
B.    Organization
2.    The central project management organization will have three layers:
*  a Ministerial level--Project Steering Committee (PSC);
* a Director level-- Executive Comrnittee (EC); and
* a working level--Project Management Unit (PMU).
3.           The SC will be chaired by the Chairman of the NCPFP, and its members
will consist of the Vice-Ministers of the Ministry of Planning and Investment (WPI),
Ministry of Finance (MOF), State Bank of Vietnam (SBV) and Ministry of Health
(MOH). The EC will be composed of the eight department directors of NCPFP. In
addition, a Director will be assigned as a standing member of EC and will serve as full-
time Project Director.
Steering Committee
Chairman (NCPFP)
4 Members (MPI, SBV, MOF,
MOH)
Executive Committee
Chairman (NCPFP)
8 Directors of Line Departments of NCPFP
1 Standing Members as Project Director
Project Management Unit       Expert Task Force



-152 -                          Annex 13
4.           Under the Director, the PMU will have 15 staff, who will be assigned to
nine sections (the eighlt functional sections plus general adminstration). In addition, the
PMU will hire local consultants as needed to form task forces to assist project
management in special areas. These task forces will work directly under the leadership of
the central Project Director. The key staff positions identified in the NCPFP Project
Management Unit are those of Director, Task Manager, Lead Civil Works Officer, Lead
Procurement Officer And Lead Disbursement Officer. In the 15 project provinces,
provincial PUs will be established and which will consist of a Manager and three staff.

C.    Functions & Operation
5.           The Steering Committee will be the highest level of decision-making
during project implementation. It will meet regularly every three months to discuss
various aspects of project supervision, and to make decisions on relevant policies and
strategies for proper project implementation. The main function of the SC will be to
foster coordination am.ong NCPFP and the other relevant agencies namely, MPI, MOF,
SBV, and MOH.
6.           The Executive Committee will play an important role in mobilizing the
policy and technical resources within NCPFP by involving the line departments in project
implementation. Each department would be assigned one component/sub-component, for
example, the IEC Department will be responsible for the dEC component, and the Family
Planning Coordination Department will be responsible forcthe  family health service
delivery component. Each Department will then be responsible for ensuring that the
project fits into the overall plan to meet national objectives and resolve any conflicts that
may arise between project and program goals and objectives. The EC will therefore meet
every month to review implementation during the course of the project.
7.           The horizontal coordination among all the line departments will be the
responsibility of the Project Director and the Project Management Unit under his
leadership. The PMU will prepare the annual working plan and progress report for review



-153 -                         Annex 13
by the SC and EC, prior to submission to the World Bank. The PMU will also conduct
the procurement of equipment and drugs, and maintain project accounts. The central
PMU will provide implementation guidance to provincial project management units.



-154-                                Annex 14
PROJECT MONITORING, EVALUATION AND SUPERVISION
1.           Projects in the social sectors are often plagued by a lack of reliable baseline data as
well as weak monitoring and evaluation systems. Bank projects, in particular, have traditionally
focused on project inputs and accountability issues, rather than on project outputs and impact
indicators in terms of reductions in fertility, improvements in health status for individuals, or the
performance of the health services. This makes it impossible to assess the impact of the proposed
interventions on the population.
Current Status of Population and Demographic Statistics in Viet Nam
2.           The Government of Viet Nam has become increasingly aware of the need for
regular and reliable population and demographic statistics for formulating and monitoring its
population policies and for other development planning purposes. Two population and housing
censuses (in 1979 and in 1989) have been conducted since reunification in 1975. Another census
is planned for 1999. These censuses contain information on childbearing, childhood and adult
mortality, and other key indicators that we have selected for measuring the progress of the
project. Disaggregation to the province level and for specific sub-groups (ethnic minorities,
married women of reproductive age, etc.) of the population is possible for some of the indicators.
3.           The General Statistics Office (GSO) also prepares a time series of national and
sub-national population and demographic estimates for use by the Ministry of Planning and
Investment and other agencies. However, the range and detail of the estimates it currently
prepares is very limited, and is insufficient to meet the needs of the Population and Family Health
Project.
4.           Civil registration of births and deaths, which has its legal origins in a government
directive in 1961, takes place at the commune level. It is widely recognized, however, that there
is very severe under-registration of births and deaths, particularly in rural areas, as well as
frequently delayed registration. Registrars are unsure about their registration duties, and are
hampered by lack of training, lack of materials, and lack of incentive to perform these duties.
Large Survey Efforts
5.           The Viet Nam Demographic and Health Survey (VNDHS) was carried out in
1988, the first such assessment of demographic and health status since the implementation of
population and family planning programs in the early 1 960s. The nationally-representative sample
survey was carried out by the National Committee for Population and Family Planning (NCPFP),
with financial support from UNFPA. A total of 4,807 households and 4,172 eligible women aged
15 to 49 years old were interviewed to collect data on fertility, family planning, and a few
indicators of child and maternal health.



- 155 -                             Annex 14
6.           A second demographic survey was carried out more recently in 1994 by the
General Statistical Office (GSO), with financial support from UNFPA. The survey, the Vietnam
1994 Intercensal Demographic Survey, was conducted in order to update information on human
reproduction, infant and child mortality, contraceptive use, and maternal and child care. More
than 13,000 households and 10,490 ever-married women between the ages of 15 and 50 were
interviewed. Estimates are representative of the population at three levels: national, urban/rural,
and for seven geographical regions.
Status of Family Planning and Family Health Service Statistics
7.           Family planning and facility-related health statistics are also routinely collected at
various levels of the Vietnamese system. The organization and control of data collection and
compilation are highly decentralized and have their roots in some 10,000 communes, the lowest
level of administrative units Two streams of data processing occur in the system, which can be
simplified as follows. Firs1, the commune family-planning collaborator reports information on
acceptors, method mix, and other factors to the Commune Population and Family Planning Board
(CPFPB), where it is summarized and then sent in turn to the District (DCPFP), to the Province
(PCPFP), and to the National level (NCPFP). Second, the commune family-planning collaborator
reports the same information to the Commune Health Center (CHC), where it is combined with
other information on CHC ifacility usage (for family planning as well as other health concerns).
Data from mobile team efforts are also incorporated. These reports are compiled and sent in turn
to the district health services (DHS), provincial health bureau (PHB), and National (MOH) levels.
At the appropriate levels, infbrmation on services provided at polyclinics, provincial hospitals, and
other facilities is added in.
8.           A number of problems are evident with this system. The foundation of the data
collection system is the collaborator or CHC-level worker. Collaborators are generally unpaid
volunteers, with low or no levels of basic training and frequently only low levels of basic skills.
The CHC-level workers, while of higher qualification than the collaborators, are often lacking the
basic skills that are required for data collection and reporting.
9.           System inefficiencies are evident throughout the country. Data are almost entirely
manually compiled and processed at all stages, increasing the probability of introducing bias into
the statistics. Furthermore, since workers in both the NCPFP and the MOH stream are compiling
family-planning information, there is a duplication of effort. Given these constraints and in the
absence of a thorough evaluation of the service statistics, the data are of unknown but
questionable quality.
Evaluation of the Viet Nam Population and Family Health Project
10.         Given this background, the Viet Nam Population and Farnily Health Project will
implement four basic strategies to monitor and evaluate the Project.



-156 -                               Annex 14
Namely,
(a)    Conduct of three major surveys (baseline, mid-term review, and final evaluation)
for the purposes of project evaluation, particularly to measure the project impact
indicators which have been selected for the project.
(b)   Conduct of three major studies (baseline, mid-term review, and final evaluation)
also for the purposes of project evaluation. These studies will measure indicators
of project impact for components of family planning and family health that cannot
be readily measured using a population-based survey sample. These studies will
mostly require evaluation of particular components based on facility records.
(c)    Conduct of special surveys and studies to evaluate the special needs and
appropriate strategies for meeting those needs for the major ethnic minority
populations.
(d)   Management Information System (MIS) efforts. This will include strengthening
the use of computers for data gathering and analysis at national and provincial
levels, strengthening of managers' capacity for using information for planning,
prioritizing and monitoring population and family planning activities through
training availability of necessary resources at levels.
11.          The data sources which will be utilized to calculate a full matrix of input,
intermediate, and impact indicators have been selected for the Viet Nam Population and Family
Health Project for national, provincial, and lower levels. Within this matrix, ten key indicators
have been selected to measure project impact and achievement. In general, most of the key
indicators can be calculated from special surveys that have been planned at three points in the
project cycle. A few will require conduct of specialized studies of routinely-collected information
that is available from CHC's and from family planning workers. Strengthening of the MIS efforts
will facilitate this task.
Project Surveys
12.          Three major surveys will be implemented as part of the Viet Nam Population and
Family Health Project at various times in the project cycle. The first, to be conducted before
project activities begin, will provide baseline data. A mid-term review will occur towards the
middle of the project. A final evaluation of the project will be conducted at completion of the
project. The World Bank requires conduct of these three surveys as part of its agreement with the
Government of Viet Nam. The National Committee for Population and Family Planning (or an
agent thereof) will be responsible for the overall implementation of the survey, including sample
design, preparation of survey instruments, testing, training, field work, data processing, and



-157 -                              Annex 14
analysis. A written report on the major findings for each of the key indicators is required for each
of the surveys.
13.         All three surveys will be national-level, population-based sample surveys. Over-
sampling of the ethnic minoriity populations in the 15 Project provinces will be carried out. These
surveys will assess the family planning and family health status of the population, with particular
emphasis on the ten key indicators that have been selected for the Project.
14.         In addition to collecting data in order to calculate the key indicators, special
supplements to the three data collection efforts should address particular components of the
project. For instance, a module on knowledge, attitudes, and practice of contraception would
help to monitor and evaluate the IEC component of the project. A module on service delivery
would address whether contraceptives that are procured by the project ultimately reach women in
the villages.

Special Studies
15.         A few of the key indicators cannot be based on a population-based survey, and will
require the conduct of special studies. Two of the studies will require analysis of facility records
in order to calculate the key indicators: (1) met need for life-saving obstetric care, and, (2)
proportion of one-year-olds who were fully immunized as infants. For these two studies, sections
emphasizing the characteristlics of the major ethnic minority populations will be included. A third
indicator, the management information score, will investigate whether 18 items of data which is
relevant for management is available at the central offices of the National Committee for
Population and Family Planning.
16.         The special studies will be conducted at three time points during the project cycle:
baseline, mid-term, and finaLl evaluation. The National Committee for Population and Family
Planning (or an agent thereof) will be responsible for the overall implementation of the studies,
including study design, preparation of reporting instruments, training, field work, data compilation
and processing, and analysis. A written report on the major findings for each of the key indicators
is required for each of the studies.
Special Surveys and Studiies to Address the Population and Family Health Needs of the
Major Ethnic Minority Polpulations
17.         With the exception of the Han, all of the major ethnic groups in Viet Nam are
known to be demographically disadvantaged relative to the Kinh, with higher rates of both fertility
and infant mortality. Little information exists, however, to understand the underlying reasons for
their disadvantage.
18.         The ten major ethnic minority populations will be over-sampled in the survey
instruments and special studies that have already been described to calculate the key indicators.
Additional participatory studies, to be conducted among each of the ten groups, will address their
special population and family health needs. These assessments will be conducted using focus-
group methodology among various sub-groups of the ethnic minority populations, including
women of reproductive age, men of reproductive age, adolescents, grand-mothers, traditional
birth attendants or others found to be important to decision-making processes in family health.
19.         Topics to be addressed will include, but are not limited to, the following issues:
(a)   Beneficiary-needs assessments: These special studies will allow members of the
ethnic minorilties to communicate what population and family health needs they
have that could reasonably be expected to be fulfilled during the project.



-160 -                              Annex 14
(b)   Knowledge,, attitudes, and practice of family planning:   Contradictory
impressions exist regarding the acceptability of family planning services in Viet
Nam. The iCommittee for Ethnic Minorities and Mountainous Areas (CEMMA)
suggests that the issue of family planning can be sensitive to certain ethnic groups
since they do not want to adopt any practice that may jeopardize their cultural
identity and preservation as a distinct ethnic group.  In contrast, preliminary
studies suggest that non-use may simply reflect lack of accessibility and poor
method mix.
(c)    Care during pregnancy and delivery: In order to gain a fuller understanding of
pregnancy- and delivery-care, special focus group studies should be conducted
with pregnant women, new mothers, traditional birth attendants, and relevant
family members. Questions should solicit information on the dietary practices of
pregnant ancl breastfeeding women, supplementation (if any) with vitamins, work
habits, traditional practices and beliefs, practices in the event of an obstetric
emergency, transportation options, etc.
(d)   Family healith care:  Suggested topics for this component include traditional
practices for the prevention and treatment of infectious diseases (including
malaria), imrnunization practices, the use of traditional healers, and the reasons for
non-use of CHCs.
Management Information System
20.          An information system will be developed to support strategic planning activities at
national and provincial levels, operational activities at commune level and a coordinating function
at the district level. In order to achieve this, the project would support hardware and software at
national and provincial levels, training in appropriate MIS skills for staff at commune, district,
provincial, and national levels, and foreign and local technical assistance for setting up the MIS.
Given that the development of the MIS system is at an early stage, calculation of the key
indicators will not be dependent upon the system.
Supervision Reporting and Arrangements
Supervision Reports
21.          The Project Management Unit in NCPFP will submit six-monthly progress reports
to IDA, beginning six months after the date of effectiveness and every six months thereafter. An
indicative outline of the structure and contents of the report format follows; the full detailed
format, including dummy tables, will be prepared by the PMU prior to the first IDA supervision



- 161 -                            Annex 14
mission and agreed upon on a trial basis. The format will then be re-evaluated during the next
supervision mission, when the first report has been submitted, and finalized.
Population and Family Health Project
Six-Monthly Implementatiion Progress Report
For June - December 1996
(a)   Executive Summary: This section would provide a brief summary overview of
project progress within the previous six months, including:
-     summary of project activities
-     financial status
-     project successes/achievements
-     problems encountered and suggested action
-     major issues requiring to be addressed
(b)   Project Activities During Previous Six Months: This section would provide a
description of the major activities carried out during the previous six months, with
accompanying tables showing progress relative to targets/plans for the period and
to   total   project  targets/plans   (e.g.   civil   works   completed,
equipment/drugs/contraceptives procured, training activities completed, IEC
activities undertaken, models/studies underway etc.). Results of any surveys,
studies, or evaluative activities which became available during the period should be
briefly described.
Any major achievements or successes during the previous six months should be
highlighted. Any problems or delays should be described and explained, together
with proposed remedial action.
(c)   Planned Activities for Next Six Months:  This section would provide a
description cf activities planned to be completed during the following six months,
with accompanying tables showing the relationship of the planned activities to
project targets/plans.
(d)   Budget and Financial Status: This section would lay out progress in project
expenditure (by component, sub-component and activity) during the previous six
months relative to amounts budgeted
for that period and the overall project cost tables. Explanations for over-spending
or under-spending should be provided. Progress on claims, reimbursement and



- 162-                             Annex 14
audits would also be described, with comments provided on any delays or
problems encountered and suggested remedial action.
(e)   Project Management Status: This section would describe problems or issues
relating to project management, if any. together with suggested remedial action.
(f)    Key Indicators: This section would provide available information on trends in
key indicators, relative to project goals, accompanied by appropriate comments.
(g)   Status of Covenants: This section would provide information on the status of
compliance with. project agreements, using the following table format.
(h)   Major Issues: This section would provide an analytical description of all major
issues in project implementation arising in any of the aspects listed above. It would
also lay out for discussion the suggested actions for addressing them on the part of
both Government and IDA together with its co-financers.

reports. Every effort would be made to coordinate mission timing with ADB and KfW supervision
visits, so that joint supervision would be maximised. Additional supervision missions would be
scheduled to resolve any special issues or needs.

the Task Manager

SPECLUL STRATEGIES FOR ETHNIC MINORITIES
1.          The Demography of Ethnic Minorities in Viet Nam: The major source
of data on the demography of ethnic minorities in Viet Nam is the 1989 census. Tables
A. 16.1 and A. 16.2 and Map No. IBRD 27338 summarise a few key features, including the
percentage of ethnic minorities in each province and the total population, estimated total
fertility rate and estimated infant mortality rate for the major ethnic groups in 1989.

2.          There is huge variation by province in the proportion of ethnic minority
groups among total provincial populations. Broadly speaking, the non-Kinh groups form
only a small minority in most lowland and delta provinces, but make up 50-80 percent of
the population in mountainous provinces. Several provinces have been subdivided since
1989, so estimation for those affected is difficult, but roughly speaking, ethnic minorities
constitute 50-80 percent of the population in three of the 15 provinces covered under
Component I of the project (Lau Cai, Lai Chau, Tuyen Quang, all in the northern
highlands), 15-30 percent in another six (Bac Thai, Dac Lac, Lam Dong, Kien Giang, Tra
Vinh, Thanh Hoa, almost all including mountainous regions), and less than four percent in
the remaining six (Hai Hung, Hai Phong, Nam Ha, Thua Thien Hue, Dong Thap, Vinh
Long, all delta or coastal).
