- 172-                           Annex 15
3.           Clearly all the ethnic minority groups except the Han are demographically
disadvantaged relative to the Kinh, with higher fertility and infant mortality. The Tay and
the Muong are not too far behind in either respect, but the other groups all show TFRs of
over 5 and IMRs 20 to 70 points higher. The H'mong, the Gia-rai and the Dao stand out
as particularly disadvantaged in either or both fertility and infant mortality levels.
4.           Findings; of the Baseline Survey Exercise:  Most ethnic groups are
known from census and survey data to be poor, ill-educated and isolated. It is therefore
hardly surprising that thieir demographic profile is unfavourable. However the proximate
determinants of their fertility and mortality levels and patterns have been little studied. A
first step in the design of any special strategy is therefore a set of baseline surveys and
participative baseline studies (see Annex 14). A model exercise of this kind was conducted
as part of project preparation in Dac Lac province.
5.           The major findings of the participative studies conducted in Dac Lac
among a number of minority groups were as follows:
(a)   family planning, modem contraception and modern health care appear to be
very acceptable to minority women, couples and communities
(b)   Government and health workers are not always in touch with community
attitudes and interests in the health and family planning sectors and
communications between program staff and communities need to be
improved
(c)   there is considerable unsatisfied demand for temporary methods of
contraception in many minority communities, particularly pills and
injectables
(d)   health facilities in minority areas were often dilapidated and poorly
equipped and supplied
(e)   there is a severe deficiency of good-quality IEC materials and activities in
minority communes
(f)   there is a large reservoir of interested and motivated minority community
members who could be tapped for extension, outreach or community-based
distribution work for family planning and health
(g)   there is a need for refresher training for health and FP staff, particularly for
family planning, with emphasis on counselling skills and up-to-date
knowledge of modern contraceptive methods



-173 -                          Annex 15
(h)   there is a need to develop new and specialised approaches to the provision
of healt]h and family planning services in minority areas, with the emphasis
on recnritment and specialised training programs for minority community-
based workers.
6.          In line with the above, field observations in Lau Cai during project
preparation identified the following major problems in the delivery of primary health care
and family planning in remote mountainous areas:
(a)   long travel distances between villages and CHCs, resulting in low
utilisation of CHCs and heavy dependence on DHC mobile teams to
provide services
(b)   serious understaffling of CHCs, with many empty facilities used only as
premises for visiting DHC teams
(c)   serious shortages of minority health workers, due to low educational levels
among rninority groups
(d)   low skill levels among minority health workers, for the same reason
7.           Outline of a Special Strategy for Health and FP Service Delivery in
Remote Minority Areas: Based on the above findings, plus available information from
other sources, the outline of a set of special strategies for delivery of health and family
planning services in minority areas that are remote and mountainous, and hence isolated
and underserved at the present moment, has been developed for introduction and testing
under the project. Details will be refined on the basis of the full findings from the baseline
surveys and studies to be conducted as a first step, but a preliminary set of possible
interventions has been identified. Its principal approach is to adapt the standard Viet Nam
model of service delivery to the special needs of remote areas and minority cultures by
upgrading, strengthening and expanding outreach, access and community-level or
community-based services and promotive activities. Major features are as follows:
(a)   Adaptation of the CHC Model for Remote Mountainous Areas: Since
larger in-patient CHC facilities which can do deliveries will not be utilised
in remote mountainous areas, only small outpatient facilities will be
provided in such areas under Component I of the project (as well as under
the National Health Support Project).
(b)   Establishment of Health Posts: A model health post system would be
tested under Component 5 in 60 communes in remote mountainous areas in



-174 -                          Annex 15
the 15 project provinces, and expanded under Component I to all such
areas in the 15 project provinces if successful. For a commune to be
eligible for coverage by the system, it must fulfil the following criteria:
(i)   the CHC must have at least 3 health staff
(ii)  the CHC must be at least 4 hours walking distance from the
nearest DHC
(iii)  there must be strong commitment from the Provincial
People's Committee
A health post would be set up in one village in each commune. It would
consist of two small rooms, one of which would be a consultation
room/office and the other a bedroom. Basic furniture, medical equipment, a
selection of essential drugs, and a bicycle for household visiting would be
supplied. The health post would be staffed by one of the CHC health
workers, on either a rotational basis or as a permanent assignment,
according to the choice of the workers. The health worker in residence
would provide basic IEC and services for MCH/FP, including the
distribution of appropriate contraceptives and some minor curative care. In
the separate new collaborator (family health worker) system to be also
tested (see below) the health post could be staffed by the new family health
worker.
(c)   Establishment of Family Health Workers: Remote mountainous areas
and minority areas would participate in the new collaborator (renamed
family health worker) model system to be tested under Component 5 and
extended to cover the 15 project provinces if successful under Component
1; out of the total 45 communes selected for testing, 22 would be in remote
mountainous areas and another 5 in midland and mountainous areas that
are not considered remote. In remote areas, 7 full-time family health
workers would be recruited per commune (compared to 3 in delta
communes and 5 in the midland/mountainous areas) and each would be
given a bicycle to facilitate household visiting. If both the health post and
the family health worker systems are successful, the health posts could be
staffed by family health workers as an alternative to CHC staff.
(d)   Additional Transport for DHC Outreach: Under Component 1, DHCs
in remote mountainous areas would be provided with extra motorcycles
(and in thie Mekong delta, motor boats) in order to enhance their outreach
capabilities. The vehicles would be used by the DHC mobile teams for EPI,
FP and control of endemic diseases. The project would also finance



-175 -                          Annex 15
maintenance and operating costs for the vehicles. The impact of this
additional transport would be evaluated at the end of the project and
continued under regular health/FP budgets if judged essential to full
functioning of the DHC mobile teams.
(e)   Special Training Strategy for Remote Mountainous Areas: Because of
the lack of local minority candidates with adequate minimum educational
qualificaltions (completion of high school), health staff in these areas are at
least 80 percent Kinh. Even so, there is an acute shortage of CHC health
workers, with more than 50 percent of the approximately 500 communes in
the remcte mountainous areas of the 15 project provinces currently lacking
any resident CHC staff. Such CHCs must be staffed by a seconded DHC
worker. Skills of minority health workers, where they exist, also often need
improveiment. Thus special strategies for pre-service and in-service training
will be implemented in the 15 project provinces in order to both increase
minority worker numbers and upgrade skills of existing minority staff, as
follows:
(i)   Pre-Service Special Training: About 300 minority health workers
with below-standard educational qualifications would be recruited
and employed by the local CPFPB, and trained as "family health
workers" to serve in CHCs. They would receive a short basic on-
the-job training course of about one month at the local DCH, plus
two supplementary courses of the same length to follow later once
the workers were in service. In addition, they would receive in-
service refresher training of 2 days every 4 months at the local
DHC. Those who showed promise could later take the regular
primary midwife pre-service course at the provincial secondary
medical school and then be upgraded to the level of primary
midwife.
(ii)   In-Service Special Training: Frequent refresher training would be
provided to the approximately 500 minority workers stationed in
'CHCs in the remote mountainous areas, in order to upgrade and
maintain their skills. The training schedule would comprise four
short courses per year of about two days each at the local DHC.
(iii)  Special Training Methodologies: All the in-service and pre-
service training described above would take place at the DHC in
order to facilitate attendance. DHCs would be provided with the
necessary teaching aids. Training methodology and materials would
be adapted to lower educational levels through: an emphasis on



- 176-                          Annex 15
practical skills and demonstration models; heavy use of pictorial
materials; translation of training materials into local minority
languages; and special training of DHC trainers in the use of these
special methodologies and materials.
A reference manual adapted to this modified training approach will be
developed and provided to all minority CHC workers (including family
planning workers) in remote mountainous areas throughout the country,
except for the National Health Support Project provinces where it would
be provided under that project's financing.
(g)   Special Family Planning Approach for Minority Groups: The project
as a whole responds to the findings above through support of increased
access to and promotion of modem temporary methods such as pills and
injectables, as well as improved models of IUD. However, special
approaches will be designed and implemented to adapt family planning
services to the particular conditions, needs and preferences of minority
communities. This will be done after the results of the baseline surveys and
studies are available for guidance, and will be based on them. It is expected
that these special approaches will include:
(i)   emphasis on promotion and provision of modem  temporary
methods
(ii)   custom-designed and high-quality IEC programs and materials for
at least the largest minority groups
(iii)  particular attention to counselling, community participation and
community-based activities and programs.



- 177-                             Annex 16
PROJECT SUSTAINABILITY ANALYSIS
A.    Estimated Project Costs
1     .      Estimated base costs for the project amount to around US$110 million and
total costs to around IJS$130 million. Recurrent costs in the project budget make up
about US$14 million (11 percent) of the project budget, or an annual amount of around
US$2 million. However some of these recurrent costs are tied to specific investments or
activities limited to the project period (such as travel, supplies and communications costs
for project management and the refresher training part of the upgrading package for the 15
project provinces) and will not enter into the sustainability analysis. Conversely, some
project costs categorised as investment costs, namely drugs & medical supplies and
contraceptive commodities, will in fact require sustaining after the life of the project.
Allowances for commtnity-based outreach workers (family health workers) in the 15
project provinces, under the strengthened collaborator system to be first piloted in the
initial 2 years of the project under funding from Component 5, are not included in this
category, since the success of the pilot, and hence the need to provide these allowances,
are quite uncertain at this point.
2.           The costs that will be taken account of in this analysis are:
(a)    maintenance costs for facilities upgraded and equipment purchased
under the project, amounting to an average annual sum of
uS$900,000
(b)   annual essential drug & medical supplies, amounting to an average
annual sum of US$ 2.1 million for the 15 project provinces
(c)   logistics costs for essential drug & medical supplies, calciulated as
7.5 percent of the costs of the commodities
(d)   annual contraceptive commodity supplies, excluding condoms (see
below for estimated costs from various aspects
(e)   logistics costs for contraceptive supplies, calculated as 7.5 percent
of the costs of the commodities
3.           Of total, maintenance costs, half will be held against the national health
budget and half against the national population/FP budget, on the assumption that a large
proportion of services provided by the facilities are related to family planning, and that this
will be reflected in some manner in the annual responsibility contracts made by NCPFP



- 178-                           Annex 16
with the health system to deliver FP services. Drug & medical supplies costs, with
accompanying logistics costs, will be held against the national health budget; and
contraceptive commodity costs, with accompanying logistics costs, will be held against the
national population/FP budget.
4.          National Contraceptives  Supply: Total costs  for contraceptive
commodities are to be financed under the project for the four years 1997-2002. These
commodities exclude condoms but are otherwise projected to cover 100 percent of the
country's needs for those years. A small reserve fund for emergency needs in the last years
of the project is also included. The total including logistics costs comes to around US$25
million.
5.          Total annual national costs for contraceptive commodities, excluding
condoms, are projected to rise during the life of the project from a current level of around
US$3 million to around US$7.5 million in 2002 ( including logistics costs). This rise is
expected to occur through a combination of increasing contraceptive prevalence rates,
higher percentages of users relying on more commodity-intensive short-term methods such
as the pill and injectables., and growing numbers of women of reproductive age due to past
high population growth. The project is assumed to contribute heavily to the first two of
these three trends. Thus it would be fair -- and conservative for the purposes of this
analysis -- to consider the increase of US$4.5 million in annual contraceptive costs
(including logistics) as the recurrent cost burden generated by the project.
6.          Drug andl Medical Supplies for the 15 Project Provinces: Total costs of
US$15 million have been budgeted under the project to finance essential drugs and
medical supplies for the 1 5 project provinces. This amounts to an annual recurrent cost of
US$2.1 million which will have to be financed out of the regular health budget after the
project has closed. Addlitional logistics costs could amount to US$150,000, making a
total annual recurrent cost burden of US$2.25 million.
B.    Projected National Budgets
7.          Populatiorn/FP Budget: Domestic population/FP funding largely flows
through the national budget of the NCPFP, which in 1994 reached about 228 billion dong
or around US$23 million. Provincial budget funding amounted to about 23 billion dong, or
around 10 percent of central spending, though funding at lower levels is difficult to track.
Altogether, total Government spending on population and family planning in 1994 may
have amounted to somewhat over 251 billion dong, or US$25 million.
8.          According to NCPFP sources, the national budget in 1995 amounts to 245
billion dong (excluding spending on basic construction which is financed through another
channel). Projections for future years are as follows:



- 179-                           Annex 16
Year        Billions           Millions
of Dong           US$
1996         456               45.6
1997        467                46.7
1998        468                46.8
1999        488                48.8
2000         500               50.0
This projection indicates that after the 86 percent increase from 1995 to 1996, NCPFP's
budget would remain fairly static. It is based on the premise that spending on
population/FP should reach a level of US$0.6 by the year 2000, the goal which has been
approved by the Prime Minister. If the budget were to remain static in real terms, it might
increase to around US$58 million by the year 2002.
9.          According to MOH projections, the total national health budget for the
year 2002 will reach 12,625 billion dong, or US$1.26 billion. If a more conservative
projection is followed of an annual growth rate of only 8 percent, the 2002 health budget
would amount to 4,205 billion dong, or US$420 million. This more conservative
assumption will be adopted for this analysis.
C.    Recurrent Cost Implications
10.         Implications for the National Population/FP Budget: The annual
recurrent cost burden on the national population/FP budget generated by the project will
amount to around US$4.5 million for contraceptive commodities plus logistics costs.
Maintenance costs would add another US$ just under 450,000, making up US$5 million in
total. This would amount to about 8.5% of the projected population/FP budget for 2002,
a significant but sustainable amount.
11.         Taking a wider perspective, however, it makes sense to assess the entire
annual contraceptive coimmodity burden that would require to be financed at the end of
the project. As mentioned above, this is projected at US$7.5 million, exclulding condoms,
by 2002. Thus, contraceptive commodity and logistic costs alone would amount to
around 13 percent of the projected 2002 total budget.
12.         This scale of resource allocation for contraceptive supplies is not excessive
by the standards of family planning programs worldwide and therefore should be
sustainable by the Government of Viet Nam even if all these supplies were to be financed
by Government itself and provided free of charge through public programs. In fact, a
proportion of users already obtain their contraceptive supplies from the private sector and
the importance of bot:h the private sector and social marketing is likely to grow



-180 -                            Annex 16
substantially during the life of the project. A program of charges for public program users
is also planned to be introduced during the second half of the project. It is moreover very
likely that donor suppo:rt to contraceptive supplies would continue to be significant after
the year 2000, outside the umbrella of the project itself The sustainability of the expanded
contraceptive costs should therefore present no major problems.
13.         Implications for the National Health Budget: The annual recurrent cost
burden on the national health budget generated by the project will amount to around
US$2.7 million, made up of US$2.25 million for drugs and medical supplies, and
US$450,000 for maintenance. This would amount to 0.64 percent of a conservative
projection of the national health budget for 2002, which is a negligible amount.
14.         Incremental costs on this scale should not by themselves be difficult to
sustain. However, again it makes sense to adopt a broader perspective, and consider the
additional impact of the National Health Support Project and any further extension of
upgrading packages throughout the country. The National Health Support Project would
provide a similar upgrading package over the same period to another 15 provinces, raising
total coverage to more than half the country. However the two projects together would
still only raise incremental recurrent costs for the 30 provinces together to around US$5.4
million or less than 1.5 percent of the national health budget.
15.         Taking a still wider view, if the provincial upgrading package should
succeed in improving service quality and utilisation, Government might perhaps consider a
similar upgrading program in the country's remaining provinces. At most, however, the
additional recurrent cost burden from a similar investment program in the rest of the
country would about double that generated by the two projects. Thus annual costs of
essential drugs and maintenance of upgraded facilities and equipment for CHCs and DHCs
for the entire country would consume only around 3 percent of the national health budget.
It does not appear, therefore, that the upgrading of Viet Nam's PHC services could create
any major problem of sustainability.



-181-                           Annex 17
OUTLINE OF THE IMPLEMENTATION VOLUME
1.    The "Implementation Volume" is a follow-up document to the Staff Appraisal
Report. It is intended to serve as a detailed guide to day-to-day implementation of the
project and will be prepared in its final form by the NCPFP itself between negotiations and
project effectiveness. An "implementation volume" consists basically of a compilation of
all usefiul background maLterials put together in the course of project preparation, together
with a summary time-bound implementation plan for the entire project and detailed time-
bound annual implementation plans for at least the first year or two of activities. It is
better viewed not as a single document, but as a set of files containing all necessary
reference materials as well as a basic blueprint for implementation planning.
2.    What follows is an indicative outline of the contents of the Implementation
Volume, for use as a guide to its preparation.
The Summary Implementation Plan for the Project
3.    The core of this implementation plan would be a timetable of major project
activities, along the lines of a Gantt chart, Pert chart or Computerized Project
Management chart. For the summary plan, time need only be shown in blocks of months,
say quarters of a year, and activities in each component of the project can be summarized
in broad categories. This plan timetable should be kept permanently on the Project
Management Unit's computer system and updated continuously by management and MIS
staff. Print-outs could then be produced at regular intervals, say every six months, for
information and use by other project staff. To supplement the timetable, a text would be
added describing the major features of the plan, together with any notes needed on details
of its content.
4.    The Implementation Volume itself would contain only the first plan timetable and
text produced at the beginning of the project, which would set the format to be used. An
indicative example follows. The latest print-out would then be included in each six-
monthly progress report submitted to IDA (see Annex 14).
The Detailed Annual Implementation Plans
5.    The core of the annual implementation plan would be a set of timetables for each
year of the project, covering all activities planned under each component of the project,
again in the form of a Gaantt chart, Pert chart, Computerized Project Management chart
etc. Again, the timetables should be supplemented by as detailed a text as is necessary.
6.    For planning purposes, the timetables should be prepared at the end of each project
or fiscal year (whichever is more convenient) for rolling time-periods of two years, i.e., a
plan/timetable for the first two years should be prepared at the beginning of the project;



-182 -                           Annex 17
then at the end of the first year, the plan/timetable for the second year should be revised in
the light of the achievements of the first year and a plan/timetable for the third year added;
then at the beginning of the third year the plan/timetable for the second year should be
revised and a plan/timetable for the fourth year added; and so on.
7.    Again, these plans/timetables should be permanently kept on the PMU computer
and updated continuously by management and MIS staff.  Only the first two-year
plan/timetable should be included in the Implementation Volume for reference, and to set
the format. Print-outs should then be produced at regular intervals, say once or twice a
year, for information and use by other project staff. For six-monthly progress reports to
be submitted to IDA, a year's time-slice should be produced and included in the report,
showing the plan/timetable for the previous six months and following six months.
Suggested Background Materials
Initial Contents
The consolidated final project proposal as appraised by the MPI and cleared by the
Politburo.
Reports of background studies and technical assistance consultancies carried out as
part of project preparation.
The Staff Appraisal Report.
Detailed cost tables, as prepared by IDA.
Aide-Memoires prepared by IDA missions during preparation, appraisal and
supervision.
Building plans or model building plans for civil works under the project.
Any other supporting documentation from project preparation that would be useful
as a guide during implementation and is not included in any of the above
categories.
IDA procurement guidelines.
Materials to be added duiing Implementation
Semi-annual progress reports
Supervision aide-memoires
Annual NCPFP plans and budgets (containing details of counterpart funding
allocations).

SELECTED DOCUMENTS
AVAILABLE IN THE PROJECT FILE
AND USED IN THE PREPARATION OF THE REPORT
1     Viet Nam  Poverty Assessment and Strategy: The World Bank, Country
Operations Division, Country Department I, East Asia and Pacific Region; January, 1995.
2.    Viet Nam National Health Support Project Staff Appraisal Report: Human
Resources Operations Division, Country Department I, East Asia and Pacific Region,
World Bank; December. 1995.
3.    Situation Analysis of Women and Children in Vietnam  (Draft): UNICEF,
Hanoi, January, 1994.
4.    "Investing in Health", World Development Report, 1993: World Bank, 1993.
5.    "Abortion in Vietnam": Measurements, Puzzles and Concerns": Daniel
Goodkind in "Studies in Family Planning", Vol. 25, Number 6; November/December,
1994.
6.    Viet Nam  1988 Demographic and Health Survey: National Committee for
Population and Family Planning, Socialist Republic of Viet Nam; Hanoi, 1990.
7.    MultiRound Survey, April 1, 1993: NCPFP and General Statistical Office;
Hanoi, 1993.
8.    Effective Family Planning Programs: World Bank, 1993.
9.    "The Economic Value of Contraception": A Comparison of Fifteen Methods:
Trussell et al. in the American Journal of Public Health, Vol. 85, No. 4; April, 1995.
10. Memorandum of the President of the International Development Association
to the Executive Directors on a Country Assistance Strategy of the World Bank
Group for the Socialist: Republic of Viet Nam: Country Department 1, East Asia and
Pacific Region, August 22, 1994.
11.   Viet Nam  Population, Health and Nutrition Sector Review: World Bank,
1992.

Chacko, Summer Intern Report, World Bank, August, 1994.

Nam": Stanley Zankel; Consultancy Report for the World Bank; March, 1995.
14.   Viet Nam Population Country Report for the International Conference on
Population and Development, 1994, in Cairo: NCPFP; September, 1994.
15.   Decree of the Government on the Functions, Responsibilities, Powers,
Mechanism and Working Regulations of the National Committee for Population
and Family Planning; Government of Viet Nam; June, 1993.
